\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In United States v. Automobile Workers, 352 U. S. 567 (1957), the Court again encountered the independent expenditure ban, which had been recodified at 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7610 (1952 ed.). See 62 Stat. 723\xe2\x80\x93724. After holding only that a union television broadcast that endorsed candidates was covered by the statute, the Court \xe2\x80\x9c[r]efus[ed] to anticipate constitutional questions\xe2\x80\x9d and remanded for the trial to proceed. 352 U. S., at 591. Three Justices dissented, arguing that the Court should have reached the constitutional question and that the ban on independent expenditures was unconstitutional:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cUnder our Constitution it is We The People who are sovereign. The people have the final say. The legislators are their spokesmen. The people determine through their votes the destiny of the nation. It is therefore important\xe2\x80\x94vitally important\xe2\x80\x94that all channels of communications be open to them during every election, that no point of view be restrained or barred, and that the people have access to the views of every group in the community.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 593 (opinion of Douglas, J., joined by Warren, C.\xc2\xa0J., and Black, J.).

\n

\nThe dissent concluded that deeming a particular group \xe2\x80\x9ctoo powerful\xe2\x80\x9d was not a \xe2\x80\x9cjustificatio[n] for withholding First Amendment rights from any group\xe2\x80\x94labor or corporate.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 597. The Court did not get another opportunity to consider the constitutional question in that case; for after a remand, a jury found the defendants not guilty. See Hayward, Revisiting the Fable of Reform, 45 Harv. J. Legis. 421, 463 (2008).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Later, in Pipefitters v. United States, 407 U. S. 385, 400\xe2\x80\x93401 (1972), the Court reversed a conviction for expenditure of union funds for political speech\xe2\x80\x94again without reaching the constitutional question. The Court would not resolve that question for another four years.

\n

2

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Buckley, 424 U. S. 1, the Court addressed various challenges to the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 (FECA) as amended in 1974. These amendments created 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7608(e) (1970 ed., Supp. V), see 88 Stat. 1265, an independent expenditure ban separate from \xc2\xa7610 that applied to individuals as well as corporations and labor unions, Buckley, 424 U. S., at 23, 39, and n. 45.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Before addressing the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7608(e)\xe2\x80\x99s independent expenditure ban, Buckley first upheld \xc2\xa7608(b), FECA\xe2\x80\x99s limits on direct contributions to candidates. The Buckley Court recognized a \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important\xe2\x80\x9d governmental interest in \xe2\x80\x9cthe prevention of corruption and the appearance of corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 25; see id., at 26. This followed from the Court\xe2\x80\x99s concern that large contributions could be given \xe2\x80\x9cto secure a political quid pro quo.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Buckley Court explained that the potential for quid pro quo corruption distinguished direct contributions to candidates from independent expenditures. The Court emphasized that \xe2\x80\x9cthe independent expenditure ceiling \xe2\x80\xa6 fails to serve any substantial governmental interest in stemming the reality or appearance of corruption in the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 47\xe2\x80\x9348, because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he absence of prearrangement and coordination . . . alleviates the danger that expenditures will be given as a quid pro quo for improper commitments from the candidate,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 47. Buckley invalidated \xc2\xa7608(e)\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on independent expenditures, with only one Justice dissenting. See Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. National Conservative Political Action Comm., 470 U. S. 480, 491, n.\xe2\x80\x823 (1985) (NCPAC).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Buckley did not consider \xc2\xa7610\xe2\x80\x99s separate ban on corporate and union independent expenditures, the prohibition that had also been in the background in CIO, Automobile Workers, and Pipefitters. Had \xc2\xa7610 been challenged in the wake of Buckley, however, it could not have been squared with the reasoning and analysis of that precedent. See WRTL, supra, at 487 (opinion of Scalia, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cBuckley might well have been the last word on limitations on independent expenditures\xe2\x80\x9d); Austin, 494 U. S., at 683 (Scalia, J., dissenting). The expenditure ban invalidated in Buckley, \xc2\xa7608(e), applied to corporations and unions, 424 U. S., at 23, 39, n. 45; and some of the prevailing plaintiffs in Buckley were corporations, id., at 8. The Buckley Court did not invoke the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s overbreadth doctrine, see Broadrick v. Oklahoma, 413 U. S. 601, 615 (1973), to suggest that \xc2\xa7608(e)\xe2\x80\x99s expenditure ban would have been constitutional if it had applied only to corporations and not to individuals, 424 U. S., at 50. Buckley cited with approval the Automobile Workers dissent, which argued that \xc2\xa7610 was unconstitutional. 424 U. S., at 43 (citing 352 U. S., at 595\xe2\x80\x93596 (opinion of Douglas, J.)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Notwithstanding this precedent, Congress recodified \xc2\xa7610\xe2\x80\x99s corporate and union expenditure ban at 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b four months after Buckley was decided. See 90 Stat. 490. Section 441b is the independent expenditure restriction challenged here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Less than two years after Buckley, Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, reaffirmed the First Amendment principle that the Government cannot restrict political speech based on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s corporate identity. Bellotti could not have been clearer when it struck down a state-law prohibition on corporate independent expenditures related to referenda issues:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cWe thus find no support in the First . . . Amendment, or in the decisions of this Court, for the proposition that speech that otherwise would be within the protection of the First Amendment loses that protection simply because its source is a corporation that cannot prove, to the satisfaction of a court, a material effect on its business or property. . . . [That proposition] amounts to an impermissible legislative prohibition of speech based on the identity of the interests that spokesmen may represent in public debate over controversial issues and a requirement that the speaker have a sufficiently great interest in the subject to justify communication.

\n

.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cIn the realm of protected speech, the legislature is constitutionally disqualified from dictating the subjects about which persons may speak and the speakers who may address a public issue.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 784\xe2\x80\x93785.

\n

\nIt is important to note that the reasoning and holding of Bellotti did not rest on the existence of a viewpoint-discriminatory statute. It rested on the principle that the Government lacks the power to ban corporations from speaking.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Bellotti did not address the constitutionality of the State\xe2\x80\x99s ban on corporate independent expenditures to support candidates. In our view, however, that restriction would have been unconstitutional under Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s central principle: that the First Amendment does not allow political speech restrictions based on a speaker\xe2\x80\x99s corporate identity. See ibid.

\n

3

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus the law stood until Austin. Austin \xe2\x80\x9cuph[eld] a direct restriction on the independent expenditure of funds for political speech for the first time in [this Court\xe2\x80\x99s] history.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 695 (Kennedy, J., dissenting). There, the Michigan Chamber of Commerce sought to use general treasury funds to run a newspaper ad supporting a specific candidate. Michigan law, however, prohibited corporate independent expenditures that supported or opposed any candidate for state office. A violation of the law was punishable as a felony. The Court sustained the speech prohibition.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To bypass Buckley and Bellotti, the Austin Court identified a new governmental interest in limiting political speech: an antidistortion interest. Austin found a compelling governmental interest in preventing \xe2\x80\x9cthe corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth that are accumulated with the help of the corporate form and that have little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 660; see id., at 659 (citing MCFL, 479 U. S., at 257; NCPAC, 470 U. S., at 500\xe2\x80\x93501).

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court is thus confronted with conflicting lines of precedent: a pre-Austin line that forbids restrictions on political speech based on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s corporate identity and a post-Austin line that permits them. No case before Austin had held that Congress could prohibit independent expenditures for political speech based on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s corporate identity. Before Austin Congress had enacted legislation for this purpose, and the Government urged the same proposition before this Court. See MCFL, supra, at 257 (FEC posited that Congress intended to \xe2\x80\x9ccurb the political influence of \xe2\x80\x98those who exercise control over large aggregations of capital\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Automobile Workers, supra, at 585)); California Medical Assn. v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 453 U. S. 182, 201 (1981) (Congress believed that \xe2\x80\x9cdiffering structures and purposes\xe2\x80\x9d of corporations and unions \xe2\x80\x9cmay require different forms of regulation in order to protect the integrity of the electoral process\xe2\x80\x9d). In neither of these cases did the Court adopt the proposition.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In its defense of the corporate-speech restrictions in \xc2\xa7441b, the Government notes the antidistortion rationale on which Austin and its progeny rest in part, yet it all but abandons reliance upon it. It argues instead that two other compelling interests support Austin\xe2\x80\x99s holding that corporate expenditure restrictions are constitutional: an anticorruption interest, see 494 U. S., at 678 (Stevens, J., concurring), and a shareholder-protection interest, see id., at 674\xe2\x80\x93675 (Brennan, J., concurring). We consider the three points in turn.

\n

1

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As for Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale, the Government does little to defend it. See Tr. of Oral Arg. 45\xe2\x80\x9348 (Sept. 9, 2009). And with good reason, for the rationale cannot support \xc2\xa7441b.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If the First Amendment has any force, it prohibits Congress from fining or jailing citizens, or associations of citizens, for simply engaging in political speech. If the antidistortion rationale were to be accepted, however, it would permit Government to ban political speech simply because the speaker is an association that has taken on the corporate form. The Government contends that Austin permits it to ban corporate expenditures for almost all forms of communication stemming from a corporation. See Part II\xe2\x80\x93E, supra; Tr. of Oral Arg. 66 (Sept. 9, 2009); see also id., at 26\xe2\x80\x9331 (Mar. 24, 2009). If Austin were correct, the Government could prohibit a corporation from expressing political views in media beyond those presented here, such as by printing books. The Government responds \xe2\x80\x9cthat the FEC has never applied this statute to a book,\xe2\x80\x9d and if it did, \xe2\x80\x9cthere would be quite [a] good as-applied challenge.\xe2\x80\x9d Tr. of Oral Arg. 65 (Sept. 9, 2009). This troubling assertion of brooding governmental power cannot be reconciled with the confidence and stability in civic discourse that the First Amendment must secure.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Political speech is \xe2\x80\x9cindispensable to decisionmaking in a democracy, and this is no less true because the speech comes from a corporation rather than an individual.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 777 (footnote omitted); see ibid. (the worth of speech \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not depend upon the identity of its source, whether corporation, association, union, or individual\xe2\x80\x9d); Buckley, 424 U. S., at 48\xe2\x80\x9349 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he concept that government may restrict the speech of some elements of our society in order to enhance the relative voice of others is wholly foreign to the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x9d); Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 597 (Douglas, J., dissenting); CIO, 335 U. S., at 154\xe2\x80\x93155 (Rutledge, J., concurring in result). This protection for speech is inconsistent with Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale. Austin sought to defend the antidistortion rationale as a means to prevent corporations from obtaining \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98an unfair advantage in the political marketplace\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d by using \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98resources amassed in the economic marketplace.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 659 (quoting MCFL, supra, at 257). But Buckley rejected the premise that the Government has an interest \xe2\x80\x9cin equalizing the relative ability of individuals and groups to influence the outcome of elections.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 48; see Bellotti, supra, at 791, n. 30. Buckley was specific in stating that \xe2\x80\x9cthe skyrocketing cost of political campaigns\xe2\x80\x9d could not sustain the governmental prohibition. 424 U. S., at 26. The First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s protections do not depend on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cfinancial ability to engage in public discussion.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 49.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court reaffirmed these conclusions when it invalidated the BCRA provision that increased the cap on contributions to one candidate if the opponent made certain expenditures from personal funds. See Davis v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 554 U. S. ___, ___ (2008) (slip op., at 16) (\xe2\x80\x9cLeveling electoral opportunities means making and implementing judgments about which strengths should be permitted to contribute to the outcome of an election. The Constitution, however, confers upon voters, not Congress, the power to choose the Members of the House of Representatives, Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa72, and it is a dangerous business for Congress to use the election laws to influence the voters\xe2\x80\x99 choices\xe2\x80\x9d). The rule that political speech cannot be limited based on a speaker\xe2\x80\x99s wealth is a necessary consequence of the premise that the First Amendment generally prohibits the suppression of political speech based on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s identity.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Either as support for its antidistortion rationale or as a further argument, the Austin majority undertook to distinguish wealthy individuals from corporations on the ground that \xe2\x80\x9c[s]tate law grants corporations special advantages\xe2\x80\x94such as limited liability, perpetual life, and favorable treatment of the accumulation and distribution of assets.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 658\xe2\x80\x93659. This does not suffice, however, to allow laws prohibiting speech. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is rudimentary that the State cannot exact as the price of those special advantages the forfeiture of First Amendment rights.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 680 (Scalia, J., dissenting).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is irrelevant for purposes of the First Amendment that corporate funds may \xe2\x80\x9chave little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 660 (majority opinion). All speakers, including individuals and the media, use money amassed from the economic marketplace to fund their speech. The First Amendment protects the resulting speech, even if it was enabled by economic transactions with persons or entities who disagree with the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s ideas. See id., at 707 (Kennedy, J., dissenting) (\xe2\x80\x9cMany persons can trace their funds to corporations, if not in the form of donations, then in the form of dividends, interest, or salary\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale would produce the dangerous, and unacceptable, consequence that Congress could ban political speech of media corporations. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 283 (opinion of Thomas, J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe chilling endpoint of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning is not difficult to foresee: outright regulation of the press\xe2\x80\x9d). Cf. Tornillo, 418 U. S., at 250 (alleging the existence of \xe2\x80\x9cvast accumulations of unreviewable power in the modern media empires\xe2\x80\x9d). Media corporations are now exempt from \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s ban on corporate expenditures. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7431(9)(B)(i), 434(f)(3)(B)(i). Yet media corporations accumulate wealth with the help of the corporate form, the largest media corporations have \xe2\x80\x9cimmense aggregations of wealth,\xe2\x80\x9d and the views expressed by media corporations often \xe2\x80\x9chave little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support\xe2\x80\x9d for those views. Austin, 494 U. S., at 660. Thus, under the Government\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning, wealthy media corporations could have their voices diminished to put them on par with other media entities. There is no precedent for permitting this under the First Amendment.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The media exemption discloses further difficulties with the law now under consideration. There is no precedent supporting laws that attempt to distinguish between corporations which are deemed to be exempt as media corporations and those which are not. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have consistently rejected the proposition that the institutional press has any constitutional privilege beyond that of other speakers.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 691 (Scalia, J., dissenting) (citing Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 782); see Dun & Bradstreet, Inc. v. Greenmoss Builders, Inc., 472 U. S. 749, 784 (1985) (Brennan, J., joined by Marshall, Blackmun, and Stevens, JJ., dissenting); id., at 773 (White, J., concurring in judgment). With the advent of the Internet and the decline of print and broadcast media, moreover, the line between the media and others who wish to comment on political and social issues becomes far more blurred.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The law\xe2\x80\x99s exception for media corporations is, on its own terms, all but an admission of the invalidity of the antidistortion rationale. And the exemption results in a further, separate reason for finding this law invalid: Again by its own terms, the law exempts some corporations but covers others, even though both have the need or the motive to communicate their views. The exemption applies to media corporations owned or controlled by corporations that have diverse and substantial investments and participate in endeavors other than news. So even assuming the most doubtful proposition that a news organization has a right to speak when others do not, the exemption would allow a conglomerate that owns both a media business and an unrelated business to influence or control the media in order to advance its overall business interest. At the same time, some other corporation, with an identical business interest but no media outlet in its ownership structure, would be forbidden to speak or inform the public about the same issue. This differential treatment cannot be squared with the First Amendment.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is simply no support for the view that the First Amendment, as originally understood, would permit the suppression of political speech by media corporations. The Framers may not have anticipated modern business and media corporations. See McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334, 360\xe2\x80\x93361 (1995) (Thomas, J., concurring in judgment). Yet television networks and major newspapers owned by media corporations have become the most important means of mass communication in modern times. The First Amendment was certainly not understood to condone the suppression of political speech in society\xe2\x80\x99s most salient media. It was understood as a response to the repression of speech and the press that had existed in England and the heavy taxes on the press that were imposed in the colonies. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 252\xe2\x80\x93253 (opinion of Scalia, J.); Grosjean, 297 U. S., at 245\xe2\x80\x93248; Near, 283 U. S., at 713\xe2\x80\x93714. The great debates between the Federalists and the Anti-Federalists over our founding document were published and expressed in the most important means of mass communication of that era\xe2\x80\x94newspapers owned by individuals. See McIntyre, 514 U. S., at 341\xe2\x80\x93343; id., at 367 (Thomas, J., concurring in judgment). At the founding, speech was open, comprehensive, and vital to society\xe2\x80\x99s definition of itself; there were no limits on the sources of speech and knowledge. See B. Bailyn, Ideological Origins of the American Revolution 5 (1967) (\xe2\x80\x9cAny number of people could join in such proliferating polemics, and rebuttals could come from all sides\xe2\x80\x9d); G. Wood, Creation of the American Republic 1776\xe2\x80\x931787, p. 6 (1969) (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]t is not surprising that the intellectual sources of [the Americans\xe2\x80\x99] Revolutionary thought were profuse and various\xe2\x80\x9d). The Framers may have been unaware of certain types of speakers or forms of communication, but that does not mean that those speakers and media are entitled to less First Amendment protection than those types of speakers and media that provided the means of communicating political ideas when the Bill of Rights was adopted.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin interferes with the \xe2\x80\x9copen marketplace\xe2\x80\x9d of ideas protected by the First Amendment. New York State Bd. of Elections v. Lopez Torres, 552 U. S. 196, 208 (2008); see ibid. (ideas \xe2\x80\x9cmay compete\xe2\x80\x9d in this marketplace \xe2\x80\x9cwithout government interference\xe2\x80\x9d); McConnell, supra, at 274 (opinion of Thomas, J.). It permits the Government to ban the political speech of millions of associations of citizens. See Statistics of Income 2 (5.8 million for-profit corporations filed 2006 tax returns). Most of these are small corporations without large amounts of wealth. See Supp. Brief for Chamber of Commerce of the United States of America as Amicus Curiae 1, 3 (96% of the 3 million businesses that belong to the U. S. Chamber of Commerce have fewer than 100 employees); M. Keightley, Congressional Research Service Report for Congress, Business Organizational Choices: Taxation and Responses to Legislative Changes 10 (2009) (more than 75% of corporations whose income is taxed under federal law, see 26 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7301, have less than $1 million in receipts per year). This fact belies the Government\xe2\x80\x99s argument that the statute is justified on the ground that it prevents the \xe2\x80\x9cdistorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth.\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 660. It is not even aimed at amassed wealth.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The censorship we now confront is vast in its reach. The Government has \xe2\x80\x9cmuffle[d] the voices that best represent the most significant segments of the economy.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, supra, at 257\xe2\x80\x93258 (opinion of Scalia, J.). And \xe2\x80\x9cthe electorate [has been] deprived of information, knowledge and opinion vital to its function.\xe2\x80\x9d CIO, 335 U. S., at 144 (Rutledge, J., concurring in result). By suppressing the speech of manifold corporations, both for-profit and nonprofit, the Government prevents their voices and viewpoints from reaching the public and advising voters on which persons or entities are hostile to their interests. Factions will necessarily form in our Republic, but the remedy of \xe2\x80\x9cdestroying the liberty\xe2\x80\x9d of some factions is \xe2\x80\x9cworse than the disease.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No. 10, p. 130 (B. Wright ed. 1961) (J. Madison). Factions should be checked by permitting them all to speak, see ibid., and by entrusting the people to judge what is true and what is false.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The purpose and effect of this law is to prevent corporations, including small and nonprofit corporations, from presenting both facts and opinions to the public. This makes Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale all the more an aberration. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he First Amendment protects the right of corporations to petition legislative and administrative bodies.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 792, n. 31 (citing California Motor Transport Co. v. Trucking Unlimited, 404 U. S. 508, 510\xe2\x80\x93511 (1972); Eastern Railroad Presidents Conference v. Noerr Motor Freight, Inc., 365 U. S. 127, 137\xe2\x80\x93138 (1961)). Corporate executives and employees counsel Members of Congress and Presidential administrations on many issues, as a matter of routine and often in private. An amici brief filed on behalf of Montana and 25 other States notes that lobbying and corporate communications with elected officials occur on a regular basis. Brief for State of Montana et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 19. When that phenomenon is coupled with \xc2\xa7441b, the result is that smaller or nonprofit corporations cannot raise a voice to object when other corporations, including those with vast wealth, are cooperating with the Government. That cooperation may sometimes be voluntary, or it may be at the demand of a Government official who uses his or her authority, influence, and power to threaten corporations to support the Government\xe2\x80\x99s policies. Those kinds of interactions are often unknown and unseen. The speech that \xc2\xa7441b forbids, though, is public, and all can judge its content and purpose. References to massive corporate treasuries should not mask the real operation of this law. Rhetoric ought not obscure reality.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s expenditure ban were constitutional, wealthy corporations could still lobby elected officials, although smaller corporations may not have the resources to do so. And wealthy individuals and unincorporated associations can spend unlimited amounts on independent expenditures. See, e.g., WRTL, 551 U. S., at 503\xe2\x80\x93504 (opinion of Scalia,\xc2\xa0J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cIn the 2004 election cycle, a mere 24 individuals contributed an astounding total of $142 million to [26 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7527 organizations]\xe2\x80\x9d). Yet certain disfavored associations of citizens\xe2\x80\x94those that have taken on the corporate form\xe2\x80\x94are penalized for engaging in the same political speech.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When Government seeks to use its full power, including the criminal law, to command where a person may get his or her information or what distrusted source he or she may not hear, it uses censorship to control thought. This is unlawful. The First Amendment confirms the freedom to think for ourselves.

\n

2

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0What we have said also shows the invalidity of other arguments made by the Government. For the most part relinquishing the antidistortion rationale, the Government falls back on the argument that corporate political speech can be banned in order to prevent corruption or its appearance. In Buckley, the Court found this interest \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important\xe2\x80\x9d to allow limits on contributions but did not extend that reasoning to expenditure limits. 424 U. S., at 25. When Buckley examined an expenditure ban, it found \xe2\x80\x9cthat the governmental interest in preventing corruption and the appearance of corruption [was] inadequate to justify [the ban] on independent expenditures.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 45.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0With regard to large direct contributions, Buckley reasoned that they could be given \xe2\x80\x9cto secure a political quid pro quo,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 26, and that \xe2\x80\x9cthe scope of such pernicious practices can never be reliably ascertained,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 27. The practices Buckley noted would be covered by bribery laws, see, e.g., 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7201, if a quid pro quo arrangement were proved. See Buckley, supra, at 27, and n. 28 (citing Buckley v. Valeo, 519 F.\xc2\xa02d 821, 839\xe2\x80\x93840, and nn. 36\xe2\x80\x9338 (CADC 1975) (en banc) (per curiam)). The Court, in consequence, has noted that restrictions on direct contributions are preventative, because few if any contributions to candidates will involve quid pro quo arrangements. MCFL, 479 U. S., at 260; NCPAC, 470 U. S., at 500; Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. National Right to Work Comm., 459 U. S. 197, 210 (1982) (NRWC). The Buckley Court, nevertheless, sustained limits on direct contributions in order to ensure against the reality or appearance of corruption. That case did not extend this rationale to independent expenditures, and the Court does not do so here.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe absence of prearrangement and coordination of an expenditure with the candidate or his agent not only undermines the value of the expenditure to the candidate, but also alleviates the danger that expenditures will be given as a quid pro quo for improper commitments from the candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 47; see ibid. (independent expenditures have a \xe2\x80\x9csubstantially diminished potential for abuse\xe2\x80\x9d). Limits on independent expenditures, such as \xc2\xa7441b, have a chilling effect extending well beyond the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interest in preventing quid pro quo corruption. The anticorruption interest is not sufficient to displace the speech here in question. Indeed, 26 States do not restrict independent expenditures by for-profit corporations. The Government does not claim that these expenditures have corrupted the political process in those States. See Supp. Brief for Appellee 18, n.\xc2\xa03; Supp. Brief for Chamber of Commerce of the United States of America as Amicus Curiae 8\xe2\x80\x939, n.\xc2\xa05.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A single footnote in Bellotti purported to leave open the possibility that corporate independent expenditures could be shown to cause corruption. 435 U. S., at 788, n. 26. For the reasons explained above, we now conclude that independent expenditures, including those made by corporations, do not give rise to corruption or the appearance of corruption. Dicta in Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s footnote suggested that \xe2\x80\x9ca corporation\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak on issues of general public interest implies no comparable right in the quite different context of participation in a political campaign for election to public office.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Citing the portion of Buckley that invalidated the federal independent expenditure ban, 424 U. S., at 46, and a law review student comment, Bellotti surmised that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress might well be able to demonstrate the existence of a danger of real or apparent corruption in independent expenditures by corporations to influence candidate elections.\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 788, n. 26. Buckley, however, struck down a ban on independent expenditures to support candidates that covered corporations, 424 U. S., at 23, 39, n. 45, and explained that \xe2\x80\x9cthe distinction between discussion of issues and candidates and advocacy of election or defeat of candidates may often dissolve in practical application,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 42. Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s dictum is thus supported only by a law review student comment, which misinterpreted Buckley. See Comment, The Regulation of Union Political Activity: Majority and Minority Rights and Remedies, 126 U.\xc2\xa0Pa. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 386, 408 (1977) (suggesting that \xe2\x80\x9ccorporations and labor unions should be held to different and more stringent standards than an individual or other associations under a regulatory scheme for campaign financing\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Seizing on this aside in Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s footnote, the Court in NRWC did say there is a \xe2\x80\x9csufficient\xe2\x80\x9d governmental interest in \xe2\x80\x9censur[ing] that substantial aggregations of wealth amassed\xe2\x80\x9d by corporations would not \xe2\x80\x9cbe used to incur political debts from legislators who are aided by the contributions.\xe2\x80\x9d 459 U. S., at 207\xe2\x80\x93208 (citing Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 579); see 459 U. S., at 210, and n.\xc2\xa07; NCPAC, supra, at 500\xe2\x80\x93501 (NRWC suggested a governmental interest in restricting \xe2\x80\x9cthe influence of political war chests funneled through the corporate form\xe2\x80\x9d). NRWC, however, has little relevance here. NRWC decided no more than that a restriction on a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s ability to solicit funds for its segregated PAC, which made direct contributions to candidates, did not violate the First Amendment. 459 U. S., at 206. NRWC thus involved contribution limits, see NCPAC, supra, at 495\xe2\x80\x93496, which, unlike limits on independent expenditures, have been an accepted means to prevent quid pro quo corruption, see McConnell, 540 U. S., at 136\xe2\x80\x93138, and n. 40; MCFL, supra, at 259\xe2\x80\x93260. Citizens United has not made direct contributions to candidates, and it has not suggested that the Court should reconsider whether contribution limits should be subjected to rigorous First Amendment scrutiny.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When Buckley identified a sufficiently important governmental interest in preventing corruption or the appearance of corruption, that interest was limited to quid pro quo corruption. See McConnell, supra, at 296\xe2\x80\x93298 (opinion of Kennedy, J.) (citing Buckley, supra, at 26\xe2\x80\x9328, 30, 46\xe2\x80\x9348); NCPAC, 470 U. S., at 497 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe hallmark of corruption is the financial quid pro quo: dollars for political favors\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 498. The fact that speakers may have influence over or access to elected officials does not mean that these officials are corrupt:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9cFavoritism and influence are not . . . avoidable in representative politics. It is in the nature of an elected representative to favor certain policies, and, by necessary corollary, to favor the voters and contributors who support those policies. It is well understood that a substantial and legitimate reason, if not the only reason, to cast a vote for, or to make a contribution to, one candidate over another is that the candidate will respond by producing those political outcomes the supporter favors. Democracy is premised on responsiveness.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 297 (opinion of Kennedy, J.).

\n

\nReliance on a \xe2\x80\x9cgeneric favoritism or influence theory . . . is at odds with standard First Amendment analyses because it is unbounded and susceptible to no limiting principle.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 296.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The appearance of influence or access, furthermore, will not cause the electorate to lose faith in our democracy. By definition, an independent expenditure is political speech presented to the electorate that is not coordinated with a candidate. See Buckley, supra, at 46. The fact that a corporation, or any other speaker, is willing to spend money to try to persuade voters presupposes that the people have the ultimate influence over elected officials. This is inconsistent with any suggestion that the electorate will refuse \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98to take part in democratic governance\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d because of additional political speech made by a corporation or any other speaker. McConnell, supra, at 144 (quoting Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 390 (2000)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Caperton v. A. T. Massey Coal Co., 556 U. S. ___ (2009), is not to the contrary. Caperton held that a judge was required to recuse himself \xe2\x80\x9cwhen a person with a personal stake in a particular case had a significant and disproportionate influence in placing the judge on the case by raising funds or directing the judge\xe2\x80\x99s election campaign when the case was pending or imminent.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at ___ (slip op., at 14). The remedy of recusal was based on a litigant\xe2\x80\x99s due process right to a fair trial before an unbiased judge. See Withrow v. Larkin, 421 U. S. 35, 46 (1975). Caperton\xe2\x80\x99s holding was limited to the rule that the judge must be recused, not that the litigant\xe2\x80\x99s political speech could be banned.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The McConnell record was \xe2\x80\x9cover 100,000 pages\xe2\x80\x9d long, McConnell I, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 209, yet it \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not have any direct examples of votes being exchanged for . . . expenditures,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 560 (opinion of Kollar-Kotelly, J.). This confirms Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning that independent expenditures do not lead to, or create the appearance of, quid pro quo corruption. In fact, there is only scant evidence that independent expenditures even ingratiate. See 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 555\xe2\x80\x93557 (opinion of Kollar-Kotelly, J.). Ingratiation and access, in any event, are not corruption. The BCRA record establishes that certain donations to political parties, called \xe2\x80\x9csoft money,\xe2\x80\x9d were made to gain access to elected officials. McConnell, supra, at 125, 130\xe2\x80\x93131, 146\xe2\x80\x93152; see McConnell I, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 471\xe2\x80\x93481, 491\xe2\x80\x93506 (opinion of Kollar-Kotelly, J.); id., at 842\xe2\x80\x93843, 858\xe2\x80\x93859 (opinion of Leon, J.). This case, however, is about independent expenditures, not soft money. When Congress finds that a problem exists, we must give that finding due deference; but Congress may not choose an unconstitutional remedy. If elected officials succumb to improper influences from independent expenditures; if they surrender their best judgment; and if they put expediency before principle, then surely there is cause for concern. We must give weight to attempts by Congress to seek to dispel either the appearance or the reality of these influences. The remedies enacted by law, however, must comply with the First Amendment; and, it is our law and our tradition that more speech, not less, is the governing rule. An outright ban on corporate political speech during the critical preelection period is not a permissible remedy. Here Congress has created categorical bans on speech that are asymmetrical to preventing quid pro quo corruption.

\n

3

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government contends further that corporate independent expenditures can be limited because of its interest in protecting dissenting shareholders from being compelled to fund corporate political speech. This asserted interest, like Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale, would allow the Government to ban the political speech even of media corporations. See supra, at 35\xe2\x80\x9337. Assume, for example, that a shareholder of a corporation that owns a newspaper disagrees with the political views the newspaper expresses. See Austin, 494 U. S., at 687 (Scalia, J., dissenting). Under the Government\xe2\x80\x99s view, that potential disagreement could give the Government the authority to restrict the media corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political speech. The First Amendment does not allow that power. There is, furthermore, little evidence of abuse that cannot be corrected by shareholders \xe2\x80\x9cthrough the procedures of corporate democracy.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 794; see id., at 794, n. 34.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Those reasons are sufficient to reject this shareholder-protection interest; and, moreover, the statute is both underinclusive and overinclusive. As to the first, if Congress had been seeking to protect dissenting shareholders, it would not have banned corporate speech in only certain media within 30 or 60 days before an election. A dissenting shareholder\xe2\x80\x99s interests would be implicated by speech in any media at any time. As to the second, the statute is overinclusive because it covers all corporations, including nonprofit corporations and for-profit corporations with only single shareholders. As to other corporations, the remedy is not to restrict speech but to consider and explore other regulatory mechanisms. The regulatory mechanism here, based on speech, contravenes the First Amendment.

\n

4

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We need not reach the question whether the Government has a compelling interest in preventing foreign individuals or associations from influencing our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s political process. Cf. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441e (contribution and expenditure ban applied to \xe2\x80\x9cforeign national[s]\xe2\x80\x9d). Section 441b is not limited to corporations or associations that were created in foreign countries or funded predominately by foreign shareholders. Section 441b therefore would be overbroad even if we assumed, arguendo, that the Government has a compelling interest in limiting foreign influence over our political process. See Broadrick, 413 U. S., at 615.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our precedent is to be respected unless the most convincing of reasons demonstrates that adherence to it puts us on a course that is sure error. \xe2\x80\x9cBeyond workability, the relevant factors in deciding whether to adhere to the principle of stare decisis include the antiquity of the precedent, the reliance interests at stake, and of course whether the decision was well reasoned.\xe2\x80\x9d Montejo v. Louisiana, 556 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 13) (overruling Michigan v. Jackson, 475 U. S. 625 (1986)). We have also examined whether \xe2\x80\x9cexperience has pointed up the precedent\xe2\x80\x99s shortcomings.\xe2\x80\x9d Pearson v. Callahan, 555 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 8) (overruling Saucier v. Katz, 533 U. S. 194 (2001)).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These considerations counsel in favor of rejecting Austin, which itself contravened this Court\xe2\x80\x99s earlier precedents in Buckley and Bellotti. \xe2\x80\x9cThis Court has not hesitated to overrule decisions offensive to the First Amendment.\xe2\x80\x9d WRTL, 551 U. S., at 500 (opinion of Scalia, J.). \xe2\x80\x9c[S]tare decisis is a principle of policy and not a mechanical formula of adherence to the latest decision.\xe2\x80\x9d Helvering v. Hallock, 309 U. S. 106, 119 (1940).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons above, it must be concluded that Austin was not well reasoned. The Government defends Austin, relying almost entirely on \xe2\x80\x9cthe quid pro quo interest, the corruption interest or the shareholder interest,\xe2\x80\x9d and not Austin\xe2\x80\x99s expressed antidistortion rationale. Tr. of Oral Arg. 48 (Sept. 9, 2009); see id., at 45\xe2\x80\x9346. When neither party defends the reasoning of a precedent, the principle of adhering to that precedent through stare decisis is diminished. Austin abandoned First Amendment principles, furthermore, by relying on language in some of our precedents that traces back to the Automobile Workers Court\xe2\x80\x99s flawed historical account of campaign finance laws, see Brief for Campaign Finance Scholars as Amici Curiae; Hayward, 45 Harv. J. Legis. 421; R. Mutch, Campaigns, Congress, and Courts 33\xe2\x80\x9335, 153\xe2\x80\x93157 (1988). See Austin, supra, at 659 (quoting MCFL, 479 U. S., at 257\xe2\x80\x93258; NCPAC, 470 U. S., at 500\xe2\x80\x93501); MCFL, supra, at 257 (quoting Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 585); NCPAC, supra, at 500 (quoting NRWC, 459 U. S., at 210); id., at 208 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe history of the movement to regulate the political contributions and expenditures of corporations and labor unions is set forth in great detail in [Automobile Workers], supra, at 570\xe2\x80\x93584, and we need only summarize the development here\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin is undermined by experience since its announcement. Political speech is so ingrained in our culture that speakers find ways to circumvent campaign finance laws. See, e.g., McConnell, 540 U. S., at 176\xe2\x80\x93177 (\xe2\x80\x9cGiven BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s tighter restrictions on the raising and spending of soft money, the incentives . . . to exploit [26 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7527] organizations will only increase\xe2\x80\x9d). Our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s speech dynamic is changing, and informative voices should not have to circumvent onerous restrictions to exercise their First Amendment rights. Speakers have become adept at presenting citizens with sound bites, talking points, and scripted messages that dominate the 24-hour news cycle. Corporations, like individuals, do not have monolithic views. On certain topics corporations may possess valuable expertise, leaving them the best equipped to point out errors or fallacies in speech of\nall sorts, including the speech of candidates and elected officials.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Rapid changes in technology\xe2\x80\x94and the creative dynamic inherent in the concept of free expression\xe2\x80\x94counsel against upholding a law that restricts political speech in certain media or by certain speakers. See Part II\xe2\x80\x93C, supra. Today, 30-second television ads may be the most effective way to convey a political message. See McConnell, supra, at 261 (opinion of Scalia, J.). Soon, however, it may be that Internet sources, such as blogs and social networking Web sites, will provide citizens with significant information about political candidates and issues. Yet, \xc2\xa7441b would seem to ban a blog post expressly advocating the election or defeat of a candidate if that blog were created with corporate funds. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(a); MCFL, supra, at 249. The First Amendment does not permit Congress to make these categorical distinctions based on the corporate identity of the speaker and the content of the political speech.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0No serious reliance interests are at stake. As the Court stated in Payne v. Tennessee, 501 U. S. 808, 828 (1991), reliance interests are important considerations in property and contract cases, where parties may have acted in conformance with existing legal rules in order to conduct transactions. Here, though, parties have been prevented from acting\xe2\x80\x94corporations have been banned from making independent expenditures. Legislatures may have enacted bans on corporate expenditures believing that those bans were constitutional. This is not a compelling interest for stare decisis. If it were, legislative acts could prevent us from overruling our own precedents, thereby interfering with our duty \xe2\x80\x9cto say what the law is.\xe2\x80\x9d Marbury v. Madison, 1 Cranch 137, 177 (1803).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Due consideration leads to this conclusion: Austin, 494 U. S. 652, should be and now is overruled. We return to the principle established in Buckley and Bellotti that the Government may not suppress political speech on the basis of the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s corporate identity. No sufficient governmental interest justifies limits on the political speech of nonprofit or for-profit corporations.

\n

D

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin is overruled, so it provides no basis for allowing the Government to limit corporate independent expenditures. As the Government appears to concede, overruling Austin \xe2\x80\x9ceffectively invalidate[s] not only BCRA Section 203, but also 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. 441b\xe2\x80\x99s prohibition on the use of corporate treasury funds for express advocacy.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellee 33, n.\xc2\xa012. Section 441b\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on corporate independent expenditures are therefore invalid and cannot be applied to Hillary.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given our conclusion we are further required to overrule the part of McConnell that upheld BCRA \xc2\xa7203\xe2\x80\x99s extension of \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on corporate independent expenditures. See 540 U. S., at 203\xe2\x80\x93209. The McConnell Court relied on the antidistortion interest recognized in Austin to uphold a greater restriction on speech than the restriction upheld in Austin, see 540 U. S., at 205, and we have found this interest unconvincing and insufficient. This part of McConnell is now overruled.

\n

IV

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United next challenges BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclaimer and disclosure provisions as applied to Hillary and the three advertisements for the movie. Under BCRA \xc2\xa7311, televised electioneering communications funded by anyone other than a candidate must include a disclaimer that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98_______ is responsible for the content of this advertising.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 2 U. S. C. \xc2\xa7441d(d)(2). The required statement must be made in a \xe2\x80\x9cclearly spoken manner,\xe2\x80\x9d and displayed on the screen in a \xe2\x80\x9cclearly readable manner\xe2\x80\x9d for at least four seconds. Ibid. It must state that the communication \xe2\x80\x9cis not authorized by any candidate or candidate\xe2\x80\x99s committee\xe2\x80\x9d; it must also display the name and address (or Web site address) of the person or group that funded the advertisement. \xc2\xa7441d(a)(3). Under BCRA \xc2\xa7201, any person who spends more than $10,000 on electioneering communications within a calendar year must file a disclosure statement with the FEC. 2 U. S. C. \xc2\xa7434(f)(1). That statement must identify the person making the expenditure, the amount of the expenditure, the election to which the communication was directed, and the names of certain contributors. \xc2\xa7434(f)(2).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Disclaimer and disclosure requirements may burden the ability to speak, but they \xe2\x80\x9cimpose no ceiling on campaign-related activities,\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 64, and \xe2\x80\x9cdo not prevent anyone from speaking,\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, supra, at 201 (internal quotation marks and brackets omitted). The Court has subjected these requirements to \xe2\x80\x9cexacting scrutiny,\xe2\x80\x9d which requires a \xe2\x80\x9csubstantial relation\xe2\x80\x9d between the disclosure requirement and a \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important\xe2\x80\x9d governmental interest. Buckley, supra, at 64, 66 (internal quotation marks omitted); see McConnell, supra, at 231\xe2\x80\x93232.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Buckley, the Court explained that disclosure could be justified based on a governmental interest in \xe2\x80\x9cprovid[ing] the electorate with information\xe2\x80\x9d about the sources of election-related spending. 424 U. S., at 66. The McConnell Court applied this interest in rejecting facial challenges to BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311. 540 U. S., at 196. There was evidence in the record that independent groups were running election-related advertisements \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98while hiding behind dubious and misleading names.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 197 (quoting McConnell I, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 237). The Court therefore upheld BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311 on the ground that they would help citizens \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98make informed choices in the political marketplace.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 197 (quoting McConnell I, supra, at 237); see 540 U. S., at 231.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although both provisions were facially upheld, the Court acknowledged that as-applied challenges would be available if a group could show a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98reasonable probability\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d that disclosure of its contributors\xe2\x80\x99 names \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98will subject them to threats, harassment, or reprisals from either Government officials or private parties.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 198 (quoting Buckley, supra, at 74).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the reasons stated below, we find the statute valid as applied to the ads for the movie and to the movie itself.

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United sought to broadcast one 30-second and two 10-second ads to promote Hillary. Under FEC regulations, a communication that \xe2\x80\x9c[p]roposes a commercial transaction\xe2\x80\x9d was not subject to 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on corporate or union funding of electioneering communications. 11 CFR \xc2\xa7114.15(b)(3)(ii). The regulations, however, do not exempt those communications from the disclaimer and disclosure requirements in BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311. See 72 Fed. Reg. 72901 (2007).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United argues that the disclaimer requirements in \xc2\xa7311 are unconstitutional as applied to its ads. It contends that the governmental interest in providing information to the electorate does not justify requiring disclaimers for any commercial advertisements, including the ones at issue here. We disagree. The ads fall within BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of an \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d: They referred to then-Senator Clinton by name shortly before a primary and contained pejorative references to her candidacy. See 530 F. Supp. 2d, at 276, nn. 2\xe2\x80\x934. The disclaimers required by \xc2\xa7311 \xe2\x80\x9cprovid[e] the electorate with information,\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, supra, at 196, and \xe2\x80\x9cinsure that the voters are fully informed\xe2\x80\x9d about the person or group who is speaking, Buckley, supra, at 76; see also Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 792, n. 32 (\xe2\x80\x9cIdentification of the source of advertising may be required as a means of disclosure, so that the people will be able to evaluate the arguments to which they are being subjected\xe2\x80\x9d). At the very least, the disclaimers avoid confusion by making clear that the ads are not funded by a candidate or political party.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United argues that \xc2\xa7311 is underinclusive because it requires disclaimers for broadcast advertisements but not for print or Internet advertising. It asserts that \xc2\xa7311 decreases both the quantity and effectiveness of the group\xe2\x80\x99s speech by forcing it to devote four seconds of each advertisement to the spoken disclaimer. We rejected these arguments in McConnell, supra, at 230\xe2\x80\x93231. And we now adhere to that decision as it pertains to the disclosure provisions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As a final point, Citizens United claims that, in any event, the disclosure requirements in \xc2\xa7201 must be confined to speech that is the functional equivalent of express advocacy. The principal opinion in WRTL limited 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on independent expenditures to express advocacy and its functional equivalent. 551 U. S., at 469\xe2\x80\x93476 (opinion of Roberts, C. J.). Citizens United seeks to import a similar distinction into BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements. We reject this contention.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court has explained that disclosure is a less restrictive alternative to more comprehensive regulations of speech. See, e.g., MCFL, 479 U. S., at 262. In Buckley, the Court upheld a disclosure requirement for independent expenditures even though it invalidated a provision that imposed a ceiling on those expenditures. 424 U. S., at 75\xe2\x80\x9376. In McConnell, three Justices who would have found \xc2\xa7441b to be unconstitutional nonetheless voted to uphold BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure and disclaimer requirements. 540 U. S., at 321 (opinion of Kennedy, J., joined by Rehnquist, C.\xc2\xa0J., and Scalia, J.). And the Court has upheld registration and disclosure requirements on lobbyists, even though Congress has no power to ban lobbying itself. United States v. Harriss, 347 U. S. 612, 625 (1954) (Congress \xe2\x80\x9chas merely provided for a modicum of information from those who for hire attempt to influence legislation or who collect or spend funds for that purpose\xe2\x80\x9d). For these reasons, we reject Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s contention that the disclosure requirements must be limited to speech that is the functional equivalent of express advocacy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United also disputes that an informational interest justifies the application of \xc2\xa7201 to its ads, which only attempt to persuade viewers to see the film. Even if it disclosed the funding sources for the ads, Citizens United says, the information would not help viewers make informed choices in the political marketplace. This is similar to the argument rejected above with respect to disclaimers. Even if the ads only pertain to a commercial transaction, the public has an interest in knowing who is speaking about a candidate shortly before an election. Because the informational interest alone is sufficient to justify application of \xc2\xa7201 to these ads, it is not necessary to consider the Government\xe2\x80\x99s other asserted interests.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Last, Citizens United argues that disclosure requirements can chill donations to an organization by exposing donors to retaliation. Some amici point to recent events in which donors to certain causes were blacklisted, threatened, or otherwise targeted for retaliation. See Brief for Institute for Justice as Amicus Curiae 13\xe2\x80\x9316; Brief for Alliance Defense Fund as Amicus Curiae 16\xe2\x80\x9322. In McConnell, the Court recognized that \xc2\xa7201 would be unconstitutional as applied to an organization if there were a reasonable probability that the group\xe2\x80\x99s members would face threats, harassment, or reprisals if their names were disclosed. 540 U. S., at 198. The examples cited by amici are cause for concern. Citizens United, however, has offered no evidence that its members may face similar threats or reprisals. To the contrary, Citizens United has been disclosing its donors for years and has identified no instance of harassment or retaliation.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Shareholder objections raised through the procedures of corporate democracy, see Bellotti, supra, at 794, and n. 34, can be more effective today because modern technology makes disclosures rapid and informative. A campaign finance system that pairs corporate independent expenditures with effective disclosure has not existed before today. It must be noted, furthermore, that many of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 findings in passing BCRA were premised on a system without adequate disclosure. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 128 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he public may not have been fully informed about the sponsorship of so-called issue ads\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 196\xe2\x80\x93197 (quoting McConnell I, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 237). With the advent of the Internet, prompt disclosure of expenditures can provide shareholders and citizens with the information needed to hold corporations and elected officials accountable for their positions and supporters. Shareholders can determine whether their corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political speech advances the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s interest in making profits, and citizens can see whether elected officials are \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98in the pocket\xe2\x80\x99 of so-called moneyed interests.\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 259 (opinion of Scalia, J.); see MCFL, supra, at 261. The First Amendment protects political speech; and disclosure permits citizens and shareholders to react to the speech of corporate entities in a proper way. This transparency enables the electorate to make informed decisions and give proper weight to different speakers and messages.

\n

C

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For the same reasons we uphold the application of BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311 to the ads, we affirm their application to Hillary. We find no constitutional impediment to the application of BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclaimer and disclosure requirements to a movie broadcast via video-on-demand. And there has been no showing that, as applied in this case, these requirements would impose a chill on speech or expression.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When word concerning the plot of the movie Mr. Smith Goes to Washington reached the circles of Government, some officials sought, by persuasion, to discourage its distribution. See Smoodin, \xe2\x80\x9cCompulsory\xe2\x80\x9d Viewing for Every Citizen: Mr. Smith and the Rhetoric of Reception, 35 Cinema Journal 3, 19, and n. 52 (Winter 1996) (citing Mr. Smith Riles Washington, Time, Oct. 30, 1939, p. 49); Nugent, Capra\xe2\x80\x99s Capitol Offense, N.\xc2\xa0Y. Times, Oct. 29, 1939, p. X5. Under Austin, though, officials could have done more than discourage its distribution\xe2\x80\x94they could have banned the film. After all, it, like Hillary, was speech funded by a corporation that was critical of Members of Congress. Mr. Smith Goes to Washington may be fiction and caricature; but fiction and caricature can be a powerful force.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Modern day movies, television comedies, or skits on Youtube.com might portray public officials or public policies in unflattering ways. Yet if a covered transmission during the blackout period creates the background for candidate endorsement or opposition, a felony occurs solely because a corporation, other than an exempt media corporation, has made the \xe2\x80\x9cpurchase, payment, distribution, loan, advance, deposit, or gift of money or anything of value\xe2\x80\x9d in order to engage in political speech. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7431(9)(A)(i). Speech would be suppressed in the realm where its necessity is most evident: in the public dialogue preceding a real election. Governments are often hostile to speech, but under our law and our tradition it seems stranger than fiction for our Government to make this political speech a crime. Yet this is the statute\xe2\x80\x99s purpose and design.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Some members of the public might consider Hillary to be insightful and instructive; some might find it to be neither high art nor a fair discussion on how to set the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s course; still others simply might suspend judgment on these points but decide to think more about issues and candidates. Those choices and assessments, however, are not for the Government to make. \xe2\x80\x9cThe First Amendment underwrites the freedom to experiment and to create in the realm of thought and speech. Citizens must be free to use new forms, and new forums, for the expression of ideas. The civic discourse belongs to the people, and the Government may not prescribe the means used to conduct it.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, supra, at 341 (opinion of Kennedy, J.).\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The judgment of the District Court is reversed with respect to the constitutionality of 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s restrictions on corporate independent expenditures. The judgment is affirmed with respect to BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclaimer and disclosure requirements. The case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

\n

It is so ordered.

\n
\n
\n
\n
\n
\nROBERTS, C. J., CONCURRING
\nCITIZENS UNITED V. FEDERAL ELECTION COMM\'N
\n558 U. S. ____ (2010)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-205
\n
\n

CITIZENS UNITED, APPELLANT v. FEDERAL\nELECTION COMMISSION

\n

on appeal from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[January 21, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts, with whom Justice Alito joins, concurring.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Government urges us in this case to uphold a direct prohibition on political speech. It asks us to embrace a theory of the First Amendment that would allow censorship not only of television and radio broadcasts, but of pamphlets, posters, the Internet, and virtually any other medium that corporations and unions might find useful in expressing their views on matters of public concern. Its theory, if accepted, would empower the Government to prohibit newspapers from running editorials or opinion pieces supporting or opposing candidates for office, so long as the newspapers were owned by corporations\xe2\x80\x94as the major ones are. First Amendment rights could be confined to individuals, subverting the vibrant public discourse that is at the foundation of our democracy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court properly rejects that theory, and I join its opinion in full. The First Amendment protects more than just the individual on a soapbox and the lonely pamphleteer. I write separately to address the important principles of judicial restraint and stare decisis implicated in this case.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Judging the constitutionality of an Act of Congress is \xe2\x80\x9cthe gravest and most delicate duty that this Court is called upon to perform.\xe2\x80\x9d Blodgett v. Holden, 275 U. S. 142, 147\xe2\x80\x93148 (1927) (Holmes, J., concurring). Because the stakes are so high, our standard practice is to refrain from addressing constitutional questions except when necessary to rule on particular claims before us. See Ashwander v. TVA, 297 U. S. 288, 346\xe2\x80\x93348 (1936) (Brandeis, J., concurring). This policy underlies both our willingness to construe ambiguous statutes to avoid constitutional problems and our practice \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98never to formulate a rule of constitutional law broader than is required by the precise facts to which it is to be applied.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Raines, 362 U. S. 17, 21 (1960) (quoting Liverpool, New York & Philadelphia S.\xc2\xa0S. Co. v. Commissioners of Emigration, 113 U. S. 33, 39 (1885)).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority and dissent are united in expressing allegiance to these principles. Ante, at 12; post, at 14 (Stevens,\xc2\xa0J., concurring in part and dissenting in part). But I cannot agree with my dissenting colleagues on how these principles apply in this case.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s step-by-step analysis accords with our standard practice of avoiding broad constitutional questions except when necessary to decide the case before us. The majority begins by addressing\xe2\x80\x94and quite properly rejecting\xe2\x80\x94Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s statutory claim that 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b does not actually cover its production and distribution of Hillary: The Movie (hereinafter Hillary). If there were a valid basis for deciding this statutory claim in Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s favor (and thereby avoiding constitutional adjudication), it would be proper to do so. Indeed, that is precisely the approach the Court took just last Term in Northwest Austin Municipal Util. Dist. No. One v. Holder, 557 U. S. ___ (2009), when eight Members of the Court agreed to decide the case on statutory grounds instead of reaching the appellant\xe2\x80\x99s broader argument that the Voting Rights Act is unconstitutional.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is only because the majority rejects Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s statutory claim that it proceeds to consider the group\xe2\x80\x99s various constitutional arguments, beginning with its narrowest claim (that Hillary is not the functional equivalent of express advocacy) and proceeding to its broadest claim (that Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce, 494 U. S. 652 (1990) should be overruled). This is the same order of operations followed by the controlling opinion in Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc., 551 U. S. 449 (2007) (WRTL). There the appellant was able to prevail on its narrowest constitutional argument because its broadcast ads did not qualify as the functional equivalent of express advocacy; there was thus no need to go on to address the broader claim that McConnell v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 540 U. S. 93 (2003), should be overruled. WRTL, 551 U. S., at 482; id., at 482\xe2\x80\x93483 (Alito, J., concurring). This case is different\xe2\x80\x94not, as the dissent suggests, because the approach taken in WRTL has been deemed a \xe2\x80\x9cfailure,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 11, but because, in the absence of any valid narrower ground of decision, there is no way to avoid Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s broader constitutional argument.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The dissent advocates an approach to addressing Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s claims that I find quite perplexing. It presumably agrees with the majority that Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s narrower statutory and constitutional arguments lack merit\xe2\x80\x94otherwise its conclusion that the group should lose this case would make no sense. Despite agreeing that these narrower arguments fail, however, the dissent argues that the majority should nonetheless latch on to one of them in order to avoid reaching the broader constitutional question of whether Austin remains good law. It even suggests that the Court\xe2\x80\x99s failure to adopt one of these concededly meritless arguments is a sign that the majority is not \xe2\x80\x9cserious about judicial restraint.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 16.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This approach is based on a false premise: that our practice of avoiding unnecessary (and unnecessarily broad) constitutional holdings somehow trumps our obligation faithfully to interpret the law. It should go without saying, however, that we cannot embrace a narrow ground of decision simply because it is narrow; it must also be right. Thus while it is true that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]f it is not necessary to decide more, it is necessary not to decide more,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 14 (internal quotation marks omitted), sometimes it is necessary to decide more. There is a difference between judicial restraint and judicial abdication. When constitutional questions are \xe2\x80\x9cindispensably necessary\xe2\x80\x9d to resolving the case at hand, \xe2\x80\x9cthe court must meet and decide them.\xe2\x80\x9d Ex parte Randolph, 20 F.\xc2\xa0Cas. 242, 254 (No. 11, 558) (CC Va. 1833) (Marshall, C.\xc2\xa0J.).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because it is necessary to reach Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s broader argument that Austin should be overruled, the debate over whether to consider this claim on an as-applied or facial basis strikes me as largely beside the point. Citizens United has standing\xe2\x80\x94it is being injured by the Government\xe2\x80\x99s enforcement of the Act. Citizens United has a constitutional claim\xe2\x80\x94the Act violates the First Amendment, because it prohibits political speech. The Government has a defense\xe2\x80\x94the Act may be enforced, consistent with the First Amendment, against corporations. Whether the claim or the defense prevails is the question before us.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Given the nature of that claim and defense, it makes no difference of any substance whether this case is resolved by invalidating the statute on its face or only as applied to Citizens United. Even if considered in as-applied terms, a holding in this case that the Act may not be applied to Citizens United\xe2\x80\x94because corporations as well as individuals enjoy the pertinent First Amendment rights\xe2\x80\x94would mean that any other corporation raising the same challenge would also win. Likewise, a conclusion that the Act may be applied to Citizens United\xe2\x80\x94because it is constitutional to prohibit corporate political speech\xe2\x80\x94would similarly govern future cases. Regardless whether we label Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s claim a \xe2\x80\x9cfacial\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cas-applied\xe2\x80\x9d challenge, the consequences of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision are the same.[Footnote 1]

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The text and purpose of the First Amendment point in the same direction: Congress may not prohibit political speech, even if the speaker is a corporation or union. What makes this case difficult is the need to confront our prior decision in Austin.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This is the first case in which we have been asked to overrule Austin, and thus it is also the first in which we have had reason to consider how much weight to give stare decisis in assessing its continued validity. The dissent erroneously declares that the Court \xe2\x80\x9creaffirmed\xe2\x80\x9d Austin\xe2\x80\x99s holding in subsequent cases\xe2\x80\x94namely, Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. 146 (2003); McConnell; and WRTL. Post, at 48\xe2\x80\x9350. Not so. Not a single party in any of those cases asked us to overrule Austin, and as the dissent points out, post, at 4\xe2\x80\x936, the Court generally does not consider constitutional arguments that have not properly been raised. Austin\xe2\x80\x99s validity was therefore not directly at issue in the cases the dissent cites. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s unwillingness to overturn Austin in those cases cannot be understood as a reaffirmation of that decision.

\n

A

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Fidelity to precedent\xe2\x80\x94the policy of stare decisis\xe2\x80\x94is vital to the proper exercise of the judicial function. \xe2\x80\x9cStare decisis is the preferred course because it promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development of legal principles, fosters reliance on judicial decisions, and contributes to the actual and perceived integrity of the judicial process.\xe2\x80\x9d Payne v. Tennessee, 501 U. S. 808, 827 (1991). For these reasons, we have long recognized that departures from precedent are inappropriate in the absence of a \xe2\x80\x9cspecial justification.\xe2\x80\x9d Arizona v. Rumsey, 467 U. S. 203, 212 (1984).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the same time, stare decisis is neither an \xe2\x80\x9cinexorable command,\xe2\x80\x9d Lawrence v. Texas, 539 U. S. 558, 577 (2003), nor \xe2\x80\x9ca mechanical formula of adherence to the latest decision,\xe2\x80\x9d Helvering v. Hallock, 309 U. S. 106, 119 (1940), especially in constitutional cases, see United States v. Scott, 437 U. S. 82, 101 (1978). If it were, segregation would be legal, minimum wage laws would be unconstitutional, and the Government could wiretap ordinary criminal suspects without first obtaining warrants. See Plessy v. Ferguson, 163 U. S. 537 (1896), overruled by Brown v. Board of Education, 347 U. S. 483 (1954); Adkins v. Children\xe2\x80\x99s Hospital of D. C., 261 U. S. 525 (1923), overruled by West Coast Hotel Co. v. Parrish, 300 U. S. 379 (1937); Olmstead v. United States, 277 U. S. 438 (1928), overruled by Katz v. United States, 389 U. S. 347 (1967). As the dissent properly notes, none of us has viewed stare decisis in such absolute terms. Post, at 17; see also, e.g., Randall v. Sorrell, 548 U. S. 230, 274\xe2\x80\x93281 (2006) (Stevens, J., dissenting) (urging the Court to overrule its invalidation of limits on independent expenditures on political speech in Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1 (1976) (per curiam)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Stare decisis is instead a \xe2\x80\x9cprinciple of policy.\xe2\x80\x9d Helvering, supra, at 119. When considering whether to reexamine a prior erroneous holding, we must balance the importance of having constitutional questions decided against the importance of having them decided right. As Justice Jackson explained, this requires a \xe2\x80\x9csober appraisal of the disadvantages of the innovation as well as those of the questioned case, a weighing of practical effects of one against the other.\xe2\x80\x9d Jackson, Decisional Law and Stare Decisis, 30 A.\xc2\xa0B.\xc2\xa0A. J. 334 (1944).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In conducting this balancing, we must keep in mind that stare decisis is not an end in itself. It is instead \xe2\x80\x9cthe means by which we ensure that the law will not merely change erratically, but will develop in a principled and intelligible fashion.\xe2\x80\x9d Vasquez v. Hillery, 474 U. S. 254, 265 (1986). Its greatest purpose is to serve a constitutional ideal\xe2\x80\x94the rule of law. It follows that in the unusual circumstance when fidelity to any particular precedent does more to damage this constitutional ideal than to advance it, we must be more willing to depart from that precedent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, for example, if the precedent under consideration itself departed from the Court\xe2\x80\x99s jurisprudence, returning to the \xe2\x80\x9c \xe2\x80\x98intrinsically sounder\xe2\x80\x99 doctrine established in prior cases\xe2\x80\x9d may \xe2\x80\x9cbetter serv[e] the values of stare decisis than would following [the] more recently decided case inconsistent with the decisions that came before it.\xe2\x80\x9d Adarand Constructors, Inc. v. Pe\xc5\x84a, 515 U. S. 200, 231 (1995); see also Helvering, supra, at 119; Randall, supra, at 274 (Stevens, J., dissenting). Abrogating the errant precedent, rather than reaffirming or extending it, might better preserve the law\xe2\x80\x99s coherence and curtail the precedent\xe2\x80\x99s disruptive effects.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Likewise, if adherence to a precedent actually impedes the stable and orderly adjudication of future cases, its stare decisis effect is also diminished. This can happen in a number of circumstances, such as when the precedent\xe2\x80\x99s validity is so hotly contested that it cannot reliably function as a basis for decision in future cases, when its rationale threatens to upend our settled jurisprudence in related areas of law, and when the precedent\xe2\x80\x99s underlying reasoning has become so discredited that the Court cannot keep the precedent alive without jury-rigging new and different justifications to shore up the original mistake. See, e.g., Pearson v. Callahan, 555 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 10); Montejo v. Louisiana, 556 U. S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 13) (stare decisis does not control when adherence to the prior decision requires \xe2\x80\x9cfundamentally revising its theoretical basis\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

B

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These considerations weigh against retaining our decision in Austin. First, as the majority explains, that decision was an \xe2\x80\x9caberration\xe2\x80\x9d insofar as it departed from the robust protections we had granted political speech in our earlier cases. Ante, at 39; see also Buckley, supra; First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765 (1978). Austin undermined the careful line that Buckley drew to distinguish limits on contributions to candidates from limits on independent expenditures on speech.
