Buckley rejected the asserted government interest in regulating independent expenditures, concluding that \xe2\x80\x9crestrict[ing] the speech of some elements of our society in order to enhance the relative voice of others is wholly foreign to the First Amendment.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 48\xe2\x80\x9349; see also Bellotti, supra, at 790\xe2\x80\x93791; Citizens Against Rent Control/Coalition for Fair Housing v. Berkeley, 454 U. S. 290, 295 (1981). Austin, however, allowed the Government to prohibit these same expenditures out of concern for \xe2\x80\x9cthe corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth\xe2\x80\x9d in the marketplace of ideas. 494 U. S., at 660. Austin\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning was\xe2\x80\x94and remains\xe2\x80\x94inconsistent with Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s explicit repudiation of any government interest in \xe2\x80\x9cequalizing the relative ability of individuals and groups to influence the outcome of elections.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 48\xe2\x80\x9349.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin was also inconsistent with Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s clear rejection of the idea that \xe2\x80\x9cspeech that otherwise would be within the protection of the First Amendment loses that protection simply because its source is a corporation.\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 784. The dissent correctly points out that Bellotti involved a referendum rather than a candidate election, and that Bellotti itself noted this factual distinction, id., at 788, n. 26; post, at 52. But this distinction does not explain why corporations may be subject to prohibitions on speech in candidate elections when individuals may not.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, the validity of Austin\xe2\x80\x99s rationale\xe2\x80\x94itself adopted over two \xe2\x80\x9cspirited dissents,\xe2\x80\x9d Payne, 501 U. S., at 829\xe2\x80\x94has proved to be the consistent subject of dispute among Members of this Court ever since. See, e.g., WRTL, 551 U. S., at 483 (Scalia, J., joined by Kennedy and Thomas, JJ., concurring in part and concurring in judgment); McConnell, 540 U. S., at 247, 264, 286 (opinions of Scalia, Thomas, and Kennedy, JJ.); Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 163, 164 (opinions of Kennedy and Thomas, JJ.). The simple fact that one of our decisions remains controversial is, of course, insufficient to justify overruling it. But it does undermine the precedent\xe2\x80\x99s ability to contribute to the stable and orderly development of the law. In such circumstances, it is entirely appropriate for the Court\xe2\x80\x94which in this case is squarely asked to reconsider Austin\xe2\x80\x99s validity for the first time\xe2\x80\x94to address the matter with a greater willingness to consider new approaches capable of restoring our doctrine to sounder footing.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Third, the Austin decision is uniquely destabilizing because it threatens to subvert our Court\xe2\x80\x99s decisions even outside the particular context of corporate express advocacy. The First Amendment theory underlying Austin\xe2\x80\x99s holding is extraordinarily broad. Austin\xe2\x80\x99s logic would authorize government prohibition of political speech by a category of speakers in the name of equality\xe2\x80\x94a point that most scholars acknowledge (and many celebrate), but that the dissent denies. Compare, e.g., Garrett, New Voices in Politics: Justice Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s Jurisprudence on Law and Politics, 52 Howard L.\xc2\xa0J. 655, 669 (2009) (Austin \xe2\x80\x9chas been understood by most commentators to be an opinion driven by equality considerations, albeit disguised in the language of \xe2\x80\x98political corruption\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9d) with post, at 74 (Austin\xe2\x80\x99s rationale \xe2\x80\x9cis manifestly not just an \xe2\x80\x98equalizing\xe2\x80\x99 ideal in disguise\xe2\x80\x9d).[Footnote 2]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It should not be surprising, then, that Members of the Court have relied on Austin\xe2\x80\x99s expansive logic to justify greater incursions on the First Amendment, even outside the original context of corporate advocacy on behalf of candidates running for office. See, e.g., Davis v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 554 U. S. ___, ___ (2008) (slip op., at 7\xe2\x80\x938) (Stevens, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part) (relying on Austin and other cases to justify restrictions on campaign spending by individual candidates, explaining that \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no reason that their logic\xe2\x80\x94specifically, their concerns about the corrosive and distorting effects of wealth on our political process\xe2\x80\x94is not equally applicable in the context of individual wealth\xe2\x80\x9d); McConnell, supra, at 203\xe2\x80\x93209 (extending Austin beyond its original context to cover not only the \xe2\x80\x9cfunctional equivalent\xe2\x80\x9d of express advocacy by corporations, but also electioneering speech conducted by labor unions). The dissent in this case succumbs to the same temptation, suggesting that Austin justifies prohibiting corporate speech because such speech might unduly influence \xe2\x80\x9cthe market for legislation.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 82. The dissent reads Austin to permit restrictions on corporate speech based on nothing more than the fact that the corporate form may help individuals coordinate and present their views more effectively. Post, at 82. A speaker\xe2\x80\x99s ability to persuade, however, provides no basis for government regulation of free and open public debate on what the laws should be.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If taken seriously, Austin\xe2\x80\x99s logic would apply most directly to newspapers and other media corporations. They have a more profound impact on public discourse than most other speakers. These corporate entities are, for the time being, not subject to \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s otherwise generally applicable prohibitions on corporate political speech. But this is simply a matter of legislative grace. The fact that the law currently grants a favored position to media corporations is no reason to overlook the danger inherent in accepting a theory that would allow government restrictions on their political speech. See generally McConnell, supra, at 283\xe2\x80\x93286 (Thomas, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These readings of Austin do no more than carry that decision\xe2\x80\x99s reasoning to its logical endpoint. In doing so, they highlight the threat Austin poses to First Amendment rights generally, even outside its specific factual context of corporate express advocacy. Because Austin is so difficult to confine to its facts\xe2\x80\x94and because its logic threatens to undermine our First Amendment jurisprudence and the nature of public discourse more broadly\xe2\x80\x94the costs of giving it stare decisis effect are unusually high.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally and most importantly, the Government\xe2\x80\x99s own effort to defend Austin\xe2\x80\x94or, more accurately, to defend something that is not quite Austin\xe2\x80\x94underscores its weakness as a precedent of the Court. The Government concedes that Austin \xe2\x80\x9cis not the most lucid opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d yet asks us to reaffirm its holding. Tr. of Oral Arg. 62 (Sept. 9, 2009). But while invoking stare decisis to support this position, the Government never once even mentions the compelling interest that Austin relied upon in the first place: the need to diminish \xe2\x80\x9cthe corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth that are accumulated with the help of the corporate form and that have little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 660.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Instead of endorsing Austin on its own terms, the Government urges us to reaffirm Austin\xe2\x80\x99s specific holding on the basis of two new and potentially expansive interests\xe2\x80\x94the need to prevent actual or apparent quid pro quo corruption, and the need to protect corporate shareholders. See Supp. Brief for Appellee 8\xe2\x80\x9310, 12\xe2\x80\x9313. Those interests may or may not support the result in Austin, but they were plainly not part of the reasoning on which Austin relied.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To its credit, the Government forthrightly concedes that Austin did not embrace either of the new rationales it now urges upon us. See, e.g., Supp. Brief for Appellee 11 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe Court did not decide in Austin \xe2\x80\xa6 whether the compelling interest in preventing actual or apparent corruption provides a constitutionally sufficient justification for prohibiting the use of corporate treasury funds for independent electioneering\xe2\x80\x9d); Tr. of Oral Arg. 45 (Sept. 9, 2009) (\xe2\x80\x9cAustin did not articulate what we believe to be the strongest compelling interest\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 61 (\xe2\x80\x9c[The Court:] I take it we have never accepted your shareholder protection interest. This is a new argument. [The Government:] I think that that\xe2\x80\x99s fair\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 64 (\xe2\x80\x9c[The Court:] In other words, you are asking us to uphold Austin on the basis of two arguments, two principles, two compelling interests we have never accepted in [the context of limits on political expenditures]. [The Government:] [I]n this particular context, fair enough\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To be clear: The Court in Austin nowhere relied upon the only arguments the Government now raises to support that decision. In fact, the only opinion in Austin endorsing the Government\xe2\x80\x99s argument based on the threat of quid pro quo corruption was Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99s concurrence. 494 U. S., at 678. The Court itself did not do so, despite the fact that the concurrence highlighted the argument. Moreover, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s only discussion of shareholder protection in Austin appeared in a section of the opinion that sought merely to distinguish Austin\xe2\x80\x99s facts from those of Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc., 479 U. S. 238 (1986). Austin, supra, at 663. Nowhere did Austin suggest that the goal of protecting shareholders is itself a compelling interest authorizing restrictions on First Amendment rights.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0To the extent that the Government\xe2\x80\x99s case for reaffirming Austin depends on radically reconceptualizing its reasoning, that argument is at odds with itself. Stare decisis is a doctrine of preservation, not transformation. It counsels deference to past mistakes, but provides no justification for making new ones. There is therefore no basis for the Court to give precedential sway to reasoning that it has never accepted, simply because that reasoning happens to support a conclusion reached on different grounds that have since been abandoned or discredited.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Doing so would undermine the rule-of-law values that justify stare decisis in the first place. It would effectively license the Court to invent and adopt new principles of constitutional law solely for the purpose of rationalizing its past errors, without a proper analysis of whether those principles have merit on their own. This approach would allow the Court\xe2\x80\x99s past missteps to spawn future mistakes, undercutting the very rule-of-law values that stare decisis is designed to protect.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0None of this is to say that the Government is barred from making new arguments to support the outcome in Austin. On the contrary, it is free to do so. And of course the Court is free to accept them. But the Government\xe2\x80\x99s new arguments must stand or fall on their own; they are not entitled to receive the special deference we accord to precedent. They are, as grounds to support Austin, literally unprecedented. Moreover, to the extent the Government relies on new arguments\xe2\x80\x94and declines to defend Austin on its own terms\xe2\x80\x94we may reasonably infer that it lacks confidence in that decision\xe2\x80\x99s original justification.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Because continued adherence to Austin threatens to subvert the \xe2\x80\x9cprincipled and intelligible\xe2\x80\x9d development of our First Amendment jurisprudence, Vasquez, 474 U. S., at 265, I support the Court\xe2\x80\x99s determination to overrule that decision.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have had two rounds of briefing in this case, two oral arguments, and 54 amicus briefs to help us carry out our obligation to decide the necessary constitutional questions according to law. We have also had the benefit of a comprehensive dissent that has helped ensure that the Court has considered all the relevant issues. This careful consideration convinces me that Congress violates the First Amendment when it decrees that some speakers may not engage in political speech at election time, when it matters most.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0The dissent suggests that I am \xe2\x80\x9cmuch too quick\xe2\x80\x9d to reach this conclusion because I \xe2\x80\x9cignore\xe2\x80\x9d Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s narrower arguments. Post, at 13, n.\xc2\xa012. But in fact I do not ignore those arguments; on the contrary, I (and my colleagues in the majority) appropriately consider and reject them on their merits, before addressing Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s broader claims. Supra, at 2\xe2\x80\x933; ante, at 5\xe2\x80\x9312.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0See also, e.g., R. Hasen, The Supreme Court and Election Law: Judging Equality from Baker v. Carr to Bush v. Gore 114 (2003) (\xe2\x80\x9cAustin represents the first and only case [before McConnell] in which a majority of the Court accepted, in deed if not in word, the equality rationale as a permissible state interest\xe2\x80\x9d); Strauss, Corruption, Equality, and Campaign Finance Reform, 94 Colum. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1369, 1369, and n.\xc2\xa01 (1994) (noting that Austin\xe2\x80\x99s rationale was based on equalizing political speech); Ashdown, Controlling Campaign Spending and the \xe2\x80\x9cNew Corruption\xe2\x80\x9d: Waiting for the Court, 44 Vand. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 767, 781 (1991); Eule, Promoting Speaker Diversity: Austin and Metro Broadcasting, 1990 S. Ct. Rev. 105, 108\xe2\x80\x93111.

\n\n

\n
\n
\nSCALIA, J., CONCURRING
\nCITIZENS UNITED V. FEDERAL ELECTION COMM\'N
\n558 U. S. ____ (2010)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-205
\n
\n

CITIZENS UNITED, APPELLANT v. FEDERAL\nELECTION COMMISSION

\n

on appeal from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[January 21, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia, with whom Justice Alito joins, and with whom Justice Thomas joins in part, concurring.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I join the opinion of the Court.[Footnote 1]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I write separately to address Justice Stevens\xe2\x80\x99 discussion of \xe2\x80\x9cOriginal Understandings,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 34 (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part) (hereinafter referred to as the dissent). This section of the dissent purports to show that today\xe2\x80\x99s decision is not supported by the original understanding of the First Amendment. The dissent attempts this demonstration, however, in splendid isolation from the text of the First Amendment. It never shows why \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of speech\xe2\x80\x9d that was the right of Englishmen did not include the freedom to speak in association with other individuals, including association in the corporate form. To be sure, in 1791 (as now) corporations could pursue only the objectives set forth in their charters; but the dissent provides no evidence that their speech in the pursuit of those objectives could be censored.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Instead of taking this straightforward approach to determining the Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s meaning, the dissent embarks on a detailed exploration of the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 views about the \xe2\x80\x9crole of corporations in society.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 35. The Framers didn\xe2\x80\x99t like corporations, the dissent concludes, and therefore it follows (as night the day) that corporations had no rights of free speech. Of course the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 personal affection or disaffection for corporations is relevant only insofar as it can be thought to be reflected in the understood meaning of the text they enacted\xe2\x80\x94not, as the dissent suggests, as a freestanding substitute for that text. But the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s distortion of proper analysis is even worse than that. Though faced with a constitutional text that makes no distinction between types of speakers, the dissent feels no necessity to provide even an isolated statement from the founding era to the effect that corporations are not covered, but places the burden on petitioners to bring forward statements showing that they are (\xe2\x80\x9cthere is not a scintilla of evidence to support the notion that anyone believed [the First Amendment] would preclude regulatory distinctions based on the corporate form,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 34\xe2\x80\x9335).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Despite the corporation-hating quotations the dissent has dredged up, it is far from clear that by the end of the 18th century corporations were despised. If so, how came there to be so many of them? The dissent\xe2\x80\x99s statement that there were few business corporations during the eighteenth century\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9conly a few hundred during all of the 18th century\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94is misleading. Post, at 35, n.\xe2\x80\x8253. There were approximately 335 charters issued to business corporations in the United States by the end of the 18th century.[Footnote 2] See 2 J.\xe2\x80\x82Davis, Essays in the Earlier History of American Corporations 24 (1917) (reprint 2006) (hereinafter Davis). This was a \xe2\x80\x9cconsiderable extension of corporate enterprise in the field of business,\xe2\x80\x9d Davis 8, and represented \xe2\x80\x9cunprecedented growth,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 309. Moreover, what seems like a small number by today\xe2\x80\x99s standards surely does not indicate the relative importance of corporations when the Nation was considerably smaller. As I have previously noted, \xe2\x80\x9c[b]y the end of the eighteenth century the corporation was a familiar figure in American economic life.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 540 U. S. 93, 256 (2003) (Scalia, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part) (quoting C. Cooke, Corporation Trust and Company 92 (1951) (hereinafter Cooke)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Even if we thought it proper to apply the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s approach of excluding from First Amendment coverage what the Founders disliked, and even if we agreed that the Founders disliked founding-era corporations; modern corporations might not qualify for exclusion. Most of the Founders\xe2\x80\x99 resentment towards corporations was directed at the state-granted monopoly privileges that individually chartered corporations enjoyed.[Footnote 3] Modern corporations do not have such privileges, and would probably have been favored by most of our enterprising Founders\xe2\x80\x94excluding, perhaps, Thomas Jefferson and others favoring perpetuation of an agrarian society. Moreover, if the Founders\xe2\x80\x99 specific intent with respect to corporations is what matters, why does the dissent ignore the Founders\xe2\x80\x99 views about other legal entities that have more in common with modern business corporations than the founding-era corporations? At the time of the founding, religious, educational, and literary corporations were incorporated under general incorporation statutes, much as business corporations are today.[Footnote 4] See Davis 16\xe2\x80\x9317; R. Seavoy, Origins of the American Business Corporation, 1784\xe2\x80\x931855, p.\xc2\xa05 (1982); Cooke 94. There were also small unincorporated business associations, which some have argued were the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98true progenitors\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d of today\xe2\x80\x99s business corporations. Friedman 200 (quoting S. Livermore, Early American Land Companies: Their Influence on Corporate Development 216 (1939)); see also Davis 33. Were all of these silently excluded from the protections of the First Amendment?

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The lack of a textual exception for speech by corporations cannot be explained on the ground that such organizations did not exist or did not speak. To the contrary, colleges, towns and cities, religious institutions, and guilds had long been organized as corporations at common law and under the King\xe2\x80\x99s charter, see 1 W. Blackstone, Commentaries on the Laws of England 455\xe2\x80\x93473 (1765); 1 S. Kyd, A Treatise on the Law of Corporations 1\xe2\x80\x9332, 63 (1793) (reprinted 2006), and as I have discussed, the practice of incorporation only expanded in the United States. Both corporations and voluntary associations actively petitioned the Government and expressed their views in newspapers and pamphlets. For example: An antislavery Quaker corporation petitioned the First Congress, distributed pamphlets, and communicated through the press in 1790. W. diGiacomantonio, \xe2\x80\x9cFor the Gratification of a Volunteering Society\xe2\x80\x9d: Antislavery and Pressure Group Politics in the First Federal Congress, 15 J. Early Republic 169 (1995). The New York Sons of Liberty sent a circular to colonies farther south in 1766. P. Maier, From Resistance to Revolution 79\xe2\x80\x9380 (1972). And the Society for the Relief and Instruction of Poor Germans circulated a biweekly paper from 1755 to 1757. Adams, The Colonial German-language Press and the American Revolution, in The Press & the American Revolution 151, 161\xe2\x80\x93162 (B. Bailyn & J. Hench eds. 1980). The dissent offers no evidence\xe2\x80\x94none whatever\xe2\x80\x94that the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s unqualified text was originally understood to exclude such associational speech from its protection.[Footnote 5]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Historical evidence relating to the textually similar clause \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of . . . the press\xe2\x80\x9d also provides no support for the proposition that the First Amendment excludes conduct of artificial legal entities from the scope of its protection. The freedom of \xe2\x80\x9cthe press\xe2\x80\x9d was widely understood to protect the publishing activities of individual editors and printers. See McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334, 360 (1995) (Thomas, J., concurring in judgment); see also McConnell, 540 U. S., at 252\xe2\x80\x93253 (opinion of Scalia, J.). But these individuals often acted through newspapers, which (much like corporations) had their own names, outlived the individuals who had founded them, could be bought and sold, were sometimes owned by more than one person, and were operated for profit. See generally F. Mott, American Journalism: A History of Newspapers in the United States Through 250 Years 3\xe2\x80\x93164 (1941); J. Smith, Freedom\xe2\x80\x99s Fetters (1956). Their activities were not stripped of First Amendment protection simply because they were carried out under the banner of an artificial legal entity. And the notion which follows from the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s view, that modern newspapers, since they are incorporated, have free-speech rights only at the sufferance of Congress, boggles the mind.[Footnote 6]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In passing, the dissent also claims that the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conception of corruption is unhistorical. The Framers \xe2\x80\x9cwould have been appalled,\xe2\x80\x9d it says, by the evidence of corruption in the congressional findings supporting the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002. Post, at 61. For this proposition, the dissent cites a law review article arguing that \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption\xe2\x80\x9d was originally understood to include \xe2\x80\x9cmoral decay\xe2\x80\x9d and even actions taken by citizens in pursuit of private rather than public ends. Teachout, The Anti-Corruption Principle, 94 Cornell L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 341, 373, 378 (2009). It is hard to see how this has anything to do with what sort of corruption can be combated by restrictions on political speech. Moreover, if speech can be prohibited because, in the view of the Government, it leads to \xe2\x80\x9cmoral decay\xe2\x80\x9d or does not serve \xe2\x80\x9cpublic ends,\xe2\x80\x9d then there is no limit to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s censorship power.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The dissent says that when the Framers \xe2\x80\x9cconstitutionalized the right to free speech in the First Amendment, it was the free speech of individual Americans that they had in mind.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 37. That is no doubt true. All the provisions of the Bill of Rights set forth the rights of individual men and women\xe2\x80\x94not, for example, of trees or polar bears. But the individual person\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak includes the right to speak in association with other individual persons. Surely the dissent does not believe that speech by the Republican Party or the Democratic Party can be censored because it is not the speech of \xe2\x80\x9can individual American.\xe2\x80\x9d It is the speech of many individual Americans, who have associated in a common cause, giving the leadership of the party the right to speak on their behalf. The association of individuals in a business corporation is no different\xe2\x80\x94or at least it cannot be denied the right to speak on the simplistic ground that it is not \xe2\x80\x9can individual American.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 7]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0But to return to, and summarize, my principal point, which is the conformity of today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion with the original meaning of the First Amendment. The Amendment is written in terms of \xe2\x80\x9cspeech,\xe2\x80\x9d not speakers. Its text offers no foothold for excluding any category of speaker, from single individuals to partnerships of individuals, to unincorporated associations of individuals, to incorporated associations of individuals\xe2\x80\x94and the dissent offers no evidence about the original meaning of the text to support any such exclusion. We are therefore simply left with the question whether the speech at issue in this case is \xe2\x80\x9cspeech\xe2\x80\x9d covered by the First Amendment. No one says otherwise. A documentary film critical of a potential Presidential candidate is core political speech, and its nature as such does not change simply because it was funded by a corporation. Nor does the character of that funding produce any reduction whatever in the \xe2\x80\x9cinherent worth of the speech\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cits capacity for informing the public,\xe2\x80\x9d First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, 777 (1978). Indeed, to exclude or impede corporate speech is to muzzle the principal agents of the modern free economy. We should celebrate rather than condemn the addition of this speech to the public debate.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas does not join Part IV of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0The dissent protests that 1791 rather than 1800 should be the relevant date, and that \xe2\x80\x9c[m]ore than half of the century\xe2\x80\x99s total business charters were issued between 1796 and 1800.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 35, n. 53. I used 1800 only because the dissent did. But in any case, it is surely fanciful to think that a consensus of hostility towards corporations was transformed into general favor at some magical moment between 1791 and 1796.

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c[P]eople in 1800 identified corporations with franchised monopolies.\xe2\x80\x9d L.\xe2\x80\x82Friedman, A History of American Law 194 (2d ed. 1985) (hereinafter Friedman). \xe2\x80\x9cThe chief cause for the changed popular attitude towards business corporations that marked the opening of the nineteenth century was the elimination of their inherent monopolistic character. This was accomplished primarily by an extension of the principle of free incorporation under general laws.\xe2\x80\x9d 1 W. Fletcher, Cyclopedia of the Law of Corporations \xc2\xa72, p.\xc2\xa08 (rev. ed. 2006).

\nFootnote 4

\xc2\xa0At times (though not always) the dissent seems to exclude such non-\xe2\x80\x9cbusiness corporations\xe2\x80\x9d from its denial of free speech rights. See post, at 37. Finding in a seemingly categorical text a distinction between the rights of business corporations and the rights of non-business corporations is even more imaginative than finding a distinction between the rights of all corporations and the rights of other associations.

\nFootnote 5

\xc2\xa0The best the dissent can come up with is that \xe2\x80\x9c[p]ostratification practice\xe2\x80\x9d supports its reading of the First Amendment. Post, at 40, n.\xc2\xa056. For this proposition, the dissent cites Justice White\xe2\x80\x99s statement (in dissent) that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he common law was generally interpreted as prohibiting corporate political participation,\xe2\x80\x9d First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, 819 (1978). The sole authority Justice White cited for this proposition, id., at 819, n.\xc2\xa014, was a law-review note that made no such claim. To the contrary, it stated that the cases dealing with the propriety of corporate political expenditures were \xe2\x80\x9cfew.\xe2\x80\x9d Note, Corporate Political Affairs Programs, 70 Yale L.\xc2\xa0J. 821, 852 (1961). More specifically, the note cites only two holdings to that effect, one by a Federal District Court, and one by the Supreme Court of Montana. Id., at 852, n. 197. Of course even if the common law was \xe2\x80\x9cgenerally interpreted\xe2\x80\x9d to prohibit corporate political expenditures as ultra vires, that would have nothing to do with whether political expenditures that were authorized by a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s charter could constitutionally be suppressed.

\n

\n\nAs additional \xe2\x80\x9c[p]ostratification practice,\xe2\x80\x9d the dissent notes that the Court \xe2\x80\x9cdid not recognize any First Amendment protections for corporations until the middle part of the 20th century.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 40, n.\xc2\xa056. But it did that in Grosjean v. American Press Co., 297 U. S. 233 (1936), a case involving freedom of the press\xe2\x80\x94which the dissent acknowledges did cover corporations from the outset. The relative recency of that first case is unsurprising. All of our First Amendment jurisprudence was slow to develop. We did not consider application of the First Amendment to speech restrictions other than prior restraints until 1919, see Schenck v. United States, 249 U. S. 47 (1919); we did not invalidate a state law on First Amendment grounds until 1931, see Stromberg v. California, 283 U. S. 359 (1931), and a federal law until 1965, see Lamont v. Postmaster General, 381 U. S. 301 (1965).

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0The dissent seeks to avoid this conclusion (and to turn a liability into an asset) by interpreting the Freedom of the Press Clause to refer to the institutional press (thus demonstrating, according to the dissent, that the Founders \xe2\x80\x9cdid draw distinctions\xe2\x80\x94explicit distinctions\xe2\x80\x94between types of \xe2\x80\x98speakers,\xe2\x80\x99 or speech outlets or forms\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d). Post, at 40 and n. 57. It is passing strange to interpret the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of speech, or of the press\xe2\x80\x9d to mean, not everyone\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak or publish, but rather everyone\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak or the institutional press\xe2\x80\x99s right to publish. No one thought that is what it meant. Patriot Noah Webster\xe2\x80\x99s 1828 dictionary contains, under the word \xe2\x80\x9cpress,\xe2\x80\x9d the following entry:

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cLiberty of the press, in civil policy, is the free right of publishing books, pamphlets, or papers without previous restraint; or the unrestrained right which every citizen enjoys of publishing his thoughts and opinions, subject only to punishment for publishing what is pernicious to morals or to the peace of the state.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 American Dictionary of the English Language (1828) (reprinted 1970).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion describes, ante, at 36, our jurisprudence agrees with Noah Webster and contradicts the dissent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe liberty of the press is not confined to newspapers and periodicals. It necessarily embraces pamphlets and leaflets. . . . The press in its historical connotation comprehends every sort of publication which affords a vehicle of information and opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d Lovell v. City of Griffin, 303 U. S. 444, 452 (1938).

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0The dissent says that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98speech\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d refers to oral communications of human beings, and since corporations are not human beings they cannot speak. Post, at 37, n. 55. This is sophistry. The authorized spokesman of a corporation is a human being, who speaks on behalf of the human beings who have formed that association\xe2\x80\x94just as the spokesman of an unincorporated association speaks on behalf of its members. The power to publish thoughts, no less than the power to speak thoughts, belongs only to human beings, but the dissent sees no problem with a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s enjoying the freedom of the press.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The same footnote asserts that \xe2\x80\x9cit has been \xe2\x80\x98claimed that the notion of institutional speech . . . did not exist in post-revolutionary America.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d This is quoted from a law-review article by a Bigelow Fellow at the University of Chicago (Fagundes, State Actors as First Amendment Speakers, 100 Nw. U. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1637, 1654 (2006)), which offers as the sole support for its statement a treatise dealing with government speech, M. Yudof, When Government Speaks 42\xe2\x80\x9350 (1983). The cited pages of that treatise provide no support whatever for the statement\xe2\x80\x94unless, as seems overwhelmingly likely, the \xe2\x80\x9cinstitutional speech\xe2\x80\x9d referred to was speech by the subject of the law-review article, governmental institutions.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The other authority cited in the footnote, a law-review article by a professor at Washington and Lee Law School, Bezanson, Institutional Speech, 80 Iowa L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 735, 775 (1995), in fact contradicts the dissent, in that it would accord free-speech protection to associations.

\n\n
\n
\n
\n
\n
\nOPINION OF STEVENS, J.
\nCITIZENS UNITED V. FEDERAL ELECTION COMM\'N
\n558 U. S. ____ (2010)
\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-205
\n
\n

CITIZENS UNITED, APPELLANT v. FEDERAL\nELECTION COMMISSION

\n

on appeal from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[January 21, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Stevens, with whom Justice Ginsburg, Justice Breyer, and Justice Sotomayor join, concurring in part and dissenting in part.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The real issue in this case concerns how, not if, the appellant may finance its electioneering. Citizens United is a wealthy nonprofit corporation that runs a political action committee (PAC) with millions of dollars in assets. Under the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA), it could have used those assets to televise and promote Hillary: The Movie wherever and whenever it wanted to. It also could have spent unrestricted sums to broadcast Hillary at any time other than the 30 days before the last primary election. Neither Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s nor any other corporation\xe2\x80\x99s speech has been \xe2\x80\x9cbanned,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1. All that the parties dispute is whether Citizens United had a right to use the funds in its general treasury to pay for broadcasts during the 30-day period. The notion that the First Amendment dictates an affirmative answer to that question is, in my judgment, profoundly misguided. Even more misguided is the notion that the Court must rewrite the law relating to campaign expenditures by for-profit corporations and unions to decide this case.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The basic premise underlying the Court\xe2\x80\x99s ruling is its iteration, and constant reiteration, of the proposition that the First Amendment bars regulatory distinctions based on a speaker\xe2\x80\x99s identity, including its \xe2\x80\x9cidentity\xe2\x80\x9d as a corporation. While that glittering generality has rhetorical appeal, it is not a correct statement of the law. Nor does it tell us when a corporation may engage in electioneering that some of its shareholders oppose. It does not even resolve the specific question whether Citizens United may be required to finance some of its messages with the money in its PAC. The conceit that corporations must be treated identically to natural persons in the political sphere is not only inaccurate but also inadequate to justify the Court\xe2\x80\x99s disposition of this case.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In the context of election to public office, the distinction between corporate and human speakers is significant. Although they make enormous contributions to our society, corporations are not actually members of it. They cannot vote or run for office. Because they may be managed and controlled by nonresidents, their interests may conflict in fundamental respects with the interests of eligible voters. The financial resources, legal structure, and instrumental orientation of corporations raise legitimate concerns about their role in the electoral process. Our lawmakers have a compelling constitutional basis, if not also a democratic duty, to take measures designed to guard against the potentially deleterious effects of corporate spending in local and national races.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s approach to corporate electioneering marks a dramatic break from our past. Congress has placed special limitations on campaign spending by corporations ever since the passage of the Tillman Act in 1907, ch. 420, 34 Stat. 864. We have unanimously concluded that this \xe2\x80\x9creflects a permissible assessment of the dangers posed by those entities to the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x9d FEC v. National Right to Work Comm., 459 U. S. 197, 209 (1982) (NRWC), and have accepted the \xe2\x80\x9clegislative judgment that the special characteristics of the corporate structure require particularly careful regulation,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210. The Court today rejects a century of history when it treats the distinction between corporate and individual campaign spending as an invidious novelty born of Austin v. Michigan Chamber of Commerce, 494 U. S. 652 (1990). Relying largely on individual dissenting opinions, the majority blazes through our precedents, overruling or disavowing a body of case law including FEC v. Wisconsin Right to Life, Inc., 551 U. S. 449 (2007) (WRTL), McConnell v. FEC, 540 U. S. 93 (2003), FEC v. Beaumont, 539 U. S. 146 (2003), FEC v. Massachusetts Citizens for Life, Inc., 479 U. S. 238 (1986) (MCFL), NRWC, 459 U. S. 197, and California Medical Assn. v. FEC, 453 U. S. 182 (1981).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In his landmark concurrence in Ashwander v. TVA, 297 U. S. 288, 346 (1936), Justice Brandeis stressed the importance of adhering to rules the Court has \xe2\x80\x9cdeveloped \xe2\x80\xa6 for its own governance\xe2\x80\x9d when deciding constitutional questions. Because departures from those rules always enhance the risk of error, I shall review the background of this case in some detail before explaining why the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis rests on a faulty understanding of Austin and McConnell and of our campaign finance jurisprudence more generally\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0.[Footnote 1] I regret the length of what follows, but the importance and novelty of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion require a full response. Although I concur in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision to sustain BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure provisions and join Part IV\nof its opinion, I emphatically dissent from its principal holding.

\n

I

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s ruling threatens to undermine the integrity of elected institutions across the Nation. The path it has taken to reach its outcome will, I fear, do damage to this institution. Before turning to the question whether to overrule Austin and part of McConnell, it is important\nto explain why the Court should not be deciding that question.

\n

Scope of the Case

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The first reason is that the question was not properly brought before us. In declaring \xc2\xa7203 of BCRA facially unconstitutional on the ground that corporations\xe2\x80\x99 electoral expenditures may not be regulated any more stringently than those of individuals, the majority decides this case on a basis relinquished below, not included in the questions presented to us by the litigants, and argued here only in response to the Court\xe2\x80\x99s invitation. This procedure is unusual and inadvisable for a court.[Footnote 2] Our colleagues\xe2\x80\x99 suggestion that \xe2\x80\x9cwe are asked to reconsider Austin and, in effect, McConnell,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1, would be more accurate if rephrased to state that \xe2\x80\x9cwe have asked ourselves\xe2\x80\x9d to reconsider those cases.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In the District Court, Citizens United initially raised a facial challenge to the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7203. App. 23a\xe2\x80\x9324a. In its motion for summary judgment, however, Citizens United expressly abandoned its facial challenge, 1:07\xe2\x80\x93cv\xe2\x80\x932240\xe2\x80\x93RCL\xe2\x80\x93RWR, Docket Entry No. 52, pp. 1\xe2\x80\x932 (May 16, 2008), and the parties stipulated to the dismissal of that claim, id., Nos. 53 (May 22, 2008), 54 (May 23, 2008), App. 6a. The District Court therefore resolved the case on alternative grounds,[Footnote 3] and in its jurisdictional statement to this Court, Citizens United properly advised us that it was raising only \xe2\x80\x9can as-applied challenge to the constitutionality of \xe2\x80\xa6 BCRA \xc2\xa7203.\xe2\x80\x9d Juris. Statement 5. The jurisdictional statement never so much as cited Austin, the key case the majority today overrules. And not one of the questions presented suggested that Citizens United was surreptitiously raising the facial challenge to \xc2\xa7203 that it previously agreed to dismiss. In fact, not one of those questions raised an issue based on Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s corporate status. Juris. Statement (i). Moreover, even in its merits briefing, when Citizens United injected its request to overrule Austin, it never sought a declaration that \xc2\xa7203 was facially unconstitutional as to all corporations and unions; instead it argued only that the statute could not be applied to it because it was \xe2\x80\x9cfunded overwhelmingly by individuals.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellant 29; see also id., at 10, 12, 16, 28 (affirming \xe2\x80\x9cas applied\xe2\x80\x9d character of challenge to \xc2\xa7203); Tr. of Oral Arg. 4\xe2\x80\x939 (Mar. 24, 2009) (counsel for Citizens United conceding that \xc2\xa7203 could be applied to General Motors); id., at 55 (counsel for Citizens United stating that \xe2\x80\x9cwe accept the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision in Wisconsin Right to Life\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98It is only in exceptional cases coming here from the federal courts that questions not pressed or passed upon below are reviewed,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Youakim v. Miller, 425 U. S. 231, 234 (1976) (per curiam) (quoting Duignan v. United States, 274 U. S. 195, 200 (1927)), and it is \xe2\x80\x9conly in the most exceptional cases\xe2\x80\x9d that we will consider issues outside the questions presented, Stone v. Powell, 428 U. S. 465, 481, n. 15 (1976). The appellant in this case did not so much as assert an exceptional circumstance, and one searches the majority opinion in vain for the mention of any. That is unsurprising, for none exists.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Setting the case for reargument was a constructive step, but it did not cure this fundamental problem. Essentially, five Justices were unhappy with the limited nature of the case before us, so they changed the case to give themselves an opportunity to change the law.

\n

As-Applied and Facial Challenges

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This Court has repeatedly emphasized in recent years that \xe2\x80\x9c[f]acial challenges are disfavored.\xe2\x80\x9d Washington State Grange v. Washington State Republican Party, 552 U. S. 442, 450 (2008); see also Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., 546 U. S. 320, 329 (2006) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he \xe2\x80\x98normal rule\xe2\x80\x99 is that \xe2\x80\x98partial, rather than facial, invalidation is the required course,\xe2\x80\x99 such that a \xe2\x80\x98statute may \xe2\x80\xa6 be declared invalid to the extent that it reaches too far, but otherwise left intact\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Brockett v. Spokane Arcades, Inc., 472 U. S. 491, 504 (1985); alteration in original)). By declaring \xc2\xa7203 facially unconstitutional, our colleagues have turned an as-applied challenge into a facial challenge, in defiance of this principle.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This is not merely a technical defect in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision. The unnecessary resort to a facial inquiry \xe2\x80\x9crun[s] contrary to the fundamental principle of judicial restraint that courts should neither anticipate a question of constitutional law in advance of the necessity of deciding it nor formulate a rule of constitutional law broader than is required by the precise facts to which it is to be applied.\xe2\x80\x9d Washington State Grange, 552 U. S., at 450 (internal quotation marks omitted). Scanting that principle \xe2\x80\x9cthreaten[s] to short circuit the democratic process by preventing laws embodying the will of the people from being implemented in a manner consistent with the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 451. These concerns are heightened when judges overrule settled doctrine upon which the legislature has relied. The Court operates with a sledge hammer rather than a scalpel when it strikes down one of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 most significant efforts to regulate the role that corporations and unions play in electoral politics. It compounds the offense by implicitly striking down a great many state laws as well.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The problem goes still deeper, for the Court does all of this on the basis of pure speculation. Had Citizens United maintained a facial challenge, and thus argued that there are virtually no circumstances in which BCRA \xc2\xa7203 can be applied constitutionally, the parties could have developed, through the normal process of litigation, a record about the actual effects of \xc2\xa7203, its actual burdens and its actual benefits, on all manner of corporations and unions.[Footnote 4] \xe2\x80\x9cClaims of facial invalidity often rest on speculation,\xe2\x80\x9d and consequently \xe2\x80\x9craise the risk of premature interpretation of statutes on the basis of factually barebones records.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 450 (internal quotation marks omitted). In this case, the record is not simply incomplete or unsatisfactory; it is nonexistent. Congress crafted BCRA in response to a virtual mountain of research on the corruption that previous legislation had failed to avert. The Court now negates Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts without a shred of evidence on how \xc2\xa7203 or its state-law counterparts have been affecting any entity other than Citizens United.[Footnote 5]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Faced with this gaping empirical hole, the majority throws up its hands. Were we to confine our inquiry to Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s as-applied challenge, it protests, we would commence an \xe2\x80\x9cextended\xe2\x80\x9d process of \xe2\x80\x9cdraw[ing], and then redraw[ing], constitutional lines based on the particular media or technology used to disseminate political speech from a particular speaker.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 9. While tacitly acknowledging that some applications of \xc2\xa7203 might be found constitutional, the majority thus posits a future in which novel First Amendment standards must be devised on an ad\xc2\xa0hoc basis, and then leaps from this unfounded prediction to the unfounded conclusion that such complexity counsels the abandonment of all normal restraint. Yet it is a pervasive feature of regulatory systems that unanticipated events, such as new technologies, may raise some unanticipated difficulties at the margins. The fluid nature of electioneering communications does not make this case special. The fact that a Court can hypothesize situations in which a statute might, at some point down the line, pose some unforeseen as-applied problems, does not come close to meeting the standard for a facial challenge.[Footnote 6]

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority proposes several other justifications for the sweep of its ruling. It suggests that a facial ruling is necessary because, if the Court were to continue on its normal course of resolving as-applied challenges as they present themselves, that process would itself run afoul of the First Amendment. See, e.g., ante, at 9 (as-applied review process \xe2\x80\x9cwould raise questions as to the courts\xe2\x80\x99 own lawful authority\xe2\x80\x9d); ibid. (\xe2\x80\x9cCourts, too, are bound by the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x9d). This suggestion is perplexing. Our colleagues elsewhere trumpet \xe2\x80\x9cour duty \xe2\x80\x98to say what the law is,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d even when our predecessors on the bench and our counterparts in Congress have interpreted the law differently. Ante, at 49 (quoting Marbury v. Madison, 1 Cranch 137, 177 (1803)). We do not typically say what the law is not as a hedge against future judicial error. The possibility that later courts will misapply a constitutional provision does not give us a basis for pretermitting litigation relating to that provision.[Footnote 7]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority suggests that a facial ruling is necessary because anything less would chill too much protected speech. See ante, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310, 12, 16\xe2\x80\x9320. In addition to begging the question what types of corporate spending are constitutionally protected and to what extent, this claim rests on the assertion that some significant number of corporations have been cowed into quiescence by FEC \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98censor[ship].\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 18\xe2\x80\x9319. That assertion is unsubstantiated, and it is hard to square with practical experience. It is particularly hard to square with the legal landscape following WRTL, which held that a corporate communication could be regulated under \xc2\xa7203 only if it was \xe2\x80\x9csusceptible of no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 551 U. S., at 470 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (emphasis added). The whole point of this test was to make \xc2\xa7203 as simple and speech-protective as possible. The Court does not explain how, in the span of a single election cycle, it has determined The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s project to be a failure. In this respect, too, the majority\xe2\x80\x99s critique of line-drawing collapses into a critique of the as-applied review method generally.[Footnote 8]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority suggests that, even though it expressly dismissed its facial challenge, Citizens United nevertheless preserved it\xe2\x80\x94not as a freestanding \xe2\x80\x9cclaim,\xe2\x80\x9d but as a potential argument in support of \xe2\x80\x9ca claim that the FEC has violated its First Amendment right to free speech.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 13; see also ante, at 4 (Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., concurring) (describing Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s claim as: \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Act violates the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x9d). By this novel logic, virtually any submission could be reconceptualized as \xe2\x80\x9ca claim that the Government has violated my rights,\xe2\x80\x9d and it would then be available to the Court to entertain any conceivable issue that might be relevant to that claim\xe2\x80\x99s disposition. Not only the as-applied/facial distinction, but the basic relationship between litigants and courts, would be upended if the latter had free rein to construe the former\xe2\x80\x99s claims at such high levels of generality. There would be no need for plaintiffs to argue their case; they could just cite the constitutional provisions they think relevant, and leave the rest to us.[Footnote 9]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, the majority suggests that though the scope of Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s claim may be narrow, a facial ruling is necessary as a matter of remedy. Relying on a law review article, it asserts that Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s dismissal of the facial challenge does not prevent us \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98from making broader pronouncements of invalidity in properly \xe2\x80\x9cas-applied\xe2\x80\x9d cases.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 14 (quoting Fallon, As-Applied and Facial Challenges and Third-Party Standing, 113 Harv. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1321, 1339 (2000) (hereinafter Fallon)); accord, ante, at 5 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cRegardless whether we label Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s claim a \xe2\x80\x98facial\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98as-applied\xe2\x80\x99 challenge, the consequences of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision are the same\xe2\x80\x9d). The majority is on firmer conceptual ground here. Yet even if one accepts this part of Professor Fallon\xe2\x80\x99s thesis, one must proceed to ask which as-applied challenges, if successful, will \xe2\x80\x9cproperly\xe2\x80\x9d invite or entail invalidation of the underlying statute.[Footnote 10] The paradigmatic case is a judicial determination that the legislature acted with an impermissible purpose in enacting a provision, as this carries the necessary implication that all future as-applied challenges to the provision must prevail. See Fallon 1339\xe2\x80\x931340.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s as-applied challenge was not of this sort. Until this Court ordered reargument, its contention was that BCRA \xc2\xa7203 could not lawfully be applied to a feature-length video-on-demand film (such as Hillary) or to a nonprofit corporation exempt from taxation under 26 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7501(c)(4)[Footnote 11] and funded overwhelmingly by individuals (such as itself). See Brief for Appellant 16\xe2\x80\x9341. Success on either of these claims would not necessarily carry any implications for the validity of \xc2\xa7203 as applied to other types of broadcasts, other types of corporations, or unions. It certainly would not invalidate the statute as applied to a large for-profit corporation. See Tr. of Oral Arg. 8, 4 (Mar. 24, 2009) (counsel for Citizens United emphasizing that appellant is \xe2\x80\x9ca small, nonprofit organization, which is very much like [an MCFL corporation],\xe2\x80\x9d and affirming that its argument \xe2\x80\x9cdefinitely would not be the same\xe2\x80\x9d if Hillary were distributed by General Motors).[Footnote 12] There is no legitimate basis for resurrecting a facial challenge that dropped out of this case 20 months ago.

\n

Narrower Grounds

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is all the more distressing that our colleagues have manufactured a facial challenge, because the parties have advanced numerous ways to resolve the case that would facilitate electioneering by nonprofit advocacy corporations such as Citizens United, without toppling statutes and precedents. Which is to say, the majority has transgressed yet another \xe2\x80\x9ccardinal\xe2\x80\x9d principle of the judicial process: \xe2\x80\x9c[I]f it is not necessary to decide more, it is necessary not to decide more,\xe2\x80\x9d PDK Labs., Inc. v. Drug Enforcement Admin., 362 F.\xc2\xa03d 786, 799 (CADC 2004) (Roberts, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Consider just three of the narrower grounds of decision that the majority has bypassed. First, the Court could have ruled, on statutory grounds, that a feature-length film distributed through video-on-demand does not qualify as an \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communication\xe2\x80\x9d under \xc2\xa7203 of BCRA, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b. BCRA defines that term to encompass certain communications transmitted by \xe2\x80\x9cbroadcast, cable, or satellite.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(A). When Congress was developing BCRA, the video-on-demand medium was still in its infancy, and legislators were focused on a very different sort of programming: short advertisements run on television or radio. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 207. The sponsors of BCRA acknowledge that the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s implementing regulations do not clearly apply to video-on-demand transmissions. See Brief for Senator John McCain et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 17\xe2\x80\x9319. In light of this ambiguity, the distinctive characteristics of video-on-demand, and \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he elementary rule \xe2\x80\xa6 that every reasonable construction must be resorted to, in order to save a statute from unconstitutionality,\xe2\x80\x9d Hooper v. California, 155 U. S. 648, 657 (1895), the Court could have reasonably ruled that \xc2\xa7203 does not apply to Hillary.[Footnote 13]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Second, the Court could have expanded the MCFL exemption to cover \xc2\xa7501(c)(4) nonprofits that accept only a de minimis amount of money from for-profit corporations. Citizens United professes to be such a group: Its brief says it \xe2\x80\x9cis funded predominantly by donations from individuals who support [its] ideological message.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellant 5. Numerous Courts of Appeal have held that de minimis business support does not, in itself, remove an otherwise qualifying organization from the ambit of MCFL.[Footnote 14] This Court could have simply followed their lead.[Footnote 15]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Finally, let us not forget Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s as-applied constitutional challenge. Precisely because Citizens United looks so much like the MCFL organizations we have exempted from regulation, while a feature-length video-on-demand film looks so unlike the types of electoral advocacy Congress has found deserving of regulation, this challenge is a substantial one. As the appellant\xe2\x80\x99s own arguments show, the Court could have easily limited the breadth of its constitutional holding had it declined to adopt the novel notion that speakers and speech acts must always be treated identically\xe2\x80\x94and always spared expenditures restrictions\xe2\x80\x94in the political realm. Yet the Court nonetheless turns its back on the as-applied review process that has been a staple of campaign finance litigation since Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1 (1976) (per curiam), and that was affirmed and expanded just two Terms ago in WRTL, 551 U. S. 449.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This brief tour of alternative grounds on which the case could have been decided is not meant to show that any of these grounds is ideal, though each is perfectly \xe2\x80\x9cvalid,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 12 (majority opinion).[Footnote 16] It is meant to show that there were principled, narrower paths that a Court that was serious about judicial restraint could have taken. There was also the straightforward path: applying Austin and McConnell, just as the District Court did in holding that the funding of Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s film can be regulated under them. The only thing preventing the majority from affirming the District Court, or adopting a narrower ground that would retain Austin, is its disdain for Austin.

\n

II

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The final principle of judicial process that the majority violates is the most transparent: stare decisis. I am not an absolutist when it comes to stare decisis, in the campaign finance area or in any other. No one is. But if this principle is to do any meaningful work in supporting the rule of law, it must at least demand a significant justification, beyond the preferences of five Justices, for overturning settled doctrine. \xe2\x80\x9c[A] decision to overrule should rest on some special reason over and above the belief that a prior case was wrongly decided.\xe2\x80\x9d Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa. v. Casey, 505 U. S. 833, 864 (1992). No such justification exists in this case, and to the contrary there are powerful prudential reasons to keep faith with our precedents.[Footnote 17]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s central argument for why stare decisis ought to be trumped is that it does not like Austin. The opinion \xe2\x80\x9cwas not well reasoned,\xe2\x80\x9d our colleagues assert, and it conflicts with First Amendment principles. Ante, at 47\xe2\x80\x9348. This, of course, is the Court\xe2\x80\x99s merits argument, the many defects in which we will soon consider. I am perfectly willing to concede that if one of our precedents were dead wrong in its reasoning or irreconcilable with the rest of our doctrine, there would be a compelling basis for revisiting it. But neither is true of Austin, as I explain at length in Parts III and IV, infra, at 23\xe2\x80\x9389, and restating a merits argument with additional vigor does not give it extra weight in the stare decisis calculus.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Perhaps in recognition of this point, the Court supplements its merits case with a smattering of assertions. The Court proclaims that \xe2\x80\x9cAustin is undermined by experience since its announcement.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48. This is a curious claim to make in a case that lacks a developed record. The majority has no empirical evidence with which to substantiate the claim; we just have its ipse dixit that the real world has not been kind to Austin. Nor does the majority bother to specify in what sense Austin has been \xe2\x80\x9cundermined.\xe2\x80\x9d Instead it treats the reader to a string of non sequiturs: \xe2\x80\x9cOur Nation\xe2\x80\x99s speech dynamic is changing,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 48; \xe2\x80\x9c[s]peakers have become adept at presenting citizens with sound bites, talking points, and scripted messages,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid.; \xe2\x80\x9c[c]orporations \xe2\x80\xa6 do not have monolithic views,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. How any of these ruminations weakens the force of stare decisis, escapes my comprehension.[Footnote 18]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority also contends that the Government\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation to rely on Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale \xe2\x80\x9cdiminishe[s]\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cthe principle of adhering to that precedent.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48; see also ante, at 11 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (Government\xe2\x80\x99s litigating position is \xe2\x80\x9cmost importan[t]\xe2\x80\x9d factor undermining Austin). Why it diminishes the value of stare decisis is left unexplained. We have never thought fit to overrule a precedent because a litigant has taken any particular tack. Nor should we. Our decisions can often be defended on multiple grounds, and a litigant may have strategic or case-specific reasons for emphasizing only a subset of them. Members of the public, moreover, often rely on our bottom-line holdings far more than our precise legal arguments; surely this is true for the legislatures that have been regulating corporate electioneering since Austin. The task of evaluating the continued viability of precedents falls to this Court, not to the parties.[Footnote 19]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although the majority opinion spends several pages making these surprising arguments, it says almost nothing about the standard considerations we have used to determine stare decisis value, such as the antiquity of the precedent, the workability of its legal rule, and the reliance interests at stake. It is also conspicuously silent about McConnell, even though the McConnell Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision to uphold BCRA \xc2\xa7203 relied not only on the antidistortion logic of Austin but also on the statute\xe2\x80\x99s historical pedigree, see, e.g., 540 U. S., at 115\xe2\x80\x93132, 223\xe2\x80\x93224, and the need to preserve the integrity of federal campaigns, see id., at 126\xe2\x80\x93129, 205\xe2\x80\x93208, and n.\xc2\xa088.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have recognized that \xe2\x80\x9c[s]tare decisis has special force when legislators or citizens \xe2\x80\x98have acted in reliance on a previous decision, for in this instance overruling the decision would dislodge settled rights and expectations or require an extensive legislative response.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Hubbard v. United States, 514 U. S. 695, 714 (1995) (quoting Hilton v. South Carolina Public Railways Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 502 U. S. 197, 202 (1991)). Stare decisis protects not only personal rights involving property or contract but also the ability of the elected branches to shape their laws in an effective and coherent fashion. Today\xe2\x80\x99s decision takes away a power that we have long permitted these branches to exercise. State legislatures have relied on their authority to regulate corporate electioneering, confirmed in Austin, for more than a century.[Footnote 20] The Federal Congress has relied on this authority for a comparable stretch of time, and it specifically relied on Austin throughout the years it spent developing and debating BCRA. The total record it compiled was 100,000 pages long.[Footnote 21] Pulling out the rug beneath Congress after affirming the constitutionality of \xc2\xa7203 six years ago shows great disrespect for a coequal branch.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0By removing one of its central components, today\xe2\x80\x99s ruling makes a hash out of BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cdelicate and interconnected regulatory scheme.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 172. Consider just one example of the distortions that will follow: Political parties are barred under BCRA from soliciting or spending \xe2\x80\x9csoft money,\xe2\x80\x9d funds that are not subject to the statute\xe2\x80\x99s disclosure requirements or its source and amount limitations. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441i; McConnell, 540 U. S., at 122\xe2\x80\x93126. Going forward, corporations and unions will be free to spend as much general treasury money as they wish on ads that support or attack specific candidates, whereas national parties will not be able to spend a dime of soft money on ads of any kind. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s ruling thus dramatically enhances the role of corporations and unions\xe2\x80\x94and the narrow interests they represent\xe2\x80\x94vis-\xc3\x80-vis the role of political parties\xe2\x80\x94and the broad coalitions they represent\xe2\x80\x94in determining who will hold public office.[Footnote 22]

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Beyond the reliance interests at stake, the other stare decisis factors also cut against the Court. Considerations of antiquity are significant for similar reasons. McConnell is only six years old, but Austin has been on the books for two decades, and many of the statutes called into question by today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion have been on the books for a half-century or more. The Court points to no intervening change in circumstances that warrants revisiting Austin. Certainly nothing relevant has changed since we decided WRTL two Terms ago. And the Court gives no reason to think that Austin and McConnell are unworkable.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In fact, no one has argued to us that Austin\xe2\x80\x99s rule has proved impracticable, and not a single for-profit corporation, union, or State has asked us to overrule it. Quite to the contrary, leading groups representing the business community,[Footnote 23] organized labor,[Footnote 24] and the nonprofit sector,[Footnote 25] together with more than half of the States,[Footnote 26] urge that we preserve Austin. As for McConnell, the portions of BCRA it upheld may be prolix, but all three branches of Government have worked to make \xc2\xa7203 as user-friendly as possible. For instance, Congress established a special mechanism for expedited review of constitutional challenges, see note following 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7437h; the FEC has established a standardized process, with clearly defined safe harbors, for corporations to claim that a particular electioneering communication is permissible under WRTL, see 11 CFR \xc2\xa7114.15 (2009);[Footnote 27] and, as noted above, The Chief Justice crafted his controlling opinion in WRTL with the express goal of maximizing clarity and administrability, 551 U. S., at 469\xe2\x80\x93470, 473\xe2\x80\x93474. The case for stare decisis may be bolstered, we have said, when subsequent rulings \xe2\x80\x9chave reduced the impact\xe2\x80\x9d of a precedent \xe2\x80\x9cwhile reaffirming the decision\xe2\x80\x99s core ruling.\xe2\x80\x9d Dickerson v. United States, 530 U. S. 428, 443 (2000).[Footnote 28]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In the end, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s rejection of Austin and McConnell comes down to nothing more than its disagreement with their results. Virtually every one of its arguments was made and rejected in those cases, and the majority opinion is essentially an amalgamation of resuscitated dissents. The only relevant thing that has changed since Austin and McConnell is the composition of this Court. Today\xe2\x80\x99s ruling thus strikes at the vitals of stare decisis, \xe2\x80\x9cthe means by which we ensure that the law will not merely change erratically, but will develop in a principled and intelligible fashion\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9cpermits society to presume that bedrock principles are founded in the law rather than in the proclivities of individuals.\xe2\x80\x9d Vasquez v. Hillery, 474 U. S. 254, 265 (1986).

\n

III

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The novelty of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s procedural dereliction and its approach to stare decisis is matched by the novelty of its ruling on the merits. The ruling rests on several premises. First, the Court claims that Austin and McConnell have \xe2\x80\x9cbanned\xe2\x80\x9d corporate speech. Second, it claims that the First Amendment precludes regulatory distinctions based on speaker identity, including the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s identity as a corporation. Third, it claims that Austin and McConnell were radical outliers in our First Amendment tradition and our campaign finance jurisprudence. Each of these claims is wrong.

\n

The So-Called \xe2\x80\x9cBan\xe2\x80\x9d

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Pervading the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis is the ominous image of a \xe2\x80\x9ccategorical ba[n]\xe2\x80\x9d on corporate speech. Ante, at 45. Indeed, the majority invokes the specter of a \xe2\x80\x9cban\xe2\x80\x9d on nearly every page of its opinion. Ante, at 1, 4, 7, 10, 11, 12, 13, 16, 20, 21, 22, 23, 26, 27, 28, 29, 30, 31, 33, 35, 38, 40, 42, 45, 46, 47, 49, 54, 56. This characterization is highly misleading, and needs to be corrected.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In fact it already has been. Our cases have repeatedly pointed out that, \xe2\x80\x9c[c]ontrary to the [majority\xe2\x80\x99s] critical assumptions,\xe2\x80\x9d the statutes upheld in Austin and McConnell do \xe2\x80\x9cnot impose an absolute ban on all forms of corporate political spending.\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 660; see also McConnell, 540 U. S., at 203\xe2\x80\x93204; Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 162\xe2\x80\x93163. For starters, both statutes provide exemptions for PACs, separate segregated funds established by a corporation for political purposes. See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(b)(2)(C); Mich. Comp. Laws Ann. \xc2\xa7169.255 (West 2005). \xe2\x80\x9cThe ability to form and administer separate segregated funds,\xe2\x80\x9d we observed in McConnell, \xe2\x80\x9chas provided corporations and unions with a constitutionally sufficient opportunity to engage in express advocacy. That has been this Court\xe2\x80\x99s unanimous view.\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 203.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Under BCRA, any corporation\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cstockholders and their families and its executive or administrative personnel and their families\xe2\x80\x9d can pool their resources to finance electioneering communications. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(b)(4)(A)(i). A significant and growing number of corporations avail themselves of this option;[Footnote 29] during the most recent election cycle, corporate and union PACs raised nearly a billion dollars.[Footnote 30] Administering a PAC entails some administrative burden, but so does complying with the disclaimer, disclosure, and reporting requirements that the Court today upholds, see ante, at 51, and no one has suggested that the burden is severe for a sophisticated for-profit corporation. To the extent the majority is worried about this issue, it is important to keep in mind that we have no record to show how substantial the burden really is, just the majority\xe2\x80\x99s own unsupported factfinding, see ante, at 21\xe2\x80\x9322. Like all other natural persons, every shareholder of every corporation remains entirely free under Austin and McConnell to do however much electioneering she pleases outside of the corporate form. The owners of a \xe2\x80\x9cmom & pop\xe2\x80\x9d store can simply place ads in their own names, rather than the store\xe2\x80\x99s. If ideologically aligned individuals wish to make unlimited expenditures through the corporate form, they may utilize an MCFL organization that has policies in place to avoid becoming a conduit for business or union interests. See MCFL, 479 U. S., at 263\xe2\x80\x93264.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The laws upheld in Austin and McConnell leave open many additional avenues for corporations\xe2\x80\x99 political speech. Consider the statutory provision we are ostensibly evaluating in this case, BCRA \xc2\xa7203. It has no application to genuine issue advertising\xe2\x80\x94a category of corporate speech Congress found to be far more substantial than election-related advertising, see McConnell, 540 U. S., at 207\xe2\x80\x94or to Internet, telephone, and print advocacy.[Footnote 31] Like numerous statutes, it exempts media companies\xe2\x80\x99 news stories, commentaries, and editorials from its electioneering restrictions, in recognition of the unique role played by the institutional press in sustaining public debate.[Footnote 32] See 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(B)(i); McConnell, 540 U. S., at 208\xe2\x80\x93209; see also Austin, 494 U. S., at 666\xe2\x80\x93668. It also allows corporations to spend unlimited sums on political communications with their executives and shareholders, \xc2\xa7441b(b)(2)(A); 11 CFR \xc2\xa7114.3(a)(1), to fund additional PAC activity through trade associations, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(b)(4)(D), to distribute voting guides and voting records, 11 CFR \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7114.4(c)(4)\xe2\x80\x93(5), to underwrite voter registration and voter turnout activities, \xc2\xa7114.3(c)(4); \xc2\xa7114.4(c)(2), to host fundraising events for candidates within certain limits, \xc2\xa7114.4(c); \xc2\xa7114.2(f)(2), and to publicly endorse candidates through a press release and press conference, \xc2\xa7114.4(c)(6).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0At the time Citizens United brought this lawsuit, the only types of speech that could be regulated under \xc2\xa7203 were: (1) broadcast, cable, or satellite communications;[Footnote 33] (2) capable of reaching at least 50,000 persons in the relevant electorate;[Footnote 34] (3) made within 30 days of a primary or 60 days of a general federal election;[Footnote 35] (4) by a labor union or a non-MCFL, nonmedia corporation;[Footnote 36] (5) paid for with general treasury funds;[Footnote 37] and (6) \xe2\x80\x9csusceptible of no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d[Footnote 38] The category of communications meeting all of these criteria is not trivial, but the notion that corporate political speech has been \xe2\x80\x9csuppress[ed] \xe2\x80\xa6 altogether,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 2, that corporations have been \xe2\x80\x9cexclu[ded] \xe2\x80\xa6 from the general public dialogue,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 25, or that a work of fiction such as Mr. Smith Goes to Washington might be covered, ante, at 56\xe2\x80\x9357, is nonsense.[Footnote 39] Even the plaintiffs in McConnell, who had every incentive to depict BCRA as negatively as possible, declined to argue that \xc2\xa7203\xe2\x80\x99s prohibition on certain uses of general treasury funds amounts to a complete ban. See 540 U. S., at 204.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In many ways, then, \xc2\xa7203 functions as a source restriction or a time, place, and manner restriction. It applies in a viewpoint-neutral fashion to a narrow subset of advocacy messages about clearly identified candidates for federal office, made during discrete time periods through discrete channels. In the case at hand, all Citizens United needed to do to broadcast Hillary right before the primary was to abjure business contributions or use the funds in its PAC, which by its own account is \xe2\x80\x9cone of the most active conservative PACs in America,\xe2\x80\x9d Citizens United Political Victory Fund, http://www.cupvf.org/.[Footnote 40]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0So let us be clear: Neither Austin nor McConnell held or implied that corporations may be silenced; the FEC is not a \xe2\x80\x9ccensor\xe2\x80\x9d; and in the years since these cases were decided, corporations have continued to play a major role in the national dialogue. Laws such as \xc2\xa7203 target a class of communications that is especially likely to corrupt the political process, that is at least one degree removed from the views of individual citizens, and that may not even reflect the views of those who pay for it. Such laws burden political speech, and that is always a serious matter, demanding careful scrutiny. But the majority\xe2\x80\x99s incessant talk of a \xe2\x80\x9cban\xe2\x80\x9d aims at a straw man.

\n

Identity-Based Distinctions

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The second pillar of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion is its assertion that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Government cannot restrict political speech based on the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\xa6 identity.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 30; accord, ante, at 1, 24, 26, 30, 31, 32, 33, 34, 49, 50. The case on which it relies for this proposition is First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765 (1978). As I shall explain, infra, at 52\xe2\x80\x9355, the holding in that case was far narrower than the Court implies. Like its paeans to unfettered discourse, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s denunciation of identity-based distinctions may have rhetorical appeal but it obscures reality.

\n
