\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cOur jurisprudence over the past 216 years has rejected an absolutist interpretation\xe2\x80\x9d of the First Amendment. WRTL, 551 U. S., at 482 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.). The First Amendment provides that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress shall make no law \xe2\x80\xa6 abridging the freedom of speech, or of the press.\xe2\x80\x9d Apart perhaps from measures designed to protect the press, that text might seem to permit no distinctions of any kind. Yet in a variety of contexts, we have held that speech can be regulated differentially on account of the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s identity, when identity is understood in categorical or institutional terms. The Government routinely places special restrictions on the speech rights of students,[Footnote 41] prisoners,[Footnote 42] members of the Armed Forces,[Footnote 43] foreigners,[Footnote 44] and its own employees.[Footnote 45] When such restrictions are justified by a legitimate governmental interest, they do not necessarily raise constitutional problems.[Footnote 46] In contrast to the blanket rule that the majority espouses, our cases recognize that the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interests may be more or less compelling with respect to different classes of speakers,[Footnote 47] cf. Minneapolis Star & Tribune Co. v. Minnesota Comm\xe2\x80\x99r of Revenue, 460 U. S. 575, 585 (1983) (\xe2\x80\x9c[D]ifferential treatment\xe2\x80\x9d is constitutionally suspect \xe2\x80\x9cunless justified by some special characteristic\xe2\x80\x9d of the regulated class of speakers (emphasis added)), and that the constitutional rights of certain categories of speakers, in certain contexts, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98are not automatically coextensive with the rights\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d that are normally accorded to members of our society, Morse v. Frederick, 551 U. S. 393, 396\xe2\x80\x93397, 404 (2007) (quoting Bethel School Dist. No. 403 v. Fraser, 478 U. S. 675, 682 (1986)).

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The free speech guarantee thus does not render every other public interest an illegitimate basis for qualifying a speaker\xe2\x80\x99s autonomy; society could scarcely function if it did. It is fair to say that our First Amendment doctrine has \xe2\x80\x9cfrowned on\xe2\x80\x9d certain identity-based distinctions, Los Angeles Police Dept. v. United Reporting Publishing Corp., 528 U. S. 32, 47, n.\xc2\xa04 (1999) (Stevens, J., dissenting), particularly those that may reflect invidious discrimination or preferential treatment of a politically powerful group. But it is simply incorrect to suggest that we have prohibited all legislative distinctions based on identity or content. Not even close.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The election context is distinctive in many ways, and the Court, of course, is right that the First Amendment closely guards political speech. But in this context, too, the authority of legislatures to enact viewpoint-neutral regulations based on content and identity is well settled. We have, for example, allowed state-run broadcasters to exclude independent candidates from televised debates. Arkansas Ed. Television Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Forbes, 523 U. S. 666 (1998).[Footnote 48] We have upheld statutes that prohibit the distribution or display of campaign materials near a polling place. Burson v. Freeman, 504 U. S. 191 (1992).[Footnote 49] Although we have not reviewed them directly, we have never cast doubt on laws that place special restrictions on campaign spending by foreign nationals. See, e.g., 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441e(a)(1). And we have consistently approved laws that bar Government employees, but not others, from contributing to or participating in political activities. See n.\xc2\xa045, supra. These statutes burden the political expression of one class of speakers, namely, civil servants. Yet we have sustained them on the basis of longstanding practice and Congress\xe2\x80\x99 reasoned judgment that certain regulations which leave \xe2\x80\x9cuntouched full participation \xe2\x80\xa6 in political decisions at the ballot box,\xe2\x80\x9d Civil Service Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Letter Carriers, 413 U. S. 548, 556 (1973) (internal quotation marks omitted), help ensure that public officials are \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently free from improper influences,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 564, and that \xe2\x80\x9cconfidence in the system of representative Government is not \xe2\x80\xa6 eroded to a disastrous extent,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 565.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The same logic applies to this case with additional force because it is the identity of corporations, rather than individuals, that the Legislature has taken into account. As we have unanimously observed, legislatures are entitled to decide \xe2\x80\x9cthat the special characteristics of the corporate structure require particularly careful regulation\xe2\x80\x9d in an electoral context. NRWC, 459 U. S., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210.[Footnote 50] Not only has the distinctive potential of corporations to corrupt the electoral process long been recognized, but within the area of campaign finance, corporate spending is also \xe2\x80\x9cfurthest from the core of political expression, since corporations\xe2\x80\x99 First Amendment speech and association interests are derived largely from those of their members and of the public in receiving information,\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 161, n.\xc2\xa08 (citation omitted). Campaign finance distinctions based on corporate identity tend to be less worrisome, in other words, because the \xe2\x80\x9cspeakers\xe2\x80\x9d are not natural persons, much less members of our political community, and the governmental interests are of the highest order. Furthermore, when corporations, as a class, are distinguished from noncorporations, as a class, there is a lesser risk that regulatory distinctions will reflect invidious discrimination or political favoritism.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If taken seriously, our colleagues\xe2\x80\x99 assumption that the identity of a speaker has no relevance to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s ability to regulate political speech would lead to some remarkable conclusions. Such an assumption would have accorded the propaganda broadcasts to our troops by \xe2\x80\x9cTokyo Rose\xe2\x80\x9d during World War II the same protection as speech by Allied commanders. More pertinently, it would appear to afford the same protection to multinational corporations controlled by foreigners as to individual Americans: To do otherwise, after all, could \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98enhance the relative voice\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d of some (i.e., humans) over others (i.e., nonhumans). Ante, at 33 (quoting Buckley, 424 U. S., at 49).[Footnote 51] Under the majority\xe2\x80\x99s view, I suppose it may be a First Amendment problem that corporations are not permitted to vote, given that voting is, among other things, a form of speech.[Footnote 52]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In short, the Court dramatically overstates its critique of identity-based distinctions, without ever explaining why corporate identity demands the same treatment as individual identity. Only the most wooden approach to the First Amendment could justify the unprecedented line it seeks to draw.

\n

Our First Amendment Tradition

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A third fulcrum of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion is the idea that Austin and McConnell are radical outliers, \xe2\x80\x9caberration[s],\xe2\x80\x9d in our First Amendment tradition. Ante, at 39; see also ante, at 45, 56 (professing fidelity to \xe2\x80\x9cour law and our tradition\xe2\x80\x9d). The Court has it exactly backwards. It is today\xe2\x80\x99s holding that is the radical departure from what had been settled First Amendment law. To see why, it is useful to take a long view.

\n

1.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 Original Understandings

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Let us start from the beginning. The Court invokes \xe2\x80\x9cancient First Amendment principles,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1 (internal quotation marks omitted), and original understandings, ante, at 37\xe2\x80\x9338, to defend today\xe2\x80\x99s ruling, yet it makes only a perfunctory attempt to ground its analysis in the principles or understandings of those who drafted and ratified the Amendment. Perhaps this is because there is not a scintilla of evidence to support the notion that anyone believed it would preclude regulatory distinctions based on the corporate form. To the extent that the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 views are discernible and relevant to the disposition of this case, they would appear to cut strongly against the majority\xe2\x80\x99s position.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This is not only because the Framers and their contemporaries conceived of speech more narrowly than we now think of it, see Bork, Neutral Principles and Some First Amendment Problems, 47 Ind. L.\xc2\xa0J. 1, 22 (1971), but also because they held very different views about the nature of the First Amendment right and the role of corporations in society. Those few corporations that existed at the founding were authorized by grant of a special legislative charter.[Footnote 53] Corporate sponsors would petition the legislature, and the legislature, if amenable, would issue a charter that specified the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s powers and purposes and \xe2\x80\x9cauthoritatively fixed the scope and content of corporate organization,\xe2\x80\x9d including \xe2\x80\x9cthe internal structure of the corporation.\xe2\x80\x9d J. Hurst, The Legitimacy of the Business Corporation in the Law of the United States 1780\xe2\x80\x931970, pp.\xc2\xa015\xe2\x80\x9316 (1970) (reprint 2004). Corporations were created, supervised, and conceptualized as quasi-public entities, \xe2\x80\x9cdesigned to serve a social function for the state.\xe2\x80\x9d Handlin & Handlin, Origin of the American Business Corporation, 5 J. Econ. Hist. 1, 22 (1945). It was \xe2\x80\x9cassumed that [they] were legally privileged organizations that had to be closely scrutinized by the legislature because their purposes had to be made consistent with public welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d R. Seavoy, Origins of the American Business Corporation, 1784\xe2\x80\x931855, p.\xc2\xa05 (1982).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The individualized charter mode of incorporation reflected the \xe2\x80\x9ccloud of disfavor under which corporations labored\xe2\x80\x9d in the early years of this Nation. 1 W. Fletcher, Cyclopedia of the Law of Corporations \xc2\xa72, p.\xc2\xa08 (rev. ed. 2006); see also Louis K. Liggett Co. v. Lee, 288 U. S. 517, 548\xe2\x80\x93549 (1933) (Brandeis, J., dissenting) (discussing fears of the \xe2\x80\x9cevils\xe2\x80\x9d of business corporations); L. Friedman, A History of American Law 194 (2d ed. 1985) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe word \xe2\x80\x98soulless\xe2\x80\x99 constantly recurs in debates over corporations\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Corporations, it was feared, could concentrate the worst urges of whole groups of men\xe2\x80\x9d). Thomas Jefferson famously fretted that corporations would subvert the Republic.[Footnote 54] General incorporation statutes, and widespread acceptance of business corporations as socially useful actors, did not emerge until the 1800\xe2\x80\x99s. See Hansmann & Kraakman, The End of History for Corporate Law, 89 Geo. L.\xc2\xa0J. 439, 440 (2001) (hereinafter Hansmann & Kraakman) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]ll general business corporation statutes appear to date from well after 1800\xe2\x80\x9d).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Framers thus took it as a given that corporations could be comprehensively regulated in the service of the public welfare. Unlike our colleagues, they had little trouble distinguishing corporations from human beings, and when they constitutionalized the right to free speech in the First Amendment, it was the free speech of individual Americans that they had in mind.[Footnote 55] While individuals might join together to exercise their speech rights, business corporations, at least, were plainly not seen as facilitating such associational or expressive ends. Even \xe2\x80\x9cthe notion that business corporations could invoke the First Amendment would probably have been quite a novelty,\xe2\x80\x9d given that \xe2\x80\x9cat the time, the legitimacy of every corporate activity was thought to rest entirely in a concession of the sovereign.\xe2\x80\x9d Shelledy, Autonomy, Debate, and Corporate Speech, 18 Hastings Const. L.\xc2\xa0Q. 541, 578 (1991); cf. Trustees of Dartmouth College v. Woodward, 4 Wheat. 518, 636 (1819) (Marshall, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (\xe2\x80\x9cA corporation is an artificial being, invisible, intangible, and existing only in contemplation of law. Being the mere creature of law, it possesses only those properties which the charter of its creation confers upon it\xe2\x80\x9d); Eule, Promoting Speaker Diversity: Austin and Metro Broadcasting, 1990 S. Ct. Rev. 105, 129 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe framers of the First Amendment could scarcely have anticipated its application to the corporation form. That, of course, ought not to be dispositive. What is compelling, however, is an understanding of who was supposed to be the beneficiary of the free speech guaranty\xe2\x80\x94the individual\xe2\x80\x9d). In light of these background practices and understandings, it seems to me implausible that the Framers believed \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of speech\xe2\x80\x9d would extend equally to all corporate speakers, much less that it would preclude legislatures from taking limited measures to guard against corporate capture of elections.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court observes that the Framers drew on diverse intellectual sources, communicated through newspapers, and aimed to provide greater freedom of speech than had existed in England. Ante, at 37. From these (accurate) observations, the Court concludes that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he First Amendment was certainly not understood to condone the suppression of political speech in society\xe2\x80\x99s most salient media.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. This conclusion is far from certain, given that many historians believe the Framers were focused on prior restraints on publication and did not understand the First Amendment to \xe2\x80\x9cprevent the subsequent punishment of such [publications] as may be deemed contrary to the public welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d Near v. Minnesota ex rel. Olson, 283 U. S. 697, 714 (1931). Yet, even if the majority\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion were correct, it would tell us only that the First Amendment was understood to protect political speech in certain media. It would tell us little about whether the Amendment was understood to protect general treasury electioneering expenditures by corporations, and to what extent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0As a matter of original expectations, then, it seems absurd to think that the First Amendment prohibits legislatures from taking into account the corporate identity of a sponsor of electoral advocacy. As a matter of original meaning, it likewise seems baseless\xe2\x80\x94unless one evaluates the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cprinciples,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1, 48, or its \xe2\x80\x9cpurpose,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 5 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.), at such a high level of generality that the historical understandings of the Amendment cease to be a meaningful constraint on the judicial task. This case sheds a revelatory light on the assumption of some that an impartial judge\xe2\x80\x99s application of an originalist methodology is likely to yield more determinate answers, or to play a more decisive role in the decisional process, than his or her views about sound policy.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia criticizes the foregoing discussion for failing to adduce statements from the founding era showing that corporations were understood to be excluded from the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s free speech guarantee. Ante, at 1\xe2\x80\x932, 9. Of course, Justice Scalia adduces no statements to suggest the contrary proposition, or even to suggest that the contrary proposition better reflects the kind of right that the drafters and ratifiers of the Free Speech Clause thought they were enshrining. Although Justice Scalia makes a perfectly sensible argument that an individual\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak entails a right to speak with others for a common cause, cf. MCFL, 479 U. S. 238, he does not explain why those two rights must be precisely identical, or why that principle applies to electioneering by corporations that serve no \xe2\x80\x9ccommon cause.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 8. Nothing in his account dislodges my basic point that members of the founding generation held a cautious view of corporate power and a narrow view of corporate rights (not that they \xe2\x80\x9cdespised\xe2\x80\x9d corporations, ante, at 2), and that they conceptualized speech in individualistic terms. If no prominent Framer bothered to articulate that corporate speech would have lesser status than individual speech, that may well be because the contrary proposition\xe2\x80\x94if not also the very notion of \xe2\x80\x9ccorporate speech\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94was inconceivable.[Footnote 56]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Scalia also emphasizes the unqualified nature of the First Amendment text. Ante, at 2, 8. Yet he would seemingly read out the Free Press Clause: How else could he claim that my purported views on newspapers must track my views on corporations generally? Ante, at 6.[Footnote 57] Like virtually all modern lawyers, Justice Scalia presumably believes that the First Amendment restricts the Executive, even though its language refers to Congress alone. In any event, the text only leads us back to the questions who or what is guaranteed \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of speech,\xe2\x80\x9d and, just as critically, what that freedom consists of and under what circumstances it may be limited. Justice Scalia appears to believe that because corporations are created and utilized by individuals, it follows (as night the day) that their electioneering must be equally protected by the First Amendment and equally immunized from expenditure limits. See ante, at 7\xe2\x80\x938. That conclusion certainly does not follow as a logical matter, and Justice Scalia fails to explain why the original public meaning leads it to follow as a matter of interpretation.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The truth is we cannot be certain how a law such as BCRA \xc2\xa7203 meshes with the original meaning of the First Amendment.[Footnote 58] I have given several reasons why I believe the Constitution would have been understood then, and ought to be understood now, to permit reasonable restrictions on corporate electioneering, and I will give many more reasons in the pages to come. The Court enlists the Framers in its defense without seriously grappling with their understandings of corporations or the free speech right, or with the republican principles that underlay those understandings.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In fairness, our campaign finance jurisprudence has never attended very closely to the views of the Framers, see Randall v. Sorrell, 548 U. S. 230, 280 (2006) (Stevens, J., dissenting), whose political universe differed profoundly from that of today. We have long since held that corporations are covered by the First Amendment, and many legal scholars have long since rejected the concession theory of the corporation. But \xe2\x80\x9chistorical context is usually relevant,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. (internal quotation marks omitted), and in light of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s effort to cast itself as guardian of ancient values, it pays to remember that nothing in our constitutional history dictates today\xe2\x80\x99s outcome. To the contrary, this history helps illuminate just how extraordinarily dissonant the decision is.

\n

2. Legislative and Judicial Interpretation

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0A century of more recent history puts to rest any notion that today\xe2\x80\x99s ruling is faithful to our First Amendment tradition. At the federal level, the express distinction between corporate and individual political spending on elections stretches back to 1907, when Congress passed the Tillman Act, ch. 420, 34 Stat. 864, banning all corporate contributions to candidates. The Senate Report on the legislation observed that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he evils of the use of [corporate] money in connection with political elections are so generally recognized that the committee deems it unnecessary to make any argument in favor of the general purpose of this measure. It is in the interest of good government and calculated to promote purity in the selection of public officials.\xe2\x80\x9d S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 3056, 59th Cong., 1st Sess., 2 (1906). President Roosevelt, in his 1905 annual message to Congress, declared:

\n

\xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98All contributions by corporations to any political committee or for any political purpose should be forbidden by law; directors should not be permitted to use stockholders\xe2\x80\x99 money for such purposes; and, moreover, a prohibition of this kind would be, as far as it went, an effective method of stopping the evils aimed at in corrupt practices acts.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Automobile Workers, 352 U. S. 567, 572 (1957) (quoting 40 Cong. Rec. 96).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court has surveyed the history leading up to the Tillman Act several times, see WRTL, 551 U. S., at 508\xe2\x80\x93510 (Souter, J., dissenting); McConnell, 540 U. S., at 115; Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 570\xe2\x80\x93575, and I will refrain from doing so again. It is enough to say that the Act was primarily driven by two pressing concerns: first, the enormous power corporations had come to wield in federal elections, with the accompanying threat of both actual corruption and a public perception of corruption; and second, a respect for the interest of shareholders and members in preventing the use of their money to support candidates they opposed. See ibid.; United States v. CIO, 335 U. S. 106, 113 (1948); Winkler, \xe2\x80\x9cOther People\xe2\x80\x99s Money\xe2\x80\x9d: Corporations, Agency Costs, and Campaign Finance Law, 92 Geo. L.\xc2\xa0J. 871 (2004).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Over the years, the limitations on corporate political spending have been modified in a number of ways, as Congress responded to changes in the American economy and political practices that threatened to displace the commonweal. Justice Souter recently traced these developments at length.[Footnote 59] WRTL, 551 U. S., at 507\xe2\x80\x93519 (dissenting opinion); see also McConnell, 540 U. S., at 115\xe2\x80\x93133; McConnell, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 188\xe2\x80\x93205. The Taft-Hartley Act of 1947 is of special significance for this case. In that Act passed more than 60 years ago, Congress extended the prohibition on corporate support of candidates to cover not only direct contributions, but independent expenditures as well. Labor Management Relations Act, 1947, \xc2\xa7304, 61 Stat. 159. The bar on contributions \xe2\x80\x9cwas being so narrowly construed\xe2\x80\x9d that corporations were easily able to defeat the purposes of the Act by supporting candidates through other means. WRTL, 551 U. S., at 511 (Souter, J., dissenting) (citing S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 1, 80th Cong., 1st Sess., 38\xe2\x80\x9339 (1947)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our colleagues emphasize that in two cases from the middle of the 20th century, several Justices wrote separately to criticize the expenditure restriction as applied to unions, even though the Court declined to pass on its constitutionality. Ante, at 27\xe2\x80\x9328. Two features of these cases are of far greater relevance. First, those Justices were writing separately; which is to say, their position failed to command a majority. Prior to today, this was a fact we found significant in evaluating precedents. Second, each case in this line expressed support for the principle that corporate and union political speech financed with PAC funds, collected voluntarily from the organization\xe2\x80\x99s stockholders or members, receives greater protection than speech financed with general treasury funds.[Footnote 60]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This principle was carried forward when Congress enacted comprehensive campaign finance reform in the Federal Election Campaign Act of 1971 (FECA), 86 Stat. 3, which retained the restriction on using general treasury funds for contributions and expenditures, 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b(a). FECA codified the option for corporations and unions to create PACs to finance contributions and expenditures forbidden to the corporation or union itself. \xc2\xa7441b(b).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0By the time Congress passed FECA in 1971, the bar on corporate contributions and expenditures had become such an accepted part of federal campaign finance regulation that when a large number of plaintiffs, including several nonprofit corporations, challenged virtually every aspect of the Act in Buckley, 424 U. S. 1, no one even bothered to argue that the bar as such was unconstitutional. Buckley famously (or infamously) distinguished direct contributions from independent expenditures, id., at 58\xe2\x80\x9359, but its silence on corporations only reinforced the understanding that corporate expenditures could be treated differently from individual expenditures. \xe2\x80\x9cSince our decision in Buckley, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to prohibit corporations and unions from using funds in their treasuries to finance advertisements expressly advocating the election or defeat of candidates in federal elections has been firmly embedded in our law.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 203.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Thus, it was unremarkable, in a 1982 case holding that Congress could bar nonprofit corporations from soliciting nonmembers for PAC funds, that then-Justice Rehnquist wrote for a unanimous Court that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9ccareful legislative adjustment of the federal electoral laws, in a cautious advance, step by step, to account for the particular legal and economic attributes of corporations \xe2\x80\xa6 warrants considerable deference,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9creflects a permissible assessment of the dangers posed by those entities to the electoral process.\xe2\x80\x9d NRWC, 459 U. S., at 209 (internal quotation marks and citation omitted). \xe2\x80\x9cThe governmental interest in preventing both actual corruption and the appearance of corruption of elected representatives has long been recognized,\xe2\x80\x9d the unanimous Court observed, \xe2\x80\x9cand there is no reason why it may not \xe2\x80\xa6 be accomplished by treating \xe2\x80\xa6 corporations \xe2\x80\xa6 differently from individuals.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 210\xe2\x80\x93211.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The corporate/individual distinction was not questioned by the Court\xe2\x80\x99s disposition, in 1986, of a challenge to the expenditure restriction as applied to a distinctive type of nonprofit corporation. In MCFL, 479 U. S. 238, we stated again \xe2\x80\x9cthat \xe2\x80\x98the special characteristics of the corporate structure require particularly careful regulation,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 256 (quoting NRWC, 459 U. S., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210), and again we acknowledged that the Government has a legitimate interest in \xe2\x80\x9cregulat[ing] the substantial aggregations of wealth amassed by the special advantages which go with the corporate form,\xe2\x80\x9d 479 U. S., at 257 (internal quotation marks omitted). Those aggregations can distort the \xe2\x80\x9cfree trade in ideas\xe2\x80\x9d crucial to candidate elections, ibid., at the expense of members or shareholders who may disagree with the object of the expenditures, id., at 260 (internal quotation marks omitted). What the Court held by a 5-to-4 vote was that a limited class of corporations must be allowed to use their general treasury funds for independent expenditures, because Congress\xe2\x80\x99 interests in protecting shareholders and \xe2\x80\x9crestrict[ing] \xe2\x80\x98the influence of political war chests funneled through the corporate form,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 257 (quoting FEC v. National Conservative Political Action Comm., 470 U. S. 480, 501 (1985) (NCPAC)), did not apply to corporations that were structurally insulated from those concerns.[Footnote 61]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is worth remembering for present purposes that the four MCFL dissenters, led by Chief Justice Rehnquist, thought the Court was carrying the First Amendment too far. They would have recognized congressional authority to bar general treasury electioneering expenditures even by this class of nonprofits; they acknowledged that \xe2\x80\x9cthe threat from corporate political activity will vary depending on the particular characteristics of a given corporation,\xe2\x80\x9d but believed these \xe2\x80\x9cdistinctions among corporations\xe2\x80\x9d were \xe2\x80\x9cdistinctions in degree,\xe2\x80\x9d not \xe2\x80\x9cin kind,\xe2\x80\x9d and thus \xe2\x80\x9cmore properly drawn by the Legislature than by the Judiciary.\xe2\x80\x9d 479 U. S., at 268 (opinion of Rehnquist, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (internal quotation marks omitted). Not a single Justice suggested that regulation of corporate political speech could be no more stringent than of speech by an individual.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Four years later, in Austin, 494 U. S. 652, we considered whether corporations falling outside the MCFL exception could be barred from using general treasury funds to make independent expenditures in support of, or in opposition to, candidates. We held they could be. Once again recognizing the importance of \xe2\x80\x9cthe integrity of the marketplace of political ideas\xe2\x80\x9d in candidate elections, MCFL, 479 U. S., at 257, we noted that corporations have \xe2\x80\x9cspecial advantages\xe2\x80\x94such as limited liability, perpetual life, and favorable treatment of the accumulation and distribution of assets,\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 658\xe2\x80\x93659\xe2\x80\x94that allow them to spend prodigious general treasury sums on campaign messages that have \xe2\x80\x9clittle or no correlation\xe2\x80\x9d with the beliefs held by actual persons, id., at 660. In light of the corrupting effects such spending might have on the political process, ibid., we permitted the State of Michigan to limit corporate expenditures on candidate elections to corporations\xe2\x80\x99 PACs, which rely on voluntary contributions and thus \xe2\x80\x9creflect actual public support for the political ideals espoused by corporations,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. Notwithstanding our colleagues\xe2\x80\x99 insinuations that Austin deprived the public of general \xe2\x80\x9cideas,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cfacts,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cknowledge,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 38\xe2\x80\x9339, the decision addressed only candidate-focused expenditures and gave the State no license to regulate corporate spending on other matters.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In the 20 years since Austin, we have reaffirmed its holding and rationale a number of times, see, e.g., Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 153\xe2\x80\x93156, most importantly in McConnell, 540 U. S. 93, where we upheld the provision challenged here, \xc2\xa7203 of BCRA.[Footnote 62] Congress crafted \xc2\xa7203 in response to a problem created by Buckley. The Buckley Court had construed FECA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of prohibited \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures\xe2\x80\x9d narrowly to avoid any problems of constitutional vagueness, holding it applicable only to \xe2\x80\x9ccommunications that expressly advocate the election or defeat of a clearly identified candidate,\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 80, i.e., statements containing so-called \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d like \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98vote for,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98elect,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98support,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98cast your ballot for,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Smith for Congress,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98vote against,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98defeat,\xe2\x80\x99 [or] \xe2\x80\x98reject,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 43\xe2\x80\x9344, and n. 52. After Buckley, corporations and unions figured out how to circumvent the limits on express advocacy by using sham \xe2\x80\x9cissue ads\xe2\x80\x9d that \xe2\x80\x9ceschewed the use of magic words\xe2\x80\x9d but nonetheless \xe2\x80\x9cadvocate[d] the election or defeat of clearly identified federal candidates.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 126. \xe2\x80\x9cCorporations and unions spent hundreds of millions of dollars of their general funds to pay for these ads.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 127. Congress passed \xc2\xa7203 to address this circumvention, prohibiting corporations and unions from using general treasury funds for electioneering communications that \xe2\x80\x9crefe[r] to a clearly identified candidate,\xe2\x80\x9d whether or not those communications use the magic words. 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7434(f)(3)(A)(i)(I).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When we asked in McConnell \xe2\x80\x9cwhether a compelling governmental interest justifie[d]\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7203, we found the question \xe2\x80\x9ceasily answered\xe2\x80\x9d: \xe2\x80\x9cWe have repeatedly sustained legislation aimed at \xe2\x80\x98the corrosive and distorting effects of immense aggregations of wealth that are accumulated with the help of the corporate form and that have little or no correlation to the public\xe2\x80\x99s support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 205 (quoting Austin, 494 U. S., at 660). These precedents \xe2\x80\x9crepresent respect for the legislative judgment that the special characteristics of the corporate structure require particularly careful regulation.\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 205 (internal quotation marks omitted). \xe2\x80\x9cMoreover, recent cases have recognized that certain restrictions on corporate electoral involvement permissibly hedge against \xe2\x80\x98\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9ccircumvention of [valid] contribution limits.\xe2\x80\x9d\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (quoting Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 155, in turn quoting FEC v. Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm., 533 U. S. 431, 456, and n.\xc2\xa018 (2001) (Colorado II); alteration in original). BCRA, we found, is faithful to the compelling governmental interests in \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98preserving the integrity of the electoral process, preventing corruption, \xe2\x80\xa6 sustaining the active, alert responsibility of the individual citizen in a democracy for the wise conduct of the government,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and maintaining \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the individual citizen\xe2\x80\x99s confidence in government.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 540 U. S., at 206\xe2\x80\x93207, n.\xc2\xa088 (quoting Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 788\xe2\x80\x93789; some internal quotation marks and brackets omitted). What made the answer even easier than it might have been otherwise was the option to form PACs, which give corporations, at the least, \xe2\x80\x9ca constitutionally sufficient opportunity to engage in\xe2\x80\x9d independent expenditures. 540 U. S., at 203.

\n

3.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 Buckley and Bellotti

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Against this extensive background of congressional regulation of corporate campaign spending, and our repeated affirmation of this regulation as constitutionally sound, the majority dismisses Austin as \xe2\x80\x9ca significant departure from ancient First Amendment principles,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1 (internal quotation marks omitted). How does the majority attempt to justify this claim? Selected passages from two cases, Buckley, 424 U. S. 1, and Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, do all of the work. In the Court\xe2\x80\x99s view, Buckley and Bellotti decisively rejected the possibility of distinguishing corporations from natural persons in the 1970\xe2\x80\x99s; it just so happens that in every single case in which the Court has reviewed campaign finance legislation in the decades since, the majority failed to grasp this truth. The Federal Congress and dozens of state legislatures, we now know, have been similarly deluded.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority emphasizes Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s statement that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[t]he concept that government may restrict the speech of some elements of our society in order to enhance the relative voice of others is wholly foreign to the First Amendment.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 33 (quoting 424 U. S., at 48\xe2\x80\x9349); ante, at 8 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.). But this elegant phrase cannot bear the weight that our colleagues have placed on it. For one thing, the Constitution does, in fact, permit numerous \xe2\x80\x9crestrictions on the speech of some in order to prevent a few from drowning out the many\xe2\x80\x9d: for example, restrictions on ballot access and on legislators\xe2\x80\x99 floor time. Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 402 (2000) (Breyer, J., concurring). For another, the Buckley Court used this line in evaluating \xe2\x80\x9cthe ancillary governmental interest in equalizing the relative ability of individuals and groups to influence the outcome of elections.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 48. It is not apparent why this is relevant to the case before us. The majority suggests that Austin rests on the foreign concept of speech equalization, ante, at 34; ante, at 8\xe2\x80\x9310 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.), but we made it clear in Austin (as in several cases before and since) that a restriction on the way corporations spend their money is no mere exercise in disfavoring the voice of some elements of our society in preference to others. Indeed, we expressly ruled that the compelling interest supporting Michigan\xe2\x80\x99s statute was not one of \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98equaliz[ing] the relative influence of speakers on elections,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 660 (quoting id., at 705 (Kennedy, J., dissenting)), but rather the need to confront the distinctive corrupting potential of corporate electoral advocacy financed by general treasury dollars, id., at 659\xe2\x80\x93660.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0For that matter, it should go without saying that when we made this statement in Buckley, we could not have been casting doubt on the restriction on corporate expenditures in candidate elections, which had not been challenged as \xe2\x80\x9cforeign to the First Amendment,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 33 (quoting Buckley, 424 U. S., at 49), or for any other reason. Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s independent expenditure analysis was focused on a very different statutory provision, 18 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7608(e)(1) (1970 ed., Supp. V). It is implausible to think, as the majority suggests, ante, at 29\xe2\x80\x9330, that Buckley covertly invalidated FECA\xe2\x80\x99s separate corporate and union campaign expenditure restriction, \xc2\xa7610 (now codified at 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b), even though that restriction had been on the books for decades before Buckley and would remain on the books, undisturbed, for decades after.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The case on which the majority places even greater weight than Buckley, however, is Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, claiming it \xe2\x80\x9ccould not have been clearer\xe2\x80\x9d that Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s holding forbade distinctions between corporate and individual expenditures like the one at issue here, ante, at 30. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s reliance is odd. The only thing about Bellotti that could not be clearer is that it declined to adopt the majority\xe2\x80\x99s position. Bellotti ruled, in an explicit limitation on the scope of its holding, that \xe2\x80\x9cour consideration of a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s right to speak on issues of general public interest implies no comparable right in the quite different context of participation in a political campaign for election to public office.\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 788, n. 26; see also id., at 787\xe2\x80\x93788 (acknowledging that the interests in preserving public confidence in Government and protecting dissenting shareholders may be \xe2\x80\x9cweighty \xe2\x80\xa6 in the context of partisan candidate elections\xe2\x80\x9d). Bellotti, in other words, did not touch the question presented in Austin and McConnell, and the opinion squarely disavowed the proposition for which the majority cites it.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority attempts to explain away the distinction Bellotti drew\xe2\x80\x94between general corporate speech and campaign speech intended to promote or prevent the election of specific candidates for office\xe2\x80\x94as inconsistent with the rest of the opinion and with Buckley. Ante, at 31, 42\xe2\x80\x9344. Yet the basis for this distinction is perfectly coherent: The anticorruption interests that animate regulations of corporate participation in candidate elections, the \xe2\x80\x9cimportance\xe2\x80\x9d of which \xe2\x80\x9chas never been doubted,\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 788, n.\xc2\xa026, do not apply equally to regulations of corporate participation in referenda. A referendum cannot owe a political debt to a corporation, seek to curry favor with a corporation, or fear the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s retaliation. Cf. Austin, 494 U. S., at 678 (Stevens, J., concurring); Citizens Against Rent Control/Coalition for Fair Housing v. Berkeley, 454 U. S. 290, 299 (1981). The majority likewise overlooks the fact that, over the past 30 years, our cases have repeatedly recognized the candidate/issue distinction. See, e.g., Austin, 494 U. S., at 659; NCPAC, 470 U. S., at 495\xe2\x80\x93496; FCC v. League of Women Voters of Cal., 468 U. S. 364, 371, n.\xc2\xa09 (1984); NRWC, 459 U. S., at 210, n.\xc2\xa07. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s critique of Bellotti\xe2\x80\x99s footnote 26 puts it in the strange position of trying to elevate Bellotti to canonical status, while simultaneously disparaging a critical piece of its analysis as unsupported and irreconcilable with Buckley. Bellotti, apparently, is both the font of all wisdom and internally incoherent.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Bellotti Court confronted a dramatically different factual situation from the one that confronts us in this case: a state statute that barred business corporations\xe2\x80\x99 expenditures on some referenda but not others. Specifically, the statute barred a business corporation \xe2\x80\x9cfrom making contributions or expenditures \xe2\x80\x98for the purpose of \xe2\x80\xa6 influencing or affecting the vote on any question submitted to the voters, other than one materially affecting any of the property, business or assets of the corporation,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 768 (quoting Mass. Gen. Laws Ann., ch. 55, \xc2\xa78 (West Supp. 1977); alteration in original), and it went so far as to provide that referenda related to income taxation would not \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98be deemed materially to affect the property, business or assets of the corporation,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 435 U. S., at 768. As might be guessed, the legislature had enacted this statute in order to limit corporate speech on a proposed state constitutional amendment to authorize a graduated income tax. The statute was a transparent attempt to prevent corporations from spending money to defeat this amendment, which was favored by a majority of legislators but had been repeatedly rejected by the voters. See id., at 769\xe2\x80\x93770, and n.\xc2\xa03. We said that \xe2\x80\x9cwhere, as here, the legislature\xe2\x80\x99s suppression of speech suggests an attempt to give one side of a debatable public question an advantage in expressing its views to the people, the First Amendment is plainly offended.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 785\xe2\x80\x93786 (footnote omitted).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Bellotti thus involved a viewpoint-discriminatory statute, created to effect a particular policy outcome. Even Justice Rehnquist, in dissent, had to acknowledge that \xe2\x80\x9ca very persuasive argument could be made that the [Massachusetts Legislature], desiring to impose a personal income tax but more than once defeated in that desire by the combination of the Commonwealth\xe2\x80\x99s referendum provision and corporate expenditures in opposition to such a tax, simply decided to muzzle corporations on this sort of issue so that it could succeed in its desire.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 827, n.\xc2\xa06. To make matters worse, the law at issue did not make any allowance for corporations to spend money through PACs. Id., at 768, n.\xc2\xa02 (opinion of the Court). This really was a complete ban on a specific, preidentified subject. See MCFL, 479 U. S., at 259, n.\xc2\xa012 (stating that 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s expenditure restriction \xe2\x80\x9cis of course distinguishable from the complete foreclosure of any opportunity for political speech that we invalidated in the state referendum context in \xe2\x80\xa6 Bellotti\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority grasps a quotational straw from Bellotti, that speech does not fall entirely outside the protection of the First Amendment merely because it comes from a corporation. Ante, at 30\xe2\x80\x9331. Of course not, but no one suggests the contrary and neither Austin nor McConnell held otherwise. They held that even though the expenditures at issue were subject to First Amendment scrutiny, the restrictions on those expenditures were justified by a compelling state interest. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 205; Austin, 494 U. S., at 658, 660. We acknowledged in Bellotti that numerous \xe2\x80\x9cinterests of the highest importance\xe2\x80\x9d can justify campaign finance regulation. 435 U. S., at 788\xe2\x80\x93789. But we found no evidence that these interests were served by the Massachusetts law. Id., at 789. We left open the possibility that our decision might have been different if there had been \xe2\x80\x9crecord or legislative findings that corporate advocacy threatened imminently to undermine democratic processes, thereby denigrating rather than serving First Amendment interests.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin and McConnell, then, sit perfectly well with Bellotti. Indeed, all six Members of the Austin majority had been on the Court at the time of Bellotti, and none so much as hinted in Austin that they saw any tension between the decisions. The difference between the cases is not that Austin and McConnell rejected First Amendment protection for corporations whereas Bellotti accepted it. The difference is that the statute at issue in Bellotti smacked of viewpoint discrimination, targeted one class of corporations, and provided no PAC option; and the State has a greater interest in regulating independent corporate expenditures on candidate elections than on referenda, because in a functioning democracy the public must have faith that its representatives owe their positions to the people, not to the corporations with the deepest pockets.

\n

*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In sum, over the course of the past century Congress has demonstrated a recurrent need to regulate corporate participation in candidate elections to \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[p]reserv[e] the integrity of the electoral process, preven[t] corruption, \xe2\x80\xa6 sustai[n] the active, alert responsibility of the individual citizen,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d protect the expressive interests of shareholders, and \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[p]reserv[e] \xe2\x80\xa6 the individual citizen\xe2\x80\x99s confidence in government.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 206\xe2\x80\x93207, n.\xc2\xa088 (quoting Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 788\xe2\x80\x93789; first alteration in original). These understandings provided the combined impetus behind the Tillman Act in 1907, see Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 570\xe2\x80\x93575, the Taft-Hartley Act in 1947, see WRTL, 551 U. S., at 511 (Souter, J., dissenting), FECA in 1971, see NRWC, 459 U. S., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210, and BCRA in 2002, see McConnell, 540 U. S., at 126\xe2\x80\x93132. Continuously for over 100 years, this line of \xe2\x80\x9c[c]ampaign finance reform has been a series of reactions to documented threats to electoral integrity obvious to any voter, posed by large sums of money from corporate or union treasuries.\xe2\x80\x9d WRTL, 551 U. S., at 522 (Souter, J., dissenting). Time and again, we have recognized these realities in approving measures that Congress and the States have taken. None of the cases the majority cites is to the contrary. The only thing new about Austin was the dissent, with its stunning failure to appreciate the legitimacy of interests recognized in the name of democratic integrity since the days of the Progressives.

\n

IV

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Having explained why this is not an appropriate case in which to revisit Austin and McConnell and why these decisions sit perfectly well with \xe2\x80\x9cFirst Amendment principles,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 1, 48, I come at last to the interests that are at stake. The majority recognizes that Austin and McConnell may be defended on anticorruption, antidistortion, and shareholder protection rationales. Ante, at 32\xe2\x80\x9346. It badly errs both in explaining the nature of these rationales, which overlap and complement each other, and in applying them to the case at hand.

\n

The Anticorruption Interest

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Undergirding the majority\xe2\x80\x99s approach to the merits is the claim that the only \xe2\x80\x9csufficiently important governmental interest in preventing corruption or the appearance of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d is one that is \xe2\x80\x9climited to quid pro quo corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 43. This is the same \xe2\x80\x9ccrabbed view of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d that was espoused by Justice Kennedy in McConnell and squarely rejected by the Court in that case. 540 U. S., at 152. While it is true that we have not always spoken about corruption in a clear or consistent voice, the approach taken by the majority cannot be right, in my judgment. It disregards our constitutional history and the fundamental demands of a democratic society.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0On numerous occasions we have recognized Congress\xe2\x80\x99 legitimate interest in preventing the money that is spent on elections from exerting an \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98undue influence on an officeholder\xe2\x80\x99s judgment\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and from creating \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98the appearance of such influence,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d beyond the sphere of quid pro quo relationships. Id., at 150; see also, e.g., id., at 143\xe2\x80\x93144, 152\xe2\x80\x93154; Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 441; Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 389. Corruption can take many forms. Bribery may be the paradigm case. But the difference between selling a vote and selling access is a matter of degree, not kind. And selling access is not qualitatively different from giving special preference to those who spent money on one\xe2\x80\x99s behalf. Corruption operates along a spectrum, and the majority\xe2\x80\x99s apparent belief that quid pro quo arrangements can be neatly demarcated from other improper influences does not accord with the theory or reality of politics. It certainly does not accord with the record Congress developed in passing BCRA, a record that stands as a remarkable testament to the energy and ingenuity with which corporations, unions, lobbyists, and politicians may go about scratching each other\xe2\x80\x99s backs\xe2\x80\x94and which amply supported Congress\xe2\x80\x99 determination to target a limited set of especially destructive practices.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The District Court that adjudicated the initial challenge to BCRA pored over this record. In a careful analysis, Judge Kollar-Kotelly made numerous findings about the corrupting consequences of corporate and union independent expenditures in the years preceding BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s passage. See McConnell, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 555\xe2\x80\x93560, 622\xe2\x80\x93625; see also id., at 804\xe2\x80\x93805, 813, n.\xc2\xa0143 (Leon, J.) (indicating agreement). As summarized in her own words:

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe factual findings of the Court illustrate that corporations and labor unions routinely notify Members of Congress as soon as they air electioneering communications relevant to the Members\xe2\x80\x99 elections. The record also indicates that Members express appreciation to organizations for the airing of these election-related advertisements. Indeed, Members of Congress are particularly grateful when negative issue advertisements are run by these organizations, leaving the candidates free to run positive advertisements and be seen as \xe2\x80\x98above the fray.\xe2\x80\x99 Political consultants testify that campaigns are quite aware of who is running advertisements on the candidate\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, when they are being run, and where they are being run. Likewise, a prominent lobbyist testifies that these organizations use issue advocacy as a means to influence various Members of Congress.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe Findings also demonstrate that Members of Congress seek to have corporations and unions run these advertisements on their behalf. The Findings show that Members suggest that corporations or individuals make donations to interest groups with the understanding that the money contributed to these groups will assist the Member in a campaign. After the election, these organizations often seek credit for their support\xe2\x80\xa6\xc2\xa0. Finally, a large majority of Americans (80%) are of the view that corporations and other organizations that engage in electioneering communications, which benefit specific elected officials, receive special consideration from those officials when matters arise that affect these corporations and organizations.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 623\xe2\x80\x93624 (citations and footnote omitted).

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Many of the relationships of dependency found by Judge Kollar-Kotelly seemed to have a quid pro quo basis, but other arrangements were more subtle. Her analysis shows the great difficulty in delimiting the precise scope of the quid pro quo category, as well as the adverse consequences that all such arrangements may have. There are threats of corruption that are far more destructive to a democratic society than the odd bribe. Yet the majority\xe2\x80\x99s understanding of corruption would leave lawmakers impotent to address all but the most discrete abuses.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Our \xe2\x80\x9cundue influence\xe2\x80\x9d cases have allowed the American people to cast a wider net through legislative experiments designed to ensure, to some minimal extent, \xe2\x80\x9cthat officeholders will decide issues \xe2\x80\xa6 on the merits or the desires of their constituencies,\xe2\x80\x9d and not \xe2\x80\x9caccording to the wishes of those who have made large financial contributions\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94or expenditures\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cvalued by the officeholder.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 153.[Footnote 63] When private interests are seen to exert outsized control over officeholders solely on account of the money spent on (or withheld from) their campaigns, the result can depart so thoroughly \xe2\x80\x9cfrom what is pure or correct\xe2\x80\x9d in the conduct of Government, Webster\xe2\x80\x99s Third New International Dictionary 512 (1966) (defining \xe2\x80\x9ccorruption\xe2\x80\x9d), that it amounts to a \xe2\x80\x9csubversion \xe2\x80\xa6 of the electoral process,\xe2\x80\x9d Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 575. At stake in the legislative efforts to address this threat is therefore not only the legitimacy and quality of Government but also the public\xe2\x80\x99s faith therein, not only \xe2\x80\x9cthe capacity of this democracy to represent its constituents [but also] the confidence of its citizens in their capacity to govern themselves,\xe2\x80\x9d WRTL, 551 U. S., at 507 (Souter, J., dissenting). \xe2\x80\x9cTake away Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to regulate the appearance of undue influence and \xe2\x80\x98the cynical assumption that large donors call the tune could jeopardize the willingness of voters to take part in democratic governance.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 144 (quoting Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 390).[Footnote 64]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The cluster of interrelated interests threatened by such undue influence and its appearance has been well captured under the rubric of \xe2\x80\x9cdemocratic integrity.\xe2\x80\x9d WRTL, 551 U. S., at 522 (Souter, J., dissenting). This value has underlined a century of state and federal efforts to regulate the role of corporations in the electoral process.[Footnote 65]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Unlike the majority\xe2\x80\x99s myopic focus on quid pro quo scenarios and the free-floating \xe2\x80\x9cFirst Amendment principles\xe2\x80\x9d on which it rests so much weight, ante, at 1, 48, this broader understanding of corruption has deep roots in the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s history. \xe2\x80\x9cDuring debates on the earliest [campaign finance] reform acts, the terms \xe2\x80\x98corruption\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98undue influence\xe2\x80\x99 were used nearly interchangeably.\xe2\x80\x9d Pasquale, Reclaiming Egalitarianism in the Political Theory of Campaign Finance Reform, 2008 U. Ill. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 599, 601. Long before Buckley, we appreciated that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o say that Congress is without power to pass appropriate legislation to safeguard \xe2\x80\xa6 an election from the improper use of money to influence the result is to deny to the nation in a vital particular the power of self protection.\xe2\x80\x9d Burroughs v. United States, 290 U. S. 534, 545 (1934). And whereas we have no evidence to support the notion that the Framers would have wanted corporations to have the same rights as natural persons in the electoral context, we have ample evidence to suggest that they would have been appalled by the evidence of corruption that Congress unearthed in developing BCRA and that the Court today discounts to irrelevance. It is fair to say that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he Framers were obsessed with corruption,\xe2\x80\x9d Teachout 348, which they understood to encompass the dependency of public officeholders on private interests, see id., at 373\xe2\x80\x93374; see also Randall, 548 U. S., at 280 (Stevens, J., dissenting). They discussed corruption \xe2\x80\x9cmore often in the Constitutional Convention than factions, violence, or instability.\xe2\x80\x9d Teachout 352. When they brought our constitutional order into being, the Framers had their minds trained on a threat to republican self-government that this Court has lost sight of.

\n

Quid Pro Quo Corruption

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is no need to take my side in the debate over the scope of the anticorruption interest to see that the Court\xe2\x80\x99s merits holding is wrong. Even under the majority\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9ccrabbed view of corruption,\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 152, the Government should not lose this case.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cThe importance of the governmental interest in preventing [corruption through the creation of political debts] has never been doubted.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 788, n.\xc2\xa026. Even in the cases that have construed the anticorruption interest most narrowly, we have never suggested that such quid pro quo debts must take the form of outright vote buying or bribes, which have long been distinct crimes. Rather, they encompass the myriad ways in which outside parties may induce an officeholder to confer a legislative benefit in direct response to, or anticipation of, some outlay of money the parties have made or will make on behalf of the officeholder. See McConnell, 540 U. S., at 143 (\xe2\x80\x9cWe have not limited [the anticorruption] interest to the elimination of cash-for-votes exchanges. In Buckley, we expressly rejected the argument that antibribery laws provided a less restrictive alternative to FECA\xe2\x80\x99s contribution limits, noting that such laws \xe2\x80\x98deal[t] with only the most blatant and specific attempts of those with money to influence governmental action\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting 424 U. S., at 28; alteration in original)). It has likewise never been doubted that \xe2\x80\x9c[o]f almost equal concern as the danger of actual quid pro quo arrangements is the impact of the appearance of corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 27. Congress may \xe2\x80\x9clegitimately conclude that the avoidance of the appearance of improper influence is also critical \xe2\x80\xa6 if confidence in the system of representative Government is not to be eroded to a disastrous extent.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (internal quotation marks omitted; alteration in original). A democracy cannot function effectively when its constituent members believe laws are being bought and sold.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In theory, our colleagues accept this much. As applied to BCRA \xc2\xa7203, however, they conclude \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he anticorruption interest is not sufficient to displace the speech here in question.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 41.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Although the Court suggests that Buckley compels its conclusion, ante, at 40\xe2\x80\x9344, Buckley cannot sustain this reading. It is true that, in evaluating FECA\xe2\x80\x99s ceiling on independent expenditures by all persons, the Buckley Court found the governmental interest in preventing corruption \xe2\x80\x9cinadequate.\xe2\x80\x9d 424 U. S., at 45. But Buckley did not evaluate corporate expenditures specifically, nor did it rule out the possibility that a future Court might find otherwise. The opinion reasoned that an expenditure limitation covering only express advocacy (i.e., magic words) would likely be ineffectual, ibid., a problem that Congress tackled in BCRA, and it concluded that \xe2\x80\x9cthe independent advocacy restricted by [FECA \xc2\xa7608(e)(1)] does not presently appear to pose dangers of real or apparent corruption comparable to those identified with large campaign contributions,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 46 (emphasis added). Buckley expressly contemplated that an anticorruption rationale might justify restrictions on independent expenditures at a later date, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause it may be that, in some circumstances, \xe2\x80\x98large independent expenditures pose the same dangers of actual or apparent quid pro quo arrangements as do large contributions.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d WRTL, 551 U. S., at 478 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.) (quoting Buckley, 424 U. S., at 45). Certainly Buckley did not foreclose this possibility with respect to electioneering communications made with corporate general treasury funds, an issue the Court had no occasion to consider.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Austin Court did not rest its holding on quid pro quo corruption, as it found the broader corruption implicated by the antidistortion and shareholder protection rationales a sufficient basis for Michigan\xe2\x80\x99s restriction on corporate electioneering. 494 U. S., at 658\xe2\x80\x93660. Concurring in that opinion, I took the position that \xe2\x80\x9cthe danger of either the fact, or the appearance, of quid pro quo relationships [also] provides an adequate justification for state regulation\xe2\x80\x9d of these independent expenditures. Id., at 678. I did not see this position as inconsistent with Buckley\xe2\x80\x99s analysis of individual expenditures. Corporations, as a class, tend to be more attuned to the complexities of the legislative process and more directly affected by tax and appropriations measures that receive little public scrutiny; they also have vastly more money with which to try to buy access and votes. See Supp. Brief for Appellee 17 (stating that the Fortune 100 companies earned revenues of $13.1 trillion during the last election cycle). Business corporations must engage the political process in instrumental terms if they are to maximize shareholder value. The unparalleled resources, professional lobbyists, and single-minded focus they bring to this effort, I believed, make quid pro quo corruption and its appearance inherently more likely when they (or their conduits or trade groups) spend unrestricted sums on elections.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is with regret rather than satisfaction that I can now say that time has borne out my concerns. The legislative and judicial proceedings relating to BCRA generated a substantial body of evidence suggesting that, as corporations grew more and more adept at crafting \xe2\x80\x9cissue ads\xe2\x80\x9d to help or harm a particular candidate, these nominally independent expenditures began to corrupt the political process in a very direct sense. The sponsors of these ads were routinely granted special access after the campaign was over; \xe2\x80\x9ccandidates and officials knew who their friends were,\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 129. Many corporate independent expenditures, it seemed, had become essentially interchangeable with direct contributions in their capacity to generate quid pro quo arrangements. In an age in which money and television ads are the coin of the campaign realm, it is hardly surprising that corporations deployed these ads to curry favor with, and to gain influence over, public officials.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority appears to think it decisive that the BCRA record does not contain \xe2\x80\x9cdirect examples of votes being exchanged for \xe2\x80\xa6 expenditures.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 45 (internal quotation marks omitted). It would have been quite remarkable if Congress had created a record detailing such behavior by its own Members. Proving that a specific vote was exchanged for a specific expenditure has always been next to impossible: Elected officials have diverse motivations, and no one will acknowledge that he sold a vote. Yet, even if \xe2\x80\x9c[i]ngratiation and access \xe2\x80\xa6 are not corruption\xe2\x80\x9d themselves, ibid., they are necessary prerequisites to it; they can create both the opportunity for, and the appearance of, quid pro quo arrangements. The influx of unlimited corporate money into the electoral realm also creates new opportunities for the mirror image of quid pro quo deals: threats, both explicit and implicit. Starting today, corporations with large war chests to deploy on electioneering may find democratically elected bodies becoming much more attuned to their interests. The majority both misreads the facts and draws the wrong conclusions when it suggests that the BCRA record provides \xe2\x80\x9conly scant evidence that independent expenditures \xe2\x80\xa6 ingratiate,\xe2\x80\x9d and that, \xe2\x80\x9cin any event,\xe2\x80\x9d none of it matters. Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In her analysis of the record, Judge Kollar-Kotelly documented the pervasiveness of this ingratiation and explained its significance under the majority\xe2\x80\x99s own touchstone for defining the scope of the anticorruption rationale, Buckley. See McConnell, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 555\xe2\x80\x93560, 622\xe2\x80\x93625. Witnesses explained how political parties and candidates used corporate independent expenditures to circumvent FECA\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9chard-money\xe2\x80\x9d limitations. See, e.g., id., at 478\xe2\x80\x93479. One former Senator candidly admitted to the District Court that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[c]andidates whose campaigns benefit from [phony \xe2\x80\x9cissue ads\xe2\x80\x9d] greatly appreciate the help of these groups. In fact, Members will also be favorably disposed to those who finance these groups when they later seek access to discuss pending legislation.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 556 (quoting declaration of Sen. Dale Bumpers). One prominent lobbyist went so far as to state, in uncontroverted testimony, that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98unregulated expenditures\xe2\x80\x94whether soft money donations to the parties or issue ad campaigns\xe2\x80\x94can sometimes generate far more influence than direct campaign contributions.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (quoting declaration of Wright Andrews; emphasis added). In sum, Judge Kollar-Kotelly found, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he record powerfully demonstrates that electioneering communications paid for with the general treasury funds of labor unions and corporations endears those entities to elected officials in a way that could be perceived by the public as corrupting.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 622\xe2\x80\x93623. She concluded that the Government\xe2\x80\x99s interest in preventing the appearance of corruption, as that concept was defined in Buckley, was itself sufficient to uphold BCRA \xc2\xa7203. 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d, at 622\xe2\x80\x93625. Judge Leon agreed. See id., at 804\xe2\x80\x93805 (dissenting only with re-\nspect to the Wellstone Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s coverage of MCFL corporations).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0When the McConnell Court affirmed the judgment of the District Court regarding \xc2\xa7203, we did not rest our holding on a narrow notion of quid pro quo corruption. Instead we relied on the governmental interest in combating the unique forms of corruption threatened by corporations, as recognized in Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion and shareholder protection rationales, 540 U. S., at 205 (citing Austin, 494 U. S., at 660), as well as the interest in preventing circumvention of contribution limits, 540 U. S., at 128\xe2\x80\x93129, 205, 206, n.\xc2\xa088. Had we felt constrained by the view of today\xe2\x80\x99s Court that quid pro quo corruption and its appearance are the only interests that count in this field, ante, at 32\xe2\x80\x9346, we of course would have looked closely at that issue. And as the analysis by Judge Kollar-Kotelly reflects, it is a very real possibility that we would have found one or both of those interests satisfied and \xc2\xa7203 appropriately tailored to them.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s rejection of the Buckley anticorruption rationale on the ground that independent corporate expenditures \xe2\x80\x9cdo not give rise to [quid pro quo] corruption or the appearance of corruption,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 42, is thus unfair as well as unreasonable. Congress and outside experts have generated significant evidence corroborating this rationale, and the only reason we do not have any of the relevant materials before us is that the Government had no reason to develop a record at trial for a facial challenge the plaintiff had abandoned. The Court cannot both sU. S.onte choose to relitigate McConnell on appeal and then complain that the Government has failed to substantiate its case. If our colleagues were really serious about the interest in preventing quid pro quo corruption, they would remand to the District Court with instructions to commence evidentiary proceedings.[Footnote 66]

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The insight that even technically independent expenditures can be corrupting in much the same way as direct contributions is bolstered by our decision last year in Caperton v. A. T. Massey Coal Co., 556 U. S. ___ (2009). In that case, Don Blankenship, the chief executive officer of a corporation with a lawsuit pending before the West Virginia high court, spent large sums on behalf of a particular candidate, Brent Benjamin, running for a seat on that court. \xe2\x80\x9cIn addition to contributing the $1,000 statutory maximum to Benjamin\xe2\x80\x99s campaign committee, Blankenship donated almost $2.5 million to \xe2\x80\x98And For The Sake Of The Kids,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d a \xc2\xa7527 corporation that ran ads targeting Benjamin\xe2\x80\x99s opponent. Id., at ___ (slip op., at 2). \xe2\x80\x9cThis was not all. Blankenship spent, in addition, just over $500,000 on independent expenditures \xe2\x80\xa6 \xe2\x80\x98\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cto support \xe2\x80\xa6 Brent Benjamin.\xe2\x80\x9d\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at ___ (slip op., at 2\xe2\x80\x933) (second alteration in original). Applying its common sense, this Court accepted petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 argument that Blankenship\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cpivotal role in getting Justice Benjamin elected created a constitutionally intolerable probability of actual bias\xe2\x80\x9d when Benjamin later declined to recuse himself from the appeal by Blankenship\xe2\x80\x99s corporation. Id., at ___ (slip op., at 11). \xe2\x80\x9cThough n[o] \xe2\x80\xa6 bribe or criminal influence\xe2\x80\x9d was involved, we recognized that \xe2\x80\x9cJustice Benjamin would nevertheless feel a debt of gratitude to Blankenship for his extraordinary efforts to get him elected.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. \xe2\x80\x9cThe difficulties of inquiring into actual bias,\xe2\x80\x9d we further noted, \xe2\x80\x9csimply underscore the need for objective rules,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at ___ (slip op., at 13)\xe2\x80\x94rules which will perforce turn on the appearance of bias rather than its actual existence.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In Caperton, then, we accepted the premise that, at least in some circumstances, independent expenditures on candidate elections will raise an intolerable specter of quid pro quo corruption. Indeed, this premise struck the Court as so intuitive that it repeatedly referred to Blankenship\xe2\x80\x99s spending on behalf of Benjamin\xe2\x80\x94spending that consisted of 99.97% independent expenditures ($3 million) and 0.03% direct contributions ($1,000)\xe2\x80\x94as a \xe2\x80\x9ccontribution.\xe2\x80\x9d See, e.g., id., at ___ (slip op., at 1) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe basis for the [recusal] motion was that the justice had received campaign contributions in an extraordinary amount from\xe2\x80\x9d Blankenship); id., at ___ (slip op., at 3) (referencing \xe2\x80\x9cBlankenship\xe2\x80\x99s $3 million in contributions\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at ___ (slip op., at 14) (\xe2\x80\x9cBlankenship contributed some $3 million to unseat the incumbent and replace him with Benjamin\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at ___ (slip op., at 15) (\xe2\x80\x9cBlankenship\xe2\x80\x99s campaign contributions \xe2\x80\xa6 had a significant and disproportionate influence on the electoral outcome\xe2\x80\x9d). The reason the Court so thoroughly conflated expenditures and contributions, one assumes, is that it realized that some expenditures may be functionally equivalent to contributions in the way they influence the outcome of a race, the way they are interpreted by the candidates and the public, and the way they taint the decisions that the officeholder thereafter takes.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Caperton is illuminating in several additional respects. It underscores the old insight that, on account of the extreme difficulty of proving corruption, \xe2\x80\x9cprophylactic measures, reaching some [campaign spending] not corrupt in purpose or effect, [may be] nonetheless required to guard against corruption.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 30; see also Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 392, n.\xc2\xa05. It underscores that \xe2\x80\x9ccertain restrictions on corporate electoral involvement\xe2\x80\x9d may likewise be needed to \xe2\x80\x9chedge against circumvention of valid contribution limits.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 205 (internal quotation marks and brackets omitted); see also Colorado II, 533 U. S., at 456 (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]ll Members of the Court agree that circumvention is a valid theory of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d). It underscores that for-profit corporations associated with electioneering communications will often prefer to use nonprofit conduits with \xe2\x80\x9cmisleading names,\xe2\x80\x9d such as And For The Sake Of The Kids, \xe2\x80\x9cto conceal their identity\xe2\x80\x9d as the sponsor of those communications, thereby frustrating the utility of disclosure laws. McConnell, 540 U. S., at 128; see also id., at 196\xe2\x80\x93197.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0And it underscores that the consequences of today\xe2\x80\x99s holding will not be limited to the legislative or executive context. The majority of the States select their judges through popular elections. At a time when concerns about the conduct of judicial elections have reached a fever pitch, see, e.g., O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, Justice for Sale, Wall St. Journal, Nov. 15, 2007, p. A25; Brief for Justice at Stake et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 2, the Court today unleashes the floodgates of corporate and union general treasury spending in these races. Perhaps \xe2\x80\x9cCaperton motions\xe2\x80\x9d will catch some of the worst abuses. This will be small comfort to those States that, after today, may no longer have the ability to place modest limits on corporate electioneering even if they believe such limits to be critical to maintaining the integrity of their judicial systems.

\n

Deference and Incumbent Self-Protection

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Rather than show any deference to a coordinate branch of Government, the majority thus rejects the anticorruption rationale without serious analysis.[Footnote 67] Today\xe2\x80\x99s opinion provides no clear rationale for being so dismissive of Congress, but the prior individual opinions on which it relies have offered one: the incentives of the legislators who passed BCRA. Section 203, our colleagues have suggested, may be little more than \xe2\x80\x9can incumbency protection plan,\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 306 (Kennedy, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); see also id., at 249\xe2\x80\x93250, 260\xe2\x80\x93263 (Scalia, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part), a disreputable attempt at legislative self-dealing rather than an earnest effort to facilitate First Amendment values and safeguard the legitimacy of our political system. This possibility, the Court apparently believes, licenses it to run roughshod over Congress\xe2\x80\x99 handiwork.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In my view, we should instead start by acknowledging that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress surely has both wisdom and experience in these matters that is far superior to ours.\xe2\x80\x9d Colorado Republican Federal Campaign Comm. v. FEC, 518 U. S. 604, 650 (1996) (Stevens, J., dissenting). Many of our campaign finance precedents explicitly and forcefully affirm the propriety of such presumptive deference. See, e.g., McConnell, 540 U. S., at 158; Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 155\xe2\x80\x93156; NRWC, 459 U. S., at 209\xe2\x80\x93210. Moreover, \xe2\x80\x9c[j]udicial deference is particularly warranted where, as here, we deal with a congressional judgment that has remained essentially unchanged throughout a century of careful legislative adjustment.\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 162, n.\xc2\xa09 (internal quotation marks omitted); cf. Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 391 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe quantum of empirical evidence needed to satisfy heightened judicial scrutiny of legislative judgments will vary up or down with the novelty and plausibility of the justification raised\xe2\x80\x9d).
