In America, incumbent legislators pass the laws that govern campaign finance, just like all other laws. To apply a level of scrutiny that effectively bars them from regulating electioneering whenever there is the faintest whiff of self-interest, is to deprive them of the ability to regulate\nelectioneering.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0This is not to say that deference would be appropriate if there were a solid basis for believing that a legislative action was motivated by the desire to protect incumbents or that it will degrade the competitiveness of the electoral process.[Footnote 68] See League of United Latin American Citizens v. Perry, 548 U. S. 399, 447 (2006) (Stevens, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part); Vieth v. Jubelirer, 541 U. S. 267, 317 (2004) (Stevens, J., dissenting). Along with our duty to balance competing constitutional concerns, we have a vital role to play in ensuring that elections remain at least minimally open, fair, and competitive. But it is the height of recklessness to dismiss Congress\xe2\x80\x99 years of bipartisan deliberation and its reasoned judgment on this basis, without first confirming that the statute in question was intended to be, or will function as, a restraint on electoral competition. \xe2\x80\x9cAbsent record evidence of invidious discrimination against challengers as a class, a court should generally be hesitant to invalidate legislation which on its face imposes evenhanded restrictions.\xe2\x80\x9d Buckley, 424 U. S., at 31.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0We have no record evidence from which to conclude that BCRA \xc2\xa7203, or any of the dozens of state laws that the Court today calls into question, reflects or fosters such invidious discrimination. Our colleagues have opined that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98any restriction upon a type of campaign speech that is equally available to challengers and incumbents tends to favor incumbents.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 249 (opinion of Scalia, J.). This kind of airy speculation could easily be turned on its head. The electioneering prohibited by \xc2\xa7203 might well tend to favor incumbents, because incumbents have pre-existing relationships with corporations and unions, and groups that wish to procure legislative benefits may tend to support the candidate who, as a sitting officeholder, is already in a position to dispense benefits and is statistically likely to retain office. If a corporation\xe2\x80\x99s goal is to induce officeholders to do its bidding, the corporation would do well to cultivate stable, long-term relationships of dependency.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0So we do not have a solid theoretical basis for condemning \xc2\xa7203 as a front for incumbent self-protection, and it seems equally if not more plausible that restrictions on corporate electioneering will be self-denying. Nor do we have a good empirical case for skepticism, as the Court\xe2\x80\x99s failure to cite any empirical research attests. Nor does the legislative history give reason for concern. Congress devoted years of careful study to the issues underlying BCRA; \xe2\x80\x9c[f]ew legislative proposals in recent years have received as much sustained public commentary or news coverage\xe2\x80\x9d; \xe2\x80\x9c[p]olitical scientists and academic experts \xe2\x80\xa6 with no self-interest in incumbent protectio[n] were central figures in pressing the case for BCRA\xe2\x80\x9d; and the legislation commanded bipartisan support from the outset. Pildes, The Supreme Court 2003 Term Foreword: The Constitutionalization of Democratic Politics, 118 Harv. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 28, 137 (2004). Finally, it is important to remember just how incumbent-friendly congressional races were prior to BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s passage. As the Solicitor General aptly remarked at the time, \xe2\x80\x9cthe evidence supports overwhelmingly that incumbents were able to get re-elected under the old system just fine.\xe2\x80\x9d Tr. of Oral Arg. in McConnell v. FEC, O. T. 2003, No. 02\xe2\x80\x931674, p. 61. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be hard to develop a scheme that could be better for incumbents.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 63.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In this case, then, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no convincing evidence that th[e] important interests favoring expenditure limits are fronts for incumbency protection.\xe2\x80\x9d Randall, 548 U. S., at 279 (Stevens, J., dissenting). \xe2\x80\x9cIn the meantime, a legislative judgment that \xe2\x80\x98enough is enough\xe2\x80\x99 should command the greatest possible deference from judges interpreting a constitutional provision that, at best, has an indirect relationship to activity that affects the quantity \xe2\x80\xa6 of repetitive speech in the marketplace of ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 279\xe2\x80\x93280. The majority cavalierly ignores Congress\xe2\x80\x99 factual findings and its constitutional judgment: It acknowledges the validity of the interest in preventing corruption, but it effectively discounts the value of that interest to zero. This is quite different from conscientious policing for impermissibly anticompetitive motive or effect in a sensitive First Amendment context. It is the denial of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to regulate corporate spending on\nelections.

\n

Austin and Corporate Expenditures

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Just as the majority gives short shrift to the general societal interests at stake in campaign finance regulation, it also overlooks the distinctive considerations raised by the regulation of corporate expenditures. The majority fails to appreciate that Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale is itself an anticorruption rationale, see 494 U. S., at 660 (describing \xe2\x80\x9ca different type of corruption\xe2\x80\x9d), tied to the special concerns raised by corporations. Understood properly, \xe2\x80\x9cantidistortion\xe2\x80\x9d is simply a variant on the classic governmental interest in protecting against improper influences on officeholders that debilitate the democratic process. It is manifestly not just an \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98equalizing\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ideal in disguise. Ante, at 34 (quoting Buckley, 424 U. S., at 48).[Footnote 69]

\n

1.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 Antidistortion

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The fact that corporations are different from human beings might seem to need no elaboration, except that the majority opinion almost completely elides it. Austin set forth some of the basic differences. Unlike natural persons, corporations have \xe2\x80\x9climited liability\xe2\x80\x9d for their owners and managers, \xe2\x80\x9cperpetual life,\xe2\x80\x9d separation of ownership and control, \xe2\x80\x9cand favorable treatment of the accumulation and distribution of assets \xe2\x80\xa6 that enhance their ability to attract capital and to deploy their resources in ways that maximize the return on their shareholders\xe2\x80\x99 investments.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 658\xe2\x80\x93659. Unlike voters in U. S. elections, corporations may be foreign controlled.[Footnote 70] Unlike other interest groups, business corporations have been \xe2\x80\x9ceffectively delegated responsibility for ensuring society\xe2\x80\x99s economic welfare\xe2\x80\x9d;[Footnote 71] they inescapably structure the life of every citizen. \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[T]he resources in the treasury of a business corporation,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d furthermore, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98are not an indication of popular support for the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s political ideas.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 659 (quoting MCFL, 479 U. S., at 258). \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98They reflect instead the economically motivated decisions of investors and customers. The availability of these resources may make a corporation a formidable political presence, even though the power of the corporation may be no reflection of the power of its ideas.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 659 (quoting MCFL, 479 U. S., at 258).[Footnote 72]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It might also be added that corporations have no consciences, no beliefs, no feelings, no thoughts, no desires. Corporations help structure and facilitate the activities of human beings, to be sure, and their \xe2\x80\x9cpersonhood\xe2\x80\x9d often serves as a useful legal fiction. But they are not themselves members of \xe2\x80\x9cWe the People\xe2\x80\x9d by whom and for whom our Constitution was established.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These basic points help explain why corporate electioneering is not only more likely to impair compelling governmental interests, but also why restrictions on that electioneering are less likely to encroach upon First Amendment freedoms. One fundamental concern of the First Amendment is to \xe2\x80\x9cprotec[t] the individual\xe2\x80\x99s interest in self-expression.\xe2\x80\x9d Consolidated Edison Co. of N.\xc2\xa0Y. v. Public Serv. Comm\xe2\x80\x99n of N.\xc2\xa0Y., 447 U. S. 530, 534, n.\xc2\xa02 (1980); see also Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 777, n.\xc2\xa012. Freedom of speech helps \xe2\x80\x9cmake men free to develop their faculties,\xe2\x80\x9d Whitney v. California, 274 U. S. 357, 375 (1927) (Brandeis, J., concurring), it respects their \xe2\x80\x9cdignity and choice,\xe2\x80\x9d Cohen v. California, 403 U. S. 15, 24 (1971), and it facilitates the value of \xe2\x80\x9cindividual self-realization,\xe2\x80\x9d Redish, The Value of Free Speech, 130 U.\xc2\xa0Pa. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 591, 594 (1982). Corporate speech, however, is derivative speech, speech by proxy. A regulation such as BCRA \xc2\xa7203 may affect the way in which individuals disseminate certain messages through the corporate form, but it does not prevent anyone from speaking in his or her own voice. \xe2\x80\x9cWithin the realm of [campaign spending] generally,\xe2\x80\x9d corporate spending is \xe2\x80\x9cfurthest from the core of political expression.\xe2\x80\x9d Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 161, n.\xc2\xa08.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It is an interesting question \xe2\x80\x9cwho\xe2\x80\x9d is even speaking when a business corporation places an advertisement that endorses or attacks a particular candidate. Presumably it is not the customers or employees, who typically have no say in such matters. It cannot realistically be said to be the shareholders, who tend to be far removed from the day-to-day decisions of the firm and whose political preferences may be opaque to management. Perhaps the officers or directors of the corporation have the best claim to be the ones speaking, except their fiduciary duties generally prohibit them from using corporate funds for personal ends. Some individuals associated with the corporation must make the decision to place the ad, but the idea that these individuals are thereby fostering their self-expression or cultivating their critical faculties is fanciful. It is entirely possible that the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s electoral message will conflict with their personal convictions. Take away the ability to use general treasury funds for some of those ads, and no one\xe2\x80\x99s autonomy, dignity, or political equality has been impinged upon in the least.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Corporate expenditures are distinguishable from individual expenditures in this respect. I have taken the view that a legislature may place reasonable restrictions on individuals\xe2\x80\x99 electioneering expenditures in the service of the governmental interests explained above, and in recognition of the fact that such restrictions are not direct restraints on speech but rather on its financing. See, e.g., Randall, 548 U. S., at 273 (dissenting opinion). But those restrictions concededly present a tougher case, because the primary conduct of actual, flesh-and-blood persons is involved. Some of those individuals might feel that they need to spend large sums of money on behalf of a particular candidate to vindicate the intensity of their electoral preferences. This is obviously not the situation with business corporations, as their routine practice of giving \xe2\x80\x9csubstantial sums to both major national parties\xe2\x80\x9d makes pellucidly clear. McConnell, 540 U. S., at 148. \xe2\x80\x9c[C]orporate participation\xe2\x80\x9d in elections, any business executive will tell you, \xe2\x80\x9cis more transactional than ideological.\xe2\x80\x9d Supp. Brief for Committee for Economic Development as Amicus Curiae 10.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In this transactional spirit, some corporations have affirmatively urged Congress to place limits on their electioneering communications. These corporations fear that officeholders will shake them down for supportive ads, that they will have to spend increasing sums on elections in an ever-escalating arms race with their competitors, and that public trust in business will be eroded. See id., at 10\xe2\x80\x9319. A system that effectively forces corporations to use their shareholders\xe2\x80\x99 money both to maintain access to, and to avoid retribution from, elected officials may ultimately prove more harmful than beneficial to many corporations. It can impose a kind of implicit tax.[Footnote 73]

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In short, regulations such as \xc2\xa7203 and the statute upheld in Austin impose only a limited burden on First Amendment freedoms not only because they target a narrow subset of expenditures and leave untouched the broader \xe2\x80\x9cpublic dialogue,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 25, but also because they leave untouched the speech of natural persons. Recognizing the weakness of a speaker-based critique of Austin, the Court places primary emphasis not on the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s right to electioneer, but rather on the listener\xe2\x80\x99s interest in hearing what every possible speaker may have to say. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s central argument is that laws such as \xc2\xa7203 have \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98deprived [the electorate] of information, knowledge and opinion vital to its function,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 38 (quoting CIO, 335 U. S., at 144 (Rutledge, J., concurring in judgment)), and this, in turn, \xe2\x80\x9cinterferes with the \xe2\x80\x98open marketplace\xe2\x80\x99 of ideas protected by the First Amendment,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 38 (quoting New York State Bd. of Elections v. Lopez Torres, 552 U. S. 196, 208 (2008)).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There are many flaws in this argument. If the overriding concern depends on the interests of the audience, surely the public\xe2\x80\x99s perception of the value of corporate speech should be given important weight. That perception today is the same as it was a century ago when Theodore Roosevelt delivered the speeches to Congress that, in time, led to the limited prohibition on corporate campaign expenditures that is overruled today. See WRTL, 551 U. S., at 509\xe2\x80\x93510 (Souter, J., dissenting) (summarizing President Roosevelt\xe2\x80\x99s remarks). The distinctive threat to democratic integrity posed by corporate domination of politics was recognized at \xe2\x80\x9cthe inception of the republic\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9chas been a persistent theme in American political life\xe2\x80\x9d ever since. Regan 302. It is only certain Members of this Court, not the listeners themselves, who have agitated for more corporate electioneering.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Austin recognized that there are substantial reasons why a legislature might conclude that unregulated general treasury expenditures will give corporations \xe2\x80\x9cunfai[r] influence\xe2\x80\x9d in the electoral process, 494 U. S., at 660, and distort public debate in ways that undermine rather than advance the interests of listeners. The legal structure of corporations allows them to amass and deploy financial resources on a scale few natural persons can match. The structure of a business corporation, furthermore, draws a line between the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s economic interests and the political preferences of the individuals associated with the corporation; the corporation must engage the electoral process with the aim \xe2\x80\x9cto enhance the profitability of the company, no matter how persuasive the arguments for a broader or conflicting set of priorities,\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for American Independent Business Alliance as Amicus Curiae 11; see also ALI, Principles of Corporate Governance: Analysis and Recommendations \xc2\xa72.01(a), p.\xc2\xa055 (1992) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A] corporation \xe2\x80\xa6 should have as its objective the conduct of business activities with a view to enhancing corporate profit and shareholder gain\xe2\x80\x9d). In a state election such as the one at issue in Austin, the interests of nonresident corporations may be fundamentally adverse to the interests of local voters. Consequently, when corporations grab up the prime broadcasting slots on the eve of an election, they can flood the market with advocacy that bears \xe2\x80\x9clittle or no correlation\xe2\x80\x9d to the ideas of natural persons or to any broader notion of the public good, 494 U. S., at 660. The opinions of real people may be marginalized. \xe2\x80\x9cThe expenditure restrictions of [2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C.] \xc2\xa7441b are thus meant to ensure that competition among actors in the political arena is truly competition among ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d MCFL, 479 U. S., at 259.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In addition to this immediate drowning out of noncorporate voices, there may be deleterious effects that follow soon thereafter. Corporate \xe2\x80\x9cdomination\xe2\x80\x9d of electioneering, Austin, 494 U. S., at 659, can generate the impression that corporations dominate our democracy. When citizens turn on their televisions and radios before an election and hear only corporate electioneering, they may lose faith in their capacity, as citizens, to influence public policy. A Government captured by corporate interests, they may come to believe, will be neither responsive to their needs nor willing to give their views a fair hearing. The predictable result is cynicism and disenchantment: an increased perception that large spenders \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98call the tune\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and a reduced \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98willingness of voters to take part in democratic governance.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 144 (quoting Shrink Missouri, 528 U. S., at 390). To the extent that corporations are allowed to exert undue influence in electoral races, the speech of the eventual winners of those races may also be chilled. Politicians who fear that a certain corporation can make or break their reelection chances may be cowed into silence about that corporation. On a variety of levels, unregulated corporate electioneering might diminish the ability of citizens to \xe2\x80\x9chold officials accountable to the people,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 23, and disserve the goal of a public debate that is \xe2\x80\x9cuninhibited, robust, and wide-open,\xe2\x80\x9d New York Times Co. v. Sullivan, 376 U. S. 254, 270 (1964). At the least, I stress again, a legislature is entitled to credit these concerns and to take tailored measures in response.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s unwillingness to distinguish between corporations and humans similarly blinds it to the possibility that corporations\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cwar chests\xe2\x80\x9d and their special \xe2\x80\x9cadvantages\xe2\x80\x9d in the legal realm, Austin, 494 U. S., at 659, may translate into special advantages in the market for legislation. When large numbers of citizens have a common stake in a measure that is under consideration, it may be very difficult for them to coordinate resources on behalf of their position. The corporate form, by contrast, \xe2\x80\x9cprovides a simple way to channel rents to only those who have paid their dues, as it were. If you do not own stock, you do not benefit from the larger dividends or appreciation in the stock price caused by the passage of private interest legislation.\xe2\x80\x9d Sitkoff, Corporate Political Speech, Political Extortion, and the Competition for Corporate Charters, 69 U. Chi. L. Rev. 1103, 1113 (2002). Corporations, that is, are uniquely equipped to seek laws that favor their owners, not simply because they have a lot of money but because of their legal and organizational structure. Remove all restrictions on their electioneering, and the door may be opened to a type of rent seeking that is \xe2\x80\x9cfar more destructive\xe2\x80\x9d than what noncorporations are capable of. Ibid. It is for reasons such as these that our campaign finance jurisprudence has long appreciated that \xe2\x80\x9cthe \xe2\x80\x98differing structures and purposes\xe2\x80\x99 of different entities \xe2\x80\x98may require different forms of regulation in order to protect the integrity of the electoral process.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d NRWC, 459 U. S., at 210 (quoting California Medical Assn., 453 U. S., at 201).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s facile depiction of corporate electioneering assumes away all of these complexities. Our colleagues ridicule the idea of regulating expenditures based on \xe2\x80\x9cnothing more\xe2\x80\x9d than a fear that corporations have a special \xe2\x80\x9cability to persuade,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 11 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.), as if corporations were our society\xe2\x80\x99s ablest debaters and viewpoint-neutral laws such as \xc2\xa7203 were created to suppress their best arguments. In their haste to knock down yet another straw man, our colleagues simply ignore the fundamental concerns of the Austin Court and the legislatures that have passed laws like \xc2\xa7203: to safeguard the integrity, competitiveness, and democratic responsiveness of the electoral process. All of the majority\xe2\x80\x99s theoretical arguments turn on a proposition with undeniable surface appeal but little grounding in evidence or experience, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there is no such thing as too much speech,\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 695 (Scalia, J., dissenting)).[Footnote 74] If individuals in our society had infinite free time to listen to and contemplate every last bit of speech uttered by anyone, anywhere; and if broadcast advertisements had no special ability to influence elections apart from the merits of their arguments (to the extent they make any); and if legislators always operated with nothing less than perfect virtue; then I suppose the majority\xe2\x80\x99s premise would be sound. In the real world, we have seen, corporate domination of the airwaves prior to an election may decrease the average listener\xe2\x80\x99s exposure to relevant viewpoints, and it may diminish citizens\xe2\x80\x99 willingness and capacity to participate in the democratic process.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0None of this is to suggest that corporations can or should be denied an opportunity to participate in election campaigns or in any other public forum (much less that a work of art such as Mr. Smith Goes to Washington may be banned), or to deny that some corporate speech may contribute significantly to public debate. What it shows, however, is that Austin\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cconcern about corporate domination of the political process,\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 659, reflects more than a concern to protect governmental interests outside of the First Amendment. It also reflects a concern to facilitate First Amendment values by preserving some breathing room around the electoral \xe2\x80\x9cmarketplace\xe2\x80\x9d of ideas, ante, at 19, 34, 38, 52, 54, the marketplace in which the actual people of this Nation determine how they will govern themselves. The majority seems oblivious to the simple truth that laws such as \xc2\xa7203 do not merely pit the anticorruption interest against the First Amendment, but also pit competing First Amendment values against each other. There are, to be sure, serious concerns with any effort to balance the First Amendment rights of speakers against the First Amendment rights of listeners. But when the speakers in question are not real people and when the appeal to \xe2\x80\x9cFirst Amendment principles\xe2\x80\x9d depends almost entirely on the listeners\xe2\x80\x99 perspective, ante, at 1, 48, it becomes necessary to consider how listeners will actually be affected.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0In critiquing Austin\xe2\x80\x99s antidistortion rationale and campaign finance regulation more generally, our colleagues place tremendous weight on the example of media corporations. See ante, at 35\xe2\x80\x9338, 46; ante, at 1, 11 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J.); ante, at 6 (opinion of Scalia, J.). Yet it is not at all clear that Austin would permit \xc2\xa7203 to be applied to them. The press plays a unique role not only in the text, history, and structure of the First Amendment but also in facilitating public discourse; as the Austin Court explained, \xe2\x80\x9cmedia corporations differ significantly from other corporations in that their resources are devoted to the collection of information and its dissemination to the public,\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 667. Our colleagues have raised some interesting and difficult questions about Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to regulate electioneering by the press, and about how to define what constitutes the press. But that is not the case before us. Section 203 does not apply to media corporations, and even if it did, Citizens United is not a media corporation. There would be absolutely no reason to consider the issue of media corporations if the majority did not, first, transform Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s as-applied challenge into a facial challenge and, second, invent the theory that legislatures must eschew all \xe2\x80\x9cidentity\xe2\x80\x9d-based distinctions and treat a local nonprofit news outlet exactly the same as General Motors.[Footnote 75] This calls to mind George Berkeley\xe2\x80\x99s description of philosophers: \xe2\x80\x9c[W]e have first raised a dust and then complain we cannot see.\xe2\x80\x9d Principles of Human Knowledge/Three Dialogues 38, \xc2\xb63 (R. Woolhouse ed. 1988).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0It would be perfectly understandable if our colleagues feared that a campaign finance regulation such as \xc2\xa7203 may be counterproductive or self-interested, and therefore attended carefully to the choices the Legislature has made. But the majority does not bother to consider such practical matters, or even to consult a record; it simply stipulates that \xe2\x80\x9cenlightened self-government\xe2\x80\x9d can arise only in the absence of regulation. Ante, at 23. In light of the distinctive features of corporations identified in Austin, there is no valid basis for this assumption. The marketplace of ideas is not actually a place where items\xe2\x80\x94or laws\xe2\x80\x94are meant to be bought and sold, and when we move from the realm of economics to the realm of corporate electioneering, there may be no \xe2\x80\x9creason to think the market ordering is intrinsically good at all,\xe2\x80\x9d Strauss 1386.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court\xe2\x80\x99s blinkered and aphoristic approach to the First Amendment may well promote corporate power at the cost of the individual and collective self-expression the Amendment was meant to serve. It will undoubtedly cripple the ability of ordinary citizens, Congress, and the States to adopt even limited measures to protect against corporate domination of the electoral process. Americans may be forgiven if they do not feel the Court has advanced the cause of self-government today.

\n

2.\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 Shareholder Protection

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0There is yet another way in which laws such as \xc2\xa7203 can serve First Amendment values. Interwoven with Austin\xe2\x80\x99s concern to protect the integrity of the electoral process is a concern to protect the rights of shareholders from a kind of coerced speech: electioneering expenditures that do not \xe2\x80\x9creflec[t] [their] support.\xe2\x80\x9d 494 U. S., at 660\xe2\x80\x93661. When corporations use general treasury funds to praise or attack a particular candidate for office, it is the shareholders, as the residual claimants, who are effectively footing the bill. Those shareholders who disagree with the corporation\xe2\x80\x99s electoral message may find their financial investments being used to undermine their political convictions.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The PAC mechanism, by contrast, helps assure that those who pay for an electioneering communication actually support its content and that managers do not use general treasuries to advance personal agendas. Ibid. It \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98allows corporate political participation without the temptation to use corporate funds for political influence, quite possibly at odds with the sentiments of some shareholders or members.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 204 (quoting Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 163). A rule that privileges the use of PACs thus does more than facilitate the political speech of like-minded shareholders; it also curbs the rent seeking behavior of executives and respects the views of dissenters. Austin\xe2\x80\x99s acceptance of restrictions on general treasury spending \xe2\x80\x9csimply allows people who have invested in the business corporation for purely economic reasons\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the vast majority of investors, one assumes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cto avoid being taken advantage of, without sacrificing their economic objectives.\xe2\x80\x9d Winkler, Beyond Bellotti, 32 Loyola (LA) L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 133, 201 (1998).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The concern to protect dissenting shareholders and union members has a long history in campaign finance reform. It provided a central motivation for the Tillman Act in 1907 and subsequent legislation, see Pipefitters v. United States, 407 U. S. 385, 414\xe2\x80\x93415 (1972); Winkler, 92 Geo. L.\xc2\xa0J., at 887\xe2\x80\x93900, and it has been endorsed in a long line of our cases, see, e.g., McConnell, 540 U. S., at 204\xe2\x80\x93205; Beaumont, 539 U. S., at 152\xe2\x80\x93154; MCFL, 479 U. S., at 258; NRWC, 459 U. S., at 207\xe2\x80\x93208; Pipefitters, 407 U. S., at 414\xe2\x80\x93416; see also n.\xc2\xa060, supra. Indeed, we have unanimously recognized the governmental interest in \xe2\x80\x9cprotect[ing] the individuals who have paid money into a corporation or union for purposes other than the support of candidates from having that money used to support political candidates to whom they may be opposed.\xe2\x80\x9d NRWC, 459 U. S., at 207\xe2\x80\x93208.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court dismisses this interest on the ground that abuses of shareholder money can be corrected \xe2\x80\x9cthrough the procedures of corporate democracy,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 46 (internal quotation marks omitted), and, it seems, through Internet-based disclosures, ante, at 55.[Footnote 76] I fail to understand how this addresses the concerns of dissenting union members, who will also be affected by today\xe2\x80\x99s ruling, and I fail to understand why the Court is so confident in these mechanisms. By \xe2\x80\x9ccorporate democracy,\xe2\x80\x9d presumably the Court means the rights of shareholders to vote and to bring derivative suits for breach of fiduciary duty. In practice, however, many corporate lawyers will tell you that \xe2\x80\x9cthese rights are so limited as to be almost nonexistent,\xe2\x80\x9d given the internal authority wielded by boards and managers and the expansive protections afforded by the business judgment rule. Blair & Stout 320; see also id., at 298\xe2\x80\x93315; Winkler, 32 Loyola (LA) L.\xc2\xa0Rev., at 165\xe2\x80\x93166, 199\xe2\x80\x93200. Modern technology may help make it easier to track corporate activity, including electoral advocacy, but it is utopian to believe that it solves the problem. Most American households that own stock do so through intermediaries such as mutual funds and pension plans, see Evans, A Requiem for the Retail Investor? 95 Va. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1105 (2009), which makes it more difficult both to monitor and to alter particular holdings. Studies show that a majority of individual investors make no trades at all during a given year. Id., at 1117. Moreover, if the corporation in question operates a PAC, an investor who sees the company\xe2\x80\x99s ads may not know whether they are being funded through the PAC or through the general treasury.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0If and when shareholders learn that a corporation has been spending general treasury money on objectionable electioneering, they can divest. Even assuming that they reliably learn as much, however, this solution is only partial. The injury to the shareholders\xe2\x80\x99 expressive rights has already occurred; they might have preferred to keep that corporation\xe2\x80\x99s stock in their portfolio for any number of economic reasons; and they may incur a capital gains tax or other penalty from selling their shares, changing their pension plan, or the like. The shareholder protection rationale has been criticized as underinclusive, in that corporations also spend money on lobbying and charitable contributions in ways that any particular shareholder might disapprove. But those expenditures do not implicate the selection of public officials, an area in which \xe2\x80\x9cthe interests of unwilling \xe2\x80\xa6 corporate shareholders [in not being] forced to subsidize that speech\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9care at their zenith.\xe2\x80\x9d Austin, 494 U. S., at 677 (Brennan, J., concurring). And in any event, the question is whether shareholder protection provides a basis for regulating expenditures in the weeks before an election, not whether additional types of corporate communications might similarly be conditioned on voluntariness.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Recognizing the limits of the shareholder protection rationale, the Austin Court did not hold it out as an adequate and independent ground for sustaining the statute in question. Rather, the Court applied it to reinforce the antidistortion rationale, in two main ways. First, the problem of dissenting shareholders shows that even if electioneering expenditures can advance the political views of some members of a corporation, they will often compromise the views of others. See, e.g., id., at 663 (discussing risk that corporation\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cmembers may be \xe2\x80\xa6 reluctant to withdraw as members even if they disagree with [its] political expression\xe2\x80\x9d). Second, it provides an additional reason, beyond the distinctive legal attributes of the corporate form, for doubting that these \xe2\x80\x9cexpenditures reflect actual public support for the political ideas espoused,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 660. The shareholder protection rationale, in other words, bolsters the conclusion that restrictions on corporate electioneering can serve both speakers\xe2\x80\x99 and listeners\xe2\x80\x99 interests, as well as the anticorruption interest. And it supplies yet another reason why corporate expenditures merit less protection than individual expenditures.

\n

V

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Today\xe2\x80\x99s decision is backwards in many senses. It elevates the majority\xe2\x80\x99s agenda over the litigants\xe2\x80\x99 submissions, facial attacks over as-applied claims, broad constitutional theories over narrow statutory grounds, individual dissenting opinions over precedential holdings, assertion over tradition, absolutism over empiricism, rhetoric over reality. Our colleagues have arrived at the conclusion that Austin must be overruled and that \xc2\xa7203 is facially unconstitutional only after mischaracterizing both the reach and rationale of those authorities, and after bypassing or ignoring rules of judicial restraint used to cabin the Court\xe2\x80\x99s lawmaking power. Their conclusion that the societal interest in avoiding corruption and the appearance of corruption does not provide an adequate justification for regulating corporate expenditures on candidate elections relies on an incorrect description of that interest, along with a failure to acknowledge the relevance of established facts and the considered judgments of state and federal legislatures over many decades.

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0 In a democratic society, the longstanding consensus on the need to limit corporate campaign spending should outweigh the wooden application of judge-made rules. The majority\xe2\x80\x99s rejection of this principle \xe2\x80\x9celevate[s] corporations to a level of deference which has not been seen at least since the days when substantive due process was regularly used to invalidate regulatory legislation thought to unfairly impinge upon established economic interests.\xe2\x80\x9d Bellotti, 435 U. S., at 817, n. 13 (White, J., dissenting). At bottom, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion is thus a rejection of the common sense of the American people, who have recognized a need to prevent corporations from undermining self-government since the founding, and who have fought against the distinctive corrupting potential of corporate electioneering since the days of Theodore Roosevelt. It is a strange time to repudiate that common sense. While American democracy is imperfect, few outside the majority of this Court would have thought its flaws included a dearth of corporate money in politics.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I would affirm the judgment of the District Court.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0Specifically, Part I, infra, at 4\xe2\x80\x9317, addresses the procedural history of the case and the narrower grounds of decision the majority has bypassed. Part II, infra, at 17\xe2\x80\x9323, addresses stare decisis. Part III, infra, at 23\xe2\x80\x9356, addresses the Court\xe2\x80\x99s assumptions that BCRA \xe2\x80\x9cbans\xe2\x80\x9d corporate speech, that identity-based distinctions may not be drawn in the political realm, and that Austin and McConnell were outliers in our First Amendment tradition. Part IV, infra, at 56\xe2\x80\x9389, addresses the Court\xe2\x80\x99s treatment of the anticorruption, antidistortion, and shareholder protection rationales for regulating corporate electioneering.

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0See Yee v. Escondido, 503 U. S. 519, 535 (1992) (\xe2\x80\x9c[U]nder this Court\xe2\x80\x99s Rule 14.1(a), only questions set forth in the petition, or fairly included therein, will be considered by the Court\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks and alteration omitted)); Wood v. Allen, ante, at __ (slip op., at 13) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he fact that petitioner discussed [an] issue in the text of his petition for certiorari does not bring it before us. Rule 14.1(a) requires that a subsidiary question be fairly included in the question presented for our review\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks and brackets omitted)); Cooper Industries, Inc. v. Aviall Services, Inc., 543 U. S. 157, 168\xe2\x80\x93169 (2004) (\xe2\x80\x9cWe ordinarily do not decide in the first instance issues not decided below\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)).

\nFootnote 3

\xc2\xa0The majority states that, in denying Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s motion for a preliminary injunction, the District Court \xe2\x80\x9caddressed\xe2\x80\x9d the facial validity of BCRA \xc2\xa7203. Ante, at 13. That is true, in the narrow sense that the court observed the issue was foreclosed by McConnell v. FEC, 540 U. S. 93 (2003). See 530 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 274, 278 (DC 2008) (per curiam). Yet as explained above, Citizens United subsequently dismissed its facial challenge, so that by the time the District Court granted the Federal Election Commission\xe2\x80\x99s (FEC) motion for summary judgment, App. 261a\xe2\x80\x93262a, any question about statutory validity had dropped out of the case. That latter ruling by the District Court was the \xe2\x80\x9cfinal decision\xe2\x80\x9d from which Citizens United appealed to this Court under BCRA \xc2\xa7403(a)(3). As regards the lower court decision that has come before us, the claim that \xc2\xa7203 is facially unconstitutional was neither pressed nor passed upon in any form.

\nFootnote 4

\xc2\xa0Shortly before Citizens United mooted the issue by abandoning its facial challenge, the Government advised the District Court that it \xe2\x80\x9crequire[d] time to develop a factual record regarding [the] facial challenge.\xe2\x80\x9d 1:07\xe2\x80\x93cv\xe2\x80\x932240\xe2\x80\x93RCL\xe2\x80\x93RWR, Docket Entry No. 47, p. 4 (Mar. 26, 2008). By reinstating a claim that Citizens United abandoned, the Court gives it a perverse litigating advantage over its adversary, which was deprived of the opportunity to gather and present information necessary to its rebuttal.

\nFootnote 5

\xc2\xa0In fact, we do not even have a good evidentiary record of how \xc2\xa7203 has been affecting Citizens United, which never submitted to the District Court the details of Hillary\xe2\x80\x99s funding or its own finances. We likewise have no evidence of how \xc2\xa7203 and comparable state laws were expected to affect corporations and unions in the future.

\n

\n\nIt is true, as the majority points out, that the McConnell Court evaluated the facial validity of \xc2\xa7203 in light of an extensive record. See ante, at 15. But that record is not before us in this case. And in any event, the majority\xe2\x80\x99s argument for striking down \xc2\xa7203 depends on its contention that the statute has proved too \xe2\x80\x9cchilling\xe2\x80\x9d in practice\xe2\x80\x94and in particular on the contention that the controlling opinion in WRTL, 551 U. S. 449 (2007), failed to bring sufficient clarity and \xe2\x80\x9cbreathing space\xe2\x80\x9d to this area of law. See ante, at 12, 16\xe2\x80\x9320. We have no record with which to assess that claim. The Court complains at length about the burdens of complying with \xc2\xa7203, but we have no meaningful evidence to show how regulated corporations and unions have experienced its restrictions.

\nFootnote 6

\xc2\xa0Our cases recognize a \xe2\x80\x9ctype of facial challenge in the First Amendment context under which a law may be overturned as impermissibly overbroad because a substantial number of its applications are unconstitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d Washington State Grange v. Washington State Republican Party, 552 U. S. 442, 449, n.\xc2\xa06 (2008) (internal quotation marks omitted). Citizens United has not made an overbreadth argument, and \xe2\x80\x9c[w]e generally do not apply the strong medicine of overbreadth analysis where the parties fail to describe the instances of arguable overbreadth of the contested law,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. (internal quotation marks omitted). If our colleagues nonetheless concluded that \xc2\xa7203\xe2\x80\x99s fatal flaw is that it affects too much protected speech, they should have invalidated it for overbreadth and given guidance as to which applications are permissible, so that Congress could go about repairing the error.

\nFootnote 7

\xc2\xa0Also perplexing is the majority\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to pass blame to the Government for its litigating position. By \xe2\x80\x9chold[ing] out the possibility of ruling for Citizens United on a narrow ground yet refrain[ing] from adopting that position,\xe2\x80\x9d the majority says, the Government has caused \xe2\x80\x9cadded uncertainty [that] demonstrates the necessity to address the question of statutory validity.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 17. Our colleagues have apparently never heard of an alternative argument. Like every litigant, the Government would prefer to win its case outright; failing that, it would prefer to lose on a narrow ground. The fact that there are numerous different ways this case could be decided, and that the Government acknowledges as much, does not demonstrate anything about the propriety of a facial ruling.

\nFootnote 8

\xc2\xa0The majority\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cchilling\xe2\x80\x9d argument is particularly inapposite with respect to 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s longstanding restriction on the use of corporate general treasury funds for express advocacy. If there was ever any significant uncertainty about what counts as the functional equivalent of express advocacy, there has been little doubt about what counts as express advocacy since the \xe2\x80\x9cmagic words\xe2\x80\x9d test of Buckley v. Valeo, 424 U. S. 1, 44, n.\xc2\xa052 (1976) (per curiam). Yet even though Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s briefs never once mention \xc2\xa7441b\xe2\x80\x99s restriction on express advocacy; even though this restriction does not generate chilling concerns; and even though no one has suggested that Hillary counts as express advocacy; the majority nonetheless reaches out to opine that this statutory provision is \xe2\x80\x9cinvalid\xe2\x80\x9d as well. Ante, at 50.

\nFootnote 9

\xc2\xa0The majority adds that the distinction between facial and as-applied challenges does not have \xe2\x80\x9csome automatic effect\xe2\x80\x9d that mechanically controls the judicial task. Ante, at 14. I agree, but it does not follow that in any given case we should ignore the distinction, much less invert it.

\nFootnote 10

\xc2\xa0Professor Fallon proposes an intricate answer to this question that the majority ignores. Fallon 1327\xe2\x80\x931359. It bears mention that our colleagues have previously cited Professor Fallon\xe2\x80\x99s article for the exact opposite point from the one they wish to make today. In Gonzales v. Carhart, 550 U. S. 124 (2007), the Court explained that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]t is neither our obligation nor within our traditional institutional role to resolve questions of constitutionality with respect to each potential situation that might develop,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c[f]or this reason, \xe2\x80\x98[a]s-applied challenges are the basic building blocks of constitutional adjudication.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 168 (opinion for the Court by Kennedy, J.) (quoting Fallon 1328 (second alteration in original)).

\nFootnote 11

\xc2\xa0Internal Revenue Code section 501(c)(4) applies, inter alia, to nonprofit organizations \xe2\x80\x9coperated exclusively for the promotion of social welfare, \xe2\x80\xa6 the net earnings of which are devoted exclusively to charitable, educational, or recreational purposes.\xe2\x80\x9d

\nFootnote 12

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice is therefore much too quick when he suggests that, \xe2\x80\x9c[e]ven if considered in as-applied terms, a holding in this case that the Act may not be applied to Citizens United\xe2\x80\x94because corporations as well as individuals enjoy the pertinent First Amendment rights\xe2\x80\x94would mean that any other corporation raising the same challenge would also win.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 4 (concurring opinion). That conclusion would only follow if the Court were to ignore Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s plausible as-applied arguments and instead take the implausible position that all corporations and all types of expenditures enjoy the same First Amendment protections, which always trump the interests in regulation. At times, the majority appears to endorse this extreme view. At other times, however, it appears to suggest that nonprofit corporations have a better claim to First Amendment protection than for-profit corporations, see ante, at 20, 39, \xe2\x80\x9cadvocacy\xe2\x80\x9d organizations have a better claim than other nonprofits, ante, at 20, domestic corporations have a better claim than foreign corporations, ante, at 46\xe2\x80\x9347, small corporations have a better claim than large corporations, ante, at 38\xe2\x80\x9340, and printed matter has a better claim than broadcast communications, ante, at 33. The majority never uses a multinational business corporation in its hypotheticals.

\xc2\xa0The Court entirely ignores this statutory argument. It concludes that \xc2\xa7203 applies to Hillary on the basis of the film\xe2\x80\x99s content, ante, at 7\xe2\x80\x938, without considering the possibility that \xc2\xa7203 does not apply to video-on-demand transmissions generally.

\nFootnote 14

\xc2\xa0See Colorado Right to Life Comm., Inc. v. Coffman, 498 F.\xc2\xa03d 1137, 1148 (CA10 2007) (adopting this rule and noting that \xe2\x80\x9cevery other circuit to have addressed this issue\xe2\x80\x9d has done likewise); Brief for Independent Sector as Amicus Curiae 10\xe2\x80\x9311 (collecting cases). The Court rejects this solution in part because the Government \xe2\x80\x9cmerely suggest[s] it\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not say that it agrees with the interpretation.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 11. Our colleagues would thus punish a defendant for showing insufficient excitement about a ground it has advanced, at the same time that they decide the case on a ground the plaintiff expressly abandoned. The Court also protests that a de minimis standard would \xe2\x80\x9crequir[e] intricate case-by-case determinations.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 12. But de minimis tests need not be intricate at all. A test that granted MCFL status to \xc2\xa7501(c)(4) organizations if they received less than a fixed dollar amount of business donations in the previous year, or if such donations represent less than a fixed percentage of their total assets, would be perfectly easy to understand and administer.

\xc2\xa0Another bypassed ground, not briefed by the parties, would have been to revive the Snowe-Jeffords Amendment in BCRA \xc2\xa7203(c), allowing certain nonprofit corporations to pay for electioneering communications with general treasury funds, to the extent they can trace the payments to individual contributions. \nFootnote 16

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice finds our discussion of these narrower solutions \xe2\x80\x9cquite perplexing\xe2\x80\x9d because we suggest that the Court should \xe2\x80\x9clatch on to one of them in order to avoid reaching the broader constitutional question,\xe2\x80\x9d without doing the same ourselves. Ante, at 3. There is nothing perplexing about the matter, because we are not similarly situated to our colleagues in the majority. We do not share their view of the First Amendment. Our reading of the Constitution would not lead us to strike down any statutes or overturn any precedents in this case, and we therefore have no occasion to practice constitutional avoidance or to vindicate Citizens United\xe2\x80\x99s as-applied challenge. Each of the arguments made above is surely at least as strong as the statutory argument the Court accepted in last year\xe2\x80\x99s Voting Rights Act case, Northwest Austin Municipal Util. Dist. No. One v. Holder, 557 U. S. __ (2009).

\xc2\xa0I will have more to say shortly about the merits\xe2\x80\x94about why Austin and McConnell are not doctrinal outliers, as the Court contends, and why their logic is not only defensible but also compelling. \nFootnote 18

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice suggests that Austin has been undermined by subsequent dissenting opinions. Ante, at 9. Under this view, it appears that the more times the Court stands by a precedent in the face of requests to overrule it, the weaker that precedent becomes. The Chief Justice further suggests that Austin \xe2\x80\x9cis uniquely destabilizing because it threatens to subvert our Court\xe2\x80\x99s decisions even outside\xe2\x80\x9d its particular facts, as when we applied its reasoning in McConnell. Ante, at 9. Once again, the theory seems to be that the more we utilize a precedent, the more we call it into question. For those who believe Austin was correctly decided\xe2\x80\x94as the Federal Government and the States have long believed, as the majority of Justices to have served on the Court since Austin have believed, and as we continue to believe\xe2\x80\x94there is nothing \xe2\x80\x9cdestabilizing\xe2\x80\x9d about the prospect of its continued application. It is gutting campaign finance laws across the country, as the Court does today, that will be destabilizing.

\nFootnote 19

\xc2\xa0Additionally, the majority cites some recent scholarship challenging the historical account of campaign finance law given in United States v. Automobile Workers, 352 U. S. 567 (1957). Ante, at 48. Austin did not so much as allude to this historical account, much less rely on it. Even if the scholarship cited by the majority is correct that certain campaign finance reforms were less deliberate or less benignly motivated than Automobile Workers suggested, the point remains that this body of law has played a significant and broadly accepted role in American political life for decades upon decades.

\xc2\xa0Concedely, Austin and McConnell were constitutional decisions, and we have often said that \xe2\x80\x9cclaims of stare decisis are at the weakest in that field, where our mistakes cannot be corrected by Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Vieth v. Jubelirer, 541 U. S. 267, 305 (2004) (plurality opinion). As a general matter, this principle is a sound one. \nFootnote 51

\xc2\xa0The Court all but confesses that a categorical approach to speaker identity is untenable when it acknowledges that Congress might be allowed to take measures aimed at \xe2\x80\x9cpreventing foreign individuals or associations from influencing our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s political process.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 46\xe2\x80\x9347. \xc2\xa0See A. Bickel, The Supreme Court and the Idea of Progress 59\xe2\x80\x9360 (1978); A. Meiklejohn, Political Freedom: The Constitutional Powers of the People 39\xe2\x80\x9340 (1965); Tokaji, First Amendment Equal Protection: On Discretion, Inequality, and Participation, 101 Mich. L. Rev. 2409, 2508\xe2\x80\x932509 (2003). Of course, voting is not speech in a pure or formal sense, but then again neither is a campaign expenditure; both are nevertheless communicative acts aimed at influencing electoral outcomes. Cf. Strauss, Corruption, Equality, and Campaign Finance Reform, 94 Colum. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1369, 1383\xe2\x80\x931384 (1994) (hereinafter Strauss).

\nFootnote 53

\xc2\xa0Scholars have found that only a handful of business corporations were issued charters during the colonial period, and only a few hundred during all of the 18th century. See E. Dodd, American Business Corporations Until 1860, p.\xc2\xa0197 (1954); L. Friedman, A History of American Law 188\xe2\x80\x93189 (2d ed. 1985); Baldwin, American Business Corporations Before 1789, 8 Am. Hist. Rev. 449, 450\xe2\x80\x93459 (1903). Justice Scalia quibbles with these figures; whereas we say that \xe2\x80\x9ca few hundred\xe2\x80\x9d charters were issued to business corporations during the 18th century, he says that the number is \xe2\x80\x9capproximately 335.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 2 (concurring opinion). Justice Scalia also raises the more serious point that it is improper to assess these figures by today\xe2\x80\x99s standards, ante, at 3, though I believe he fails to substantiate his claim that \xe2\x80\x9cthe corporation was a familiar figure in American economic life\xe2\x80\x9d by the century\xe2\x80\x99s end, ibid. (internal quotation marks omitted). His formulation of that claim is also misleading, because the relevant reference point is not 1800 but the date of the First Amendment\xe2\x80\x99s ratification, in 1791. And at that time, the number of business charters must have been significantly smaller than 335, because the pace of chartering only began to pick up steam in the last decade of the 18th century. More than half of the century\xe2\x80\x99s total business charters were issued between 1796 and 1800. Friedman, History of American Law, at 189.

\nFootnote 54

\xc2\xa0See Letter from Thomas Jefferson to Tom Logan (Nov. 12, 1816), in 12 The Works of Thomas Jefferson 42, 44 (P. Ford ed. 1905) (\xe2\x80\x9cI hope we shall \xe2\x80\xa6 crush in [its] birth the aristocracy of our monied corporations which dare already to challenge our government to a trial of strength and bid defiance to the laws of our country\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 55

\xc2\xa0In normal usage then, as now, the term \xe2\x80\x9cspeech\xe2\x80\x9d referred to oral communications by individuals. See, e.g., 2 S. Johnson, Dictionary of the English Language 1853\xe2\x80\x931854 (4th ed. 1773) (reprinted 1978) (listing as primary definition of \xe2\x80\x9cspeech\xe2\x80\x9d: \xe2\x80\x9cThe power of articulate utterance; the power of expressing thoughts by vocal words\xe2\x80\x9d); 2 N. Webster, American Dictionary of the English Language (1828) (reprinted 1970) (listing as primary definition of \xe2\x80\x9cspeech\xe2\x80\x9d: \xe2\x80\x9cThe faculty of uttering articulate sounds or words, as in human beings; the faculty of expressing thoughts by words or articulate sounds. Speech was given to man by his Creator for the noblest purposes\xe2\x80\x9d). Indeed, it has been \xe2\x80\x9cclaimed that the notion of institutional speech \xe2\x80\xa6 did not exist in post-revolutionary America.\xe2\x80\x9d Fagundes, State Actors as First Amendment Speakers, 100 Nw. U. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1637, 1654 (2006); see also Bezanson, Institutional Speech, 80 Iowa L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 735, 775 (1995) (\xe2\x80\x9cIn the intellectual heritage of the eighteenth century, the idea that free speech was individual and personal was deeply rooted and clearly manifest in the writings of Locke, Milton, and others on whom the framers of the Constitution and the Bill of Rights drew\xe2\x80\x9d). Given that corporations were conceived of as artificial entities and do not have the technical capacity to \xe2\x80\x9cspeak,\xe2\x80\x9d the burden of establishing that the Framers and ratifiers understood \xe2\x80\x9cthe freedom of speech\xe2\x80\x9d to encompass corporate speech is, I believe, far heavier than the majority acknowledges.

\nFootnote 56

\xc2\xa0Postratification practice bolsters the conclusion that the First Amendment, \xe2\x80\x9cas originally understood,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 37, did not give corporations political speech rights on a par with the rights of individuals. Well into the modern era of general incorporation statutes, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he common law was generally interpreted as prohibiting corporate political participation,\xe2\x80\x9d First Nat. Bank of Boston v. Bellotti, 435 U. S. 765, 819 (1978) (White, J., dissenting), and this Court did not recognize any First Amendment protections for corporations until the middle part of the 20th century, see ante, at 25\xe2\x80\x9326 (listing cases).

\nFootnote 57

\xc2\xa0In fact, the Free Press Clause might be turned against Justice Scalia, for two reasons. First, we learn from it that the drafters of the First Amendment did draw distinctions\xe2\x80\x94explicit distinctions\xe2\x80\x94between types of \xe2\x80\x9cspeakers,\xe2\x80\x9d or speech outlets or forms. Second, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s strongest historical evidence all relates to the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 views on the press, see ante, at 37\xe2\x80\x9338; ante, at 4\xe2\x80\x936 (Scalia, J., concurring), yet while the Court tries to sweep this evidence into the Free Speech Clause, the Free Press Clause provides a more natural textual home. The text and history highlighted by our colleagues suggests why one type of corporation, those that are part of the press, might be able to claim special First Amendment status, and therefore why some kinds of \xe2\x80\x9cidentity\xe2\x80\x9d-based distinctions might be permissible after all. Once one accepts that much, the intellectual edifice of the majority opinion crumbles.

\nFootnote 58

\xc2\xa0Cf. L. Levy, Legacy of Suppression: Freedom of Speech and Press in Early American History 4 (1960) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe meaning of no other clause of the Bill of Rights at the time of its framing and ratification has been so obscure to us\xe2\x80\x9d as the Free Speech and Press Clause).

\nFootnote 59

\xc2\xa0As the majority notes, there is some academic debate about the precise origins of these developments. Ante, at 48; see also n.\xc2\xa019, supra. There is always some academic debate about such developments; the motives of legislatures are never entirely clear or unitary. Yet the basic shape and trajectory of 20th-century campaign finance reform are clear, and one need not take a nave or triumphalist view of this history to find it highly relevant. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s skepticism does nothing to mitigate the absurdity of its claim that Austin and McConnell were outliers. Nor does it alter the fact that five Justices today destroy a longstanding American practice.

\nFootnote 60

\xc2\xa0See Pipefitters v. United States, 407 U. S. 385, 409, 414\xe2\x80\x93415 (1972) (reading the statutory bar on corporate and union campaign spending not to apply to \xe2\x80\x9cthe voluntary donations of employees,\xe2\x80\x9d when maintained in a separate account, because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he dominant [legislative] concern in requiring that contributions be voluntary was, after all, to protect the dissenting stockholder or union member\xe2\x80\x9d); Automobile Workers, 352 U. S., at 592 (advising the District Court to consider on remand whether the broadcast in question was \xe2\x80\x9cpaid for out of the general dues of the union membership or [whether] the funds [could] be fairly said to have obtained on a voluntary basis\xe2\x80\x9d); United States v. CIO, 335 U. S. 106, 123 (1948) (observing that \xe2\x80\x9cfunds voluntarily contributed [by union members or corporate stockholders] for election purposes\xe2\x80\x9d might not be covered by the expenditure bar). Both the Pipefitters and the Automobile Workers Court approvingly referenced Congress\xe2\x80\x99 goal of reducing \xe2\x80\x9cthe effect of aggregated wealth on federal elections,\xe2\x80\x9d understood as wealth drawn from a corporate or union general treasury without the stockholders\xe2\x80\x99 or members\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cfree and knowing choice.\xe2\x80\x9d Pipefitters, 407 U. \n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The two dissenters in Pipefitters would not have read the statutory provision in question, a successor to \xc2\xa7304 of the Taft-Hartley Act, to allow such robust use of corporate and union funds to finance otherwise prohibited electioneering. \xe2\x80\x9cThis opening of the door to extensive corporate and union influence on the elective and legislative processes,\xe2\x80\x9d Justice Powell wrote, \xe2\x80\x9cmust be viewed with genuine concern. \xc2\xa0Specifically, these corporations had to meet three conditions. \nFootnote 62

\xc2\xa0According to The Chief Justice, we are \xe2\x80\x9cerroneou[s]\xe2\x80\x9d in claiming that McConnell and Beaumont \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98reaffirmed\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Austin. Beaumont, O.\xc2\xa0T. 2002, No. 02\xe2\x80\x93403, passim. If this does not qualify as reaffirmation of a precedent, then I do not know what would.

\nFootnote 63

\xc2\xa0Cf. Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. Though discrete in scope, these experiments must impose some meaningful limits if they are to have a chance at functioning effectively and preserving the public\xe2\x80\x99s trust. \xe2\x80\x9cEven if it occurs only occasionally, the potential for such undue influence is manifest. \nFootnote 64

The electorate itself has consistently indicated otherwise, both in opinion polls, see McConnell v. FEC, 251 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 176, 557\xe2\x80\x93558, 623\xe2\x80\x93624 (DC 2003) (opinion of Kollar-Kotelly, J.), and in the laws its representatives have passed, and our colleagues have no basis for elevating their own optimism into a tenet of constitutional law.

\nFootnote 65

\xc2\xa0Quite distinct from the interest in preventing improper influences on the electoral process, I have long believed that \xe2\x80\x9ca number of [other] purposes, both legitimate and substantial, may justify the imposition of reasonable limitations on the expenditures permitted during the course of any single campaign.\xe2\x80\x9d Davis v. FEC, 554 U. S. ___, ___ (2008) (slip op., at 3) (opinion concurring in part and dissenting in part). In my judgment, such limitations may be justified to the extent they are tailored to \xe2\x80\x9cimproving the quality of the exposition of ideas\xe2\x80\x9d that voters receive, ibid., \xe2\x80\x9cfree[ing] candidates and their staffs from the interminable burden of fundraising,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid. I continue to adhere to these beliefs, but they have not been briefed by the parties or amici in this case, and their soundness is immaterial to its proper disposition.

\nFootnote 66

\xc2\xa0In fact, the notion that the \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d covered by \xc2\xa7203 can breed quid pro quo corruption or the appearance of such corruption has only become more plausible since we decided McConnell. Recall that The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s controlling opinion in WRTL subsequently limited BCRA\xe2\x80\x99s definition of \xe2\x80\x9celectioneering communications\xe2\x80\x9d to those that are \xe2\x80\x9csusceptible of no reasonable interpretation other than as an appeal to vote for or against a specific candidate.\xe2\x80\x9d 551 U. S., at 470. The upshot was that after WRTL, a corporate or union expenditure could be regulated under \xc2\xa7203 only if everyone would understand it as an endorsement of or attack on a particular candidate for office. It does not take much imagination to perceive why this type of advocacy might be especially apt to look like or amount to a deal or a threat.

\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9cWe must give weight\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cdue deference\xe2\x80\x9d to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts to dispel corruption, the Court states at one point. Ante, at 45. It is unclear to me what these maxims mean, but as applied by the Court they clearly do not entail \xe2\x80\x9cdeference\xe2\x80\x9d in any normal sense of that term.

\nFootnote 69

\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice denies this, ante, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310, citing scholarship that has interpreted Austin to endorse an equality rationale, along with an article by Justice Thurgood Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s former law clerk that states that Marshall, the author of Austin, accepted \xe2\x80\x9cequality of opportunity\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cequalizing access to the political process\xe2\x80\x9d as bases for campaign finance regulation, Garrett, New Voices in Politics: Justice Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s Jurisprudence on Law and Politics, 52 Howard L.\xc2\xa0J. 655, 667\xe2\x80\x93668 (2009) (internal quotation marks omitted). It is fair to say that Austin can bear an egalitarian reading, and I have no reason to doubt this characterization of Justice Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s beliefs. But the fact that Austin can be read a certain way hardly proves The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s charge that there is nothing more to it. Many of our precedents can bear multiple readings, and many of our doctrines have some \xe2\x80\x9cequalizing\xe2\x80\x9d implications but do not rest on an equalizing theory: for example, our takings jurisprudence and numerous rules of criminal procedure. More important, the Austin Court expressly declined to rely on a speech-equalization rationale, see 494 U. S., at 660, and we have never understood Austin to stand for such a rationale. Whatever his personal views, Justice Marshall simply did not write the opinion that The Chief Justice suggests he did; indeed, he \xe2\x80\x9cwould have viewed it as irresponsible to write an opinion that boldly staked out a rationale based on equality that no one other than perhaps Justice White would have even considered joining,\xe2\x80\x9d Garrett, 52 Howard L.\xc2\xa0J., at 674.

\xc2\xa0In state elections, even domestic corporations may be \xe2\x80\x9cforeign\xe2\x80\x9d-controlled in the sense that they are incorporated in another jurisdiction and primarily owned and operated by out-of-state residents.

\nFootnote 71

\xc2\xa0Regan, Corporate Speech and Civic Virtue, in Debating Democracy\xe2\x80\x99s Discontent 289, 302 (A. Allen & M. Regan eds. 1998) (hereinafter Regan).

\nFootnote 72

\xc2\xa0Nothing in this analysis turns on whether the corporation is conceptualized as a grantee of a state concession, see, e.g., Trustees of Dartmouth College v. Woodward, 4 Wheat. 518, 636 (1819) (Marshall, C.\xc2\xa0J.), a nexus of explicit and implicit contracts, see, e.g., F. Easterbrook & D. Fischel, The Economic Structure of Corporate Law 12 (1991), a mediated hierarchy of stakeholders, see, e.g., Blair & Stout, A Team Production Theory of Corporate Law, 85 Va. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 247 (1999) (hereinafter Blair & Stout), or any other recognized model. Austin referred to the structure and the advantages of corporations as \xe2\x80\x9cstate-conferred\xe2\x80\x9d in several places, 494 U. S., at 660, 665, 667, but its antidistortion argument relied only on the basic descriptive features of corporations, as sketched above. It is not necessary to agree on a precise theory of the corporation to agree that corporations differ from natural persons in fundamental ways, and that a legislature might therefore need to regulate them differently if it is human welfare that is the object of its concern. Cf. Hansmann & Kraakman 441, n.\xc2\xa05.

\xc2\xa0Under the majority\xe2\x80\x99s view, the legislature is thus damned if it does and damned if it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t. If the legislature gives media corporations an exemption from electioneering regulations that apply to other corporations, it violates the newly minted First Amendment rule against identity-based distinctions. If the legislature does not give media corporations an exemption, it violates the First Amendment rights of the press. The only way out of this invented bind: no regulations whatsoever.

Legislatures remain free in their incorporation and tax laws to condition the types of activity in which corporations may engage, including electioneering activity, on specific disclosure requirements or on prior express approval by shareholders or members.
