\nSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES
\nNO. 08-205
\n
\n

CITIZENS UNITED, APPELLANT v. FEDERAL\nELECTION COMMISSION

\n

on appeal from the united states district court for the district of columbia

\n

[January 21, 2010]

\n

\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Justice Thomas, concurring in part and dissenting in part.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I join all but Part IV of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Political speech is entitled to robust protection under the First Amendment. Section 203 of the Bipartisan Campaign Reform Act of 2002 (BCRA) has never been reconcilable with that protection. By striking down \xc2\xa7203, the Court takes an important first step toward restoring full constitutional protection to speech that is \xe2\x80\x9cindispensable to the effective and intelligent use of the processes of popular government.\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell v. Federal Election Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 540 U. S. 93, 265 (2003) (Thomas, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part) (internal quotation marks omitted). I dissent from Part IV of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, however, because the Court\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional analysis does not go far enough. The disclosure, disclaimer, and reporting requirements in BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311 are also unconstitutional. See id., at 275\xe2\x80\x93277, and n.\xc2\xa010.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Congress may not abridge the \xe2\x80\x9cright to anonymous speech\xe2\x80\x9d based on the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98simple interest in providing voters with additional relevant information,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 276 (quoting McIntyre v. Ohio Elections Comm\xe2\x80\x99n, 514 U. S. 334, 348 (1995)). In continuing to hold otherwise, the Court misapprehends the import of \xe2\x80\x9crecent events\xe2\x80\x9d that some amici describe \xe2\x80\x9cin which donors to certain causes were blacklisted, threatened, or otherwise targeted for retaliation.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 54. The Court properly recognizes these events as \xe2\x80\x9ccause for concern,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., but fails to acknowledge their constitutional significance. In my view, amici\xe2\x80\x99s submissions show why the Court\xe2\x80\x99s insistence on upholding \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311 will ultimately prove as misguided (and ill fated) as was its prior approval of \xc2\xa7203.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Amici\xe2\x80\x99s examples relate principally to Proposition 8, a state ballot proposition that California voters narrowly passed in the 2008 general election. Proposition 8 amended California\xe2\x80\x99s constitution to provide that \xe2\x80\x9c[o]nly marriage between a man and a woman is valid or recognized in California.\xe2\x80\x9d Cal. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa77.5. Any donor who gave more than $100 to any committee supporting or opposing Proposition 8 was required to disclose his full name, street address, occupation, employer\xe2\x80\x99s name (or business name, if self-employed), and the total amount of his contributions.[Footnote 1] See Cal. Govt. Code Ann. \xc2\xa784211(f) (West 2005). The California Secretary of State was then required to post this information on the Internet. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa784600\xe2\x80\x9384601; \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa784602\xe2\x80\x9384602.1 (West Supp. 2010); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa784602.5\xe2\x80\x9384604 (West 2005); \xc2\xa785605 (West Supp. 2010); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa784606\xe2\x80\x9384609 (West 2005).

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Some opponents of Proposition 8 compiled this information and created Web sites with maps showing the locations of homes or businesses of Proposition 8 supporters. Many supporters (or their customers) suffered property damage, or threats of physical violence or death, as a result. They cited these incidents in a complaint they filed after the 2008 election, seeking to invalidate California\xe2\x80\x99s mandatory disclosure laws. Supporters recounted being told: \xe2\x80\x9cConsider yourself lucky. If I had a gun I would have gunned you down along with each and every other supporter,\xe2\x80\x9d or, \xe2\x80\x9cwe have plans for you and your friends.\xe2\x80\x9d Complaint in ProtectMarriage.com\xe2\x80\x94Yes on 8 v. Bowen, Case No. 2:09\xe2\x80\x93cv\xe2\x80\x9300058\xe2\x80\x93MCE\xe2\x80\x93DAD (ED Cal.), \xc2\xb631. Proposition 8 opponents also allegedly harassed the measure\xe2\x80\x99s supporters by defacing or damaging their property. Id., \xc2\xb632. Two religious organizations supporting Proposition 8 reportedly received through the mail envelopes containing a white powdery substance. Id., \xc2\xb633.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Those accounts are consistent with media reports describing Proposition 8-related retaliation. The director of the nonprofit California Musical Theater gave $1,000 to support the initiative; he was forced to resign after artists complained to his employer. Lott & Smith, Donor Disclosure Has Its Downsides, Wall Street Journal, Dec. 26, 2008, p. A13. The director of the Los Angeles Film Festival was forced to resign after giving $1,500 because opponents threatened to boycott and picket the next festival. Ibid. And a woman who had managed her popular, family-owned restaurant for 26 years was forced to resign after she gave $100, because \xe2\x80\x9cthrongs of [angry] protesters\xe2\x80\x9d repeatedly arrived at the restaurant and \xe2\x80\x9cshout[ed] \xe2\x80\x98shame on you\xe2\x80\x99 at customers.\xe2\x80\x9d Lopez, Prop. 8 Stance Upends Her Life, Los\xc2\xa0Angeles Times, Dec. 14, 2008, p. B1. The police even had to \xe2\x80\x9carriv[e] in riot gear one night to quell the angry mob\xe2\x80\x9d at the restaurant. Ibid. Some supporters of Proposition 8 engaged in similar tactics; one real estate businessman in San Diego who had donated to a group opposing Proposition 8 \xe2\x80\x9creceived a letter from the Prop. 8 Executive Committee threatening to publish his company\xe2\x80\x99s name if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t also donate to the \xe2\x80\x98Yes on 8\xe2\x80\x99 campaign.\xe2\x80\x9d Donor Disclosure, supra, at A13.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The success of such intimidation tactics has apparently spawned a cottage industry that uses forcibly disclosed donor information to pre-empt citizens\xe2\x80\x99 exercise of their First Amendment rights. Before the 2008 Presidential election, a \xe2\x80\x9cnewly formed nonprofit group \xe2\x80\xa6 plann[ed] to confront donors to conservative groups, hoping to create a chilling effect that will dry up contributions.\xe2\x80\x9d Luo, Group Plans Campaign Against G.O.P. Donors, N.\xc2\xa0Y. Times, Aug. 8, 2008, p. A15. Its leader, \xe2\x80\x9cwho described his effort as \xe2\x80\x98going for the jugular,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d detailed the group\xe2\x80\x99s plan to send a \xe2\x80\x9cwarning letter \xe2\x80\xa6 alerting donors who might be considering giving to right-wing groups to a variety of potential dangers, including legal trouble, public exposure and watchdog groups digging through their lives.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0These instances of retaliation sufficiently demonstrate why this Court should invalidate mandatory disclosure and reporting requirements. But amici present evidence of yet another reason to do so\xe2\x80\x94the threat of retaliation from elected officials. As amici\xe2\x80\x99s submissions make clear, this threat extends far beyond a single ballot proposition in California. For example, a candidate challenging an incumbent state attorney general reported that some members of the State\xe2\x80\x99s business community feared donating to his campaign because they did not want to cross the incumbent; in his words, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98I go to so many people and hear the same thing: \xe2\x80\x9cI sure hope you beat [the incumbent], but I can\xe2\x80\x99t afford to have my name on your records. He might come after me next.\xe2\x80\x9d\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Strassel, Challenging Spitzerism at the Polls, Wall Street Journal, Aug. 1, 2008, p. A11. The incumbent won reelection in 2008.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0My point is not to express any view on the merits of the political controversies I describe. Rather, it is to demonstrate\xe2\x80\x94using real-world, recent examples\xe2\x80\x94the fallacy in the Court\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion that \xe2\x80\x9c[d]isclaimer and disclosure requirements \xe2\x80\xa6 impose no ceiling on campaign-related activities, and do not prevent anyone from speaking.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 51 (internal quotation marks and citations omitted). Of course they do. Disclaimer and disclosure requirements enable private citizens and elected officials to implement political strategies specifically calculated to curtail campaign-related activity and prevent the lawful, peaceful exercise of First Amendment rights.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0The Court nevertheless insists that as-applied challenges to disclosure requirements will suffice to vindicate those speech rights, as long as potential plaintiffs can \xe2\x80\x9cshow a reasonable probability that disclosure \xe2\x80\xa6 will subject them to threats, harassment, or reprisals from either Government officials or private parties.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 52 (internal quotation marks omitted). But the Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion itself proves the irony in this compromise. In correctly explaining why it must address the facial constitutionality of \xc2\xa7203, see ante, at 5\xe2\x80\x9320, the Court recognizes that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he First Amendment does not permit laws that force speakers to \xe2\x80\xa6 seek declaratory rulings before discussing the most salient political issues of our day,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 7; that as-applied challenges to \xc2\xa7203 \xe2\x80\x9cwould require substantial litigation over an extended time\xe2\x80\x9d and result in an \xe2\x80\x9cinterpretive process [that] itself would create an inevitable, pervasive, and serious risk of chilling protected speech pending the drawing of fine distinctions that, in the end, would themselves be questionable,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310; that \xe2\x80\x9ca court would be remiss in performing its duties were it to accept an unsound principle merely to avoid the necessity of making a broader ruling,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 12; and that avoiding a facial challenge to \xc2\xa7203 \xe2\x80\x9cwould prolong the substantial, nation-wide chilling effect\xe2\x80\x9d that \xc2\xa7203 causes, ante, at 16. This logic, of course, applies equally to as-applied challenges to \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311.

\n

\n\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0Irony aside, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s promise that as-applied challenges will adequately protect speech is a hollow assurance. Now more than ever, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311 will chill protected speech because\xe2\x80\x94as California voters can attest\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthe advent of the Internet\xe2\x80\x9d enables \xe2\x80\x9cprompt disclosure of expenditures,\xe2\x80\x9d which \xe2\x80\x9cprovide[s]\xe2\x80\x9d political opponents \xe2\x80\x9cwith the information needed\xe2\x80\x9d to intimidate and retaliate against their foes. Ante, at 55. Thus, \xe2\x80\x9cdisclosure permits citizens \xe2\x80\xa6 to react to the speech of [their political opponents] in a proper\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94or undeniably improper\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cway\xe2\x80\x9d long before a plaintiff could prevail on an as-applied challenge.[Footnote 2] Ibid.

\n

\n\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0I cannot endorse a view of the First Amendment that subjects citizens of this Nation to death threats, ruined careers, damaged or defaced property, or pre-emptive and threatening warning letters as the price for engaging in \xe2\x80\x9ccore political speech, the \xe2\x80\x98primary object of First Amendment protection.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d McConnell, 540 U. S., at 264 (Thomas, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part) (quoting Nixon v. Shrink Missouri Government PAC, 528 U. S. 377, 410\xe2\x80\x93411 (2000) (Thomas, J., dissenting)). Accordingly, I respectfully dissent from the Court\xe2\x80\x99s judgment upholding BCRA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7201 and 311.

\nFootnote 1

\xc2\xa0BCRA imposes similar disclosure requirements. See, e.g., 2 U. S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7434(f)(2)(F) (\xe2\x80\x9cEvery person who makes a disbursement for the direct costs of producing and airing electioneering communications in an aggregate amount in excess of $10,000 during any calendar year\xe2\x80\x9d must disclose \xe2\x80\x9cthe names and addresses of all contributors who contributed an aggregate amount of $1,000 or more to the person making the disbursement\xe2\x80\x9d).

\nFootnote 2

\xc2\xa0But cf. Hill v. Colorado, 530 U. S. 703, 707\xe2\x80\x93710 (2000) (approving a statute restricting speech \xe2\x80\x9cwithin 100 feet\xe2\x80\x9d of abortion clinics because it protected women seeking an abortion from \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98sidewalk counseling,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d which \xe2\x80\x9cconsists of efforts \xe2\x80\x98to educate, counsel, persuade, or inform passersby about abortion and abortion alternatives by means of verbal or written speech,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and which \xe2\x80\x9csometimes\xe2\x80\x9d involved \xe2\x80\x9cstrong and abusive language in face-to-face encounters\xe2\x80\x9d).
